THE RED PAGE.
—From “ Landlopers."
JLE GAY BRERETON, of Sydney, is
known as a writer of verses occasionally
• interesting, rarely stimulating, collected
in several pamphlets around several girls.
His new book, “ Landlopers,” is a book of prose
ramble-notes and reflections, with interpolated
verses ; and is uncommonly well printed by the
publishers, Wm. Brooks & Co. It is adorned by
Some of many small illustrations unacknowledged
by the artist; but a “D.H.S.” on page 39 may
justify one in attributing them to that liberal
and dexterous workman, D. H. Souter, by whose
courtesy some examples are printed on this page.
“ Landlopers ” strikes a more decisive note than
any,of Brereton’s booklets of verses. It records the
physical and mental ins-and-outs of a little tramp
from Sydney along the western road to Jenolan,
and round by the South Coast back to Sydney;
and exhibits the author’s personality in a manly
and attractive fashion. He begins by telling his
nostalgia for the primal life, for space and air and
sunlight—
The hideous sameness of things ! The misery of clock-
work regularity ! This limited existence galls me ; and
the enforced study of Old English poetry increases my
dissatisfaction. Freedom and action nerve every line of
Anglo-Saxon verse. To start reading the ancient songs
is to plunge into the swirl of foaming sea-waves and to
fee! the battle frenzy. If lam to stay here, I must read
something soothing and enervating—conservative and
Tennysonian. But only one thing can bring me peace
now. The old longing is upon me—the longing for the
open road and its lazy labour. I want to burst these
prison-walls of home and custom ; to meet my mates of
former days on the proper footing, and to mingle with
them unrestrained and unrebuked. My fellow-thanes
await-me. I hear their voices and I must go. I must lie
close to the living earth, encompassed by the stars. My
eyes are dim with longing for the dear blue of hills and
sea and sky. I am eager for a thousand natural plea-
sures ; to dive down into cool water and gaze up through
spaces of dim green or grey, or mark in clear depths the
blending of tints in wave-worn pebbles and aquatic
weeds; to let the branches of trees sweep across my face
and body, causing indescribable thrills of love ; to lie
with my hack against a hill and watch slow melting
clouds, and see birds gliding down the wind ; to sink my
face ih sweet-scented grass, and spread my fingers that I
may feel the caress of the elastic blades ; to rejoice in the
smooth flow of the breeze ; to stand upon the shore and
feel the ceaseless pulsing of the Pacific. lam hungry
for communion with these mates of mine. And then the
freedom from restraint, the fetterless uncertainty of
vagrant life !, And the darling children by the wayside !
children who laugh shyly at the incomprehensible tramp
who asks such foolish questions. He asks them merely
for the sake of hearing childish tones and looking into
childish eyes. . . .
And the tramp can appreciate everything, and can find
enjoyment even in misfortune. He is not stifled by con-
ventional obligations. He wanders at large in a perfect
atmosphere of love. He is on the common level; no
social lies raise him to a pedestal or depress him in a cess-
pit. He feels his fellowship with Nature. An individual,
be is yet merged in the universe. He recognises his
union with the Divine. He is a god. And the people
whom he passes on the road see only a tramp with
fringed breeches and a hat full of holes.
Then —having penned this seductive picture for
sundowners to wonder at—he borrows a boy for
mate, and is off with a swag to a sundown. The
rest is 170 pages of excellent small beer—
with occasional flights towards a dithyramb, and
frequent verses (mostly flat) of which these line*
once here before printed are perhaps the best
Intrinsically Brereton’s matter is not of the
most imperishable. The book is engaging and
valuable because it is a book of himself—it is tense
with his attitude, and alive with his earnestness.
It is good reading, and leaves a good memory.
Prayer in Time of War.
Dedicated to the Archbishop of Canterbury.
HOLY Moloch, Lord and Father! mercy on thy
children still!
Blood we owe thee ; blood we bring thee ; give
us then of blood our fill.
Here thy tale of mangled victims,—help us, teach us how
to kill,
Kill ! kill! kill! kill ! kill! kill 1 kill!
We have fed thy flames, O Moloch! do not, Lord, our
anguish spurn 1
Many captives have we flung thee—fling us captives in
thy turn ;
Give us strength that farm and hamlet may as fragrant
incense burn,
Burn ! burn ! burn 1 burn ! burn ! burn I burn !
God of massacre and carnage, give us carnage in a
flood, —
Murdered fathers, weeping mothers, infants dead in
tender bud.
Let our enemies be scattered, let thy altars swim in
blood,
Blood ! blood ! blood ! blood ! blood! blood ! blood !
To thy dripping shrine, Lord Moloch, 10, we lift hands
dripping, gory ;
Round thy image we have twined it, our ensanguined
island story;
Thine, O Moloch, is the kingdom, and the power, and the
glory,
Glory l glory ! glory ! glory ! glory ! glory ! glory 1
“ Landlopers.”
This book of ours that
sheet by sheet
I gave you as I wrote
it, Sweet,
Afar in timorous days
W'hen yet
My love in words I
dared not set,
At last I bring to you
complete.
It tells of lagging hours
and fleet.
Of two whose blood
with rapid beat
Thrilled each to each
whene’er they
met—
This book of ours.
And dreams there be that still intreat,
In passionate terms of swooning heat.
Your hopes to fly far forth and let
The Future take them in his net.
Dear eyes, glow softly when you meet
This book of ours.
Good friend of mine, you feel with me—
Your blood grows hot with sympathy
With something that I say or do ; ’
Then speak—I want a word from you.
Let not the silence wrap you"round
While you are living over-ground.
They say that earthly years are few;
Then speak—I want a word from you.
Perhaps I pass you in the street,
And when our eyes a moment meet,
I wonder are .you wishing too ;
Then speak—I'want a word from you.
Are you, too, longing for a sign,
Yet fear to stretch a hand for mine?
What other am I writing to?
Then speak—I want a word from you.
Some way our thoughts together run.
Since both lift brow towards the sun
Beneath the self-same vault of blue ;
Then speak—I want a word from you.
A Historian’s Judgment on the
Transvaal War.
THE most crushing condemnation of the
British war for Transvaal gold and terri-
tory—which “we do not seek, we do not
covet,” said Lord Salisbury, yet which
“we ” are unquestionably doing our best to
secure by organised murder and legalised rapine—
comes from the men most honoured as British
thinkers and writers. Herbert Spencer, one of
the world’s greatest philosophers, condemns this
unnecessary, unjustifiable war. Frederic Harrison
and Leonard Courtney, equally entitled by in-
telligence and character to give an opinion,
grieve that the honour of Britain—or what passes
for it—should be trailed in the dust at
the bidding of gold - syndicates and Jingo
politicians. And to these and other like
witnesses is now added James Bryce, whose
standing as economist, jurisconsult, and philoso-
phic historian is of the highest. His study of
“ The American Commonwealth ’’—the chief book
on the subject—leaves no doubt as to-his strength
or judgment ; and on a question such as the right
or wrong of the Transvaal war Bryce’s is probably
the weightiest opinion in England to-day.
It is this man, then, who in the preface to the
just-published new edition of his “Impressions
of South Africa ” casts up the balance of evidence
for and against Britain, and decides that Britain
made the war—that the war was unnecessary, that
it was unwise, that it was unjustified. All the
evil which has come upon South Africa Bryce
traces to the Jameson Raid, which was backed by
Cape Premier Rhodes and British Colonial Secre-
tary Chamberlain. The Raid, says Bryce, kindled
“ race feeling all over South Africa, and has had
the most disastrous effect upon every part of the
country.” Before the Raid there was “no hos-
tility at all ” in Cape Colony and Natal between
the British and the Dutch. In the Orange Free
State there was perfect co-operation between both
races. There was also perfect friendliness to
Britain. As to the Transvaal, there was a desire
for reform and a more liberal franchise. In the
Transvaal itself there was a party, growing in in-
fluence, which desired reform, perceived that the
existing state of things could not continue, and
was ready to join the Uitlanders in agitating for
sweeping changes. The Raid changed everything
in all these communities. In Cape Colony Dutch
feeling revived, and a Dutch Ministry came into
power, and politicians were turned into violent
partisans. Nevertheless, the loyalty of the Dutch
was undiminished, for, at the Diamond Jubilee,
the Bond Ministry of Mr. Schreiner voted a grant
of £30.000 per annum to the naval defence of the
Empire.
In the Orange Free State, the explosion of Dutch
sentiment was still stronger. A Dutch President
was elected, the chance of the British candidate
vanishing. Sympathy with the threatened sister
Republic led to a defensive alliance between the
two Republics. As the Free State had no reason
to fear attack, this furnishes a signal proof of the
love of independence which animates it. In the
Transvaal, the progressive burghers were para-
lysed, and the reactionary party was strengthened.
Forts were built, and munitions of war were im-
ported.
Such precaution* were natural. Any Government
which had been nearly overthrown, and expected another
attack, would have done the like.
This of course incensed the Uitlanders and made
the capitalists “angry and restless, because the
prospect of securing reforms which would reduce
the cost of working the gold reefs became more
remote. ”
Mr. Bryce does not deny that the Boers should
have either extended their franchise or reformed
their administration, but he explains that they
could not do the one without the danger of being
swamped by the new burghers, or the other,
because they had pot the necessary knowledge or
skill. On the other hand he maintains that
Britain was bound to observe the treaties made
with the Transvaal. As ito the franchise “it was
a purely internal affair, in which Britain had no
right to intermeddle, either under the Convention
of 1 884 or under the general right of the State to
protect its subjects.” For many reasons, prudent
and conciliatory diplomacy was necessary. The
Boers have regarded British policy
as a policy of violence and duplicity. They recall how
Natal was conquered from them in 1842, after they had
conquered it from the Zulus; how their country was
annexed in 1877 ; how the promises made at the time of
that annexation were broken. . . . Their passion for in-
dependence is something- which we in modern Europe
find it hard to realise. It recalls the long struggle of the
Swiss for freedom in the fourteenth century, or the fierce
tenacity which the Scotch showed in the same age in
their resistance to the claim of England to be their
“ Suzerain Power.’’
Mr. Bryce insists that Britain had no right to
force the Transvaal to enlarge the franchise. A
refusal would not furnish any casus belli. Yet the
franchise negotiations were conducted in such a
way as to lead up to an ultimatum :
There was already a war party in the colony, and
voices clamorous for war were heard in the English Press.
Every check to the negotiations evoked a burst of joy
from organs of opinion at home and in the Cape, whose
articles were unfortunately telegraphed to Pretoria.
Worse still, the cry of “ Avenge Majuba” was frequently
heard in the colonies, and sometimes even in England.
The war was unnecessary—
The alienation of more than half the population of
Cape Colony, the destruction of a peaceful and prosper-
ous Republic with whioh Britain had no quarrel, the re-
sponsibility for governing the Transvaal when conqn*red<
with its old inhabitants bitterly hostile,—these were'evils
■o grave that the benefits to be secured to the Uitlanders •
might well seem small in comparison. A nation is, no
doubt, bound to protect its subjects. But it could Hardly
be said that the hardships of this group of subjects,
which did not prevent others from flocking into the couq*
try, and which were no worse than they had been for
some time previously, were such as to forbid the exercise
of a little mors patience.
As to the Boer Ultimatum, Mr. Bryce says:
It was only in a technical or formal sense that the
Boers can be said to have begun the war; for a weak
State, which sees its enemy approach with a power that
will soon be irresistible, has only two alternatives, to
submit or to attack at once. In such a quarrel the
responsibility does not necessarily rest with those who
strike first. It rests with those whose action has
bloodshed inevitable.
The British Government placed themselves before
the world in a position of having caused a war
without ever formulating a casus belli. “ Strong
Powers dealing with weak ones are prone to think
that any kind of diplomacy will do.”
As to the Pan-Afrikander conspiracy theory,
Mr. Bryce says it seems to be an after-thought
intended to excuse the failure of diplomacy. “‘lt
could not have been helped anyhow.’ ‘lt was
bound to come,’ —phrases such as these are the
last refuge of despairing incompetence.” And no
evidence in support of this theory has been pro-
duced. “There is not,” he says, “a shred of
evidence that the Boers contemplated an attack
on Britain. The needs of defence sufficiently ex-
plain the accumulation of warlike munitions on
whioh so much stress has been laid.” A conflict
between English and Dutch was not inevitable.
It is to be hoped that this authoritative judgment
will silence the. slanders formulated by the
Rhodesian press in Africa, and copied by some
Australian newspapers, to the effect that the
Boers desired war and conspired for war. Before
the Jameson Raid, Pretoria was practically un-
defended. After thp Raid —backed by a British
Minister—the Boers naturally hastened to set
their country in readiness against a second attack
on their liberties.
So far as Britain was concerned, the way to
avert a conflict was to have patience. Haste had
been her bane in South Africa. It was haste
which annexed the Transvaal in 1877. It was
haste which in 1880 wrecked the plan of South
African Confederation. It was haste which
brought about the main source of recent troubles,
the invasion by the South African Company’s
police in 1895. With regard to the future, Mr.
Bryce takes a gloomy view :
Reconcilement and fusion have now, to al! appearances,
been thrown back into a dim and distant future. That
man must be sanguine indeed who expects, as some per-
sons say they do expect, to see the relations of the two
races placed on a better footing by a bitter war between
them, a war which has many of the incidents of a civil
war, and is waged on one side by citizen soldiers. To
most observers it seems more likely to sow a crop of
dragon's teeth, which will produce a harvest, if not of
armed men, yet of permanent hatred and dissatisfaction.
And this is the war in which Australians have
been lured and encouraged to take part !

FROM two, or three sources come hints that
Mrs. Marcus Clarke isn’t prospering in her
pilgrimage to sell Clarke’s minor novels in
England. How could she, hope it?
“Chidipck Tichbourne,” “ ’Twixt Shadow and
Shine, ” and the rest —they are worth nothing.
Clarke always wan ted a spark of reality to fire his
imagination ; he never provided his spark.
(Mnemonic mem. for the people who will write
“Clark”—he ends with an e ; same as “His
Natural Life.”)
The pirate.publisher lives on both sides of the
Atlantic. The second “Dooley” book is (in
America) dedicated to Newnes and Routledge,
who issued unauthorised editions of the first.

Perhaps America is (tardily) beginning to
honour Poe; and a bust of him was recently un-
veiled for the University of Virginia —a perturbed,
anxious, wrinkled bust by Zolnay (whom one
takes on credit as a sculptor of renown).
A now American book, John Sa* tain’s ‘ Re*®* -
lections,” relates that “Annabel Lee” was the
last poem Poe ever wrote. It was bought tor
said Bartain’s Union Magazine, but, before publi-
cation, they found that Annabel had previously
been sold to three other publishers. This pose* as
fresh news ; but it can’t be. Some local writers
assimilated the incident and the moral year* ago.

“ The Pineros were originally Jews from Porta-
gal, who settled in London at the beginning of the
last century. The dramatist’s grandfather, Isaac,
changed the name ‘ Pinheiro ’ to its present form.
Said grandfather married the niece of Captain
Wing, who fought at Trafalgar ; hence Mr.
Pinero’s middle name. . . Of mere literary coinci-
dences in modem times, the most curious was that
of the Mrs. Ebb-Smith who drowned herself be-
cause she thought Mr. Pinero had sketched her U*
his play of almost exactly that name.”
From “Landlopers.”
Read in Charles Kingsley’s “Hereward the
Wake ” an account of a plunder-war of eight cen-
turies ago, when the Normans attacked England
—the veneered, “ religious,” sophisticated race
conquered the simple, pious, “ barbarous ” one.
Compare the Norman shouts “Dex aie! On for
the gold of Ely ! ” with the current British “Dex
aie! On for the gold of Johannesburg!” No
essential difference in eight centuries— blood,
plunder, glory, still the animating motives—and
the same old shibboleths invoked. The more this
humanity changes, the more it is the same thing.
Perhaps the complacent African historian of eight
centuries hence will congratulate his nation on the
benefits of an early infusion of predatory blood.
Would Harold’s English have found slaughter
and slavery “comfortable” words and things in
view of “the benefits of the Norman' infusion ”
and the double-harnessing of “testament ” and co.
with “will” and co. ? How much comfort has
the frog under the harrow in somebody’s theory
that he makes a fine fertiliser ?

It has been suggested in England that a day be
set apart for humiliation and prayer for success
in the Transvaal, war. As the British nation is
on its knees to Moloch, and the Nazarene is tem-
porarily put on the shelf till needed to impress
“ savages ” with the “idealmorality ” of the “Ser-
mon on the Mount ” (when practised by the other
fellow whose country you want to grab), the
“ humiliation” would probably consist in some-
thing akin to the gorilla’s breast-beating when
exciting himself to combat, and the prayer
“preyer ”) might run in the fashion sug-
gested at the head of this page.
JX JX
Admirers of that very great writer, Turgenev,
are commended to a fine notice of his work in
1 The Academy of Nov. 4 —and to the cheap English
translation of his works which forms the basis of
the notice. It is permitted to snatch this frag-
ment of reminiscence, with the note that Turgenev
never married. He said his existence was “satu-
rated with femininity”—
With me, neither books nor anything whatever in the
world could take the place of woman. How can I make
that plain to you? I find it is only love which produces
a certain expansion of the being, that nothing else gives
.. . eh? Listen! When quite a young man X once
had a mistress, a miller’s girl in the neighborhood of St.
Petersburg, whom I used to see when out hunting. She
was charming, very fair, with a flash of the eye rather
■ common among us. She would accept nothing from roe.
But one day she said to me ; “ You must give me a pre-
sent.”
“ What is it you want ?"
“ Bring me some scented soap from St. Petersburg.”
I brought her the soap. She took it, disappeared,
came back blushing, and murmured, offering roe her
hands, delicately scented :
“ Kiss my hands—like you kiss the hands of ladies in
drawing rooms at St. Petersburg.”
I threw myself on my knees—and, you know, that was
the finest moment of my life.
According to Marcel Schwob, classed by Sidney
Colvin (that note of exclamation to Stevenson) as
“the first living authority on Francois Villon,”
the famous “ Testament ’’ is really a violent satire
against the speculators and the administration of
the exchequer in Villon’s time. Colin Laurent,
Girard Gossouyn, and Jehan Mareeau were
greedy usurers and traffickers in salt; and the
passage concerning them is bitter irony.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Charon.
Who goes across those waters
On which the moon ne’er shone,
With the passenger he came for
As in a dream moved on ?
Cypress and yews o’ershadow
The verge on either side,
Within whose boughs for ever
The winds of woe abide.
And all the air is haunted
With a wail that seems to flow
From the living lips of Sorrow
As the ages oome and go.
The boatman, dumb and hoary.
Pulls with a steady pull,
And the dead man seems to listen
To voices beautiful.
And it may be the weird River
Has sights we cannot see,
And the far shore burns its signals
Of eerie mystery.
And Charon knows each signal—
Above the River’s rim
The spectral lights that glimmer
Are pilot-stars for him.
Ay me ! he knows the water
As few, few boatmen know)
*Tis not the first he’s taking
Down where we all must go !
R. CRAWFORD .
Aphrodite.
A pearling lugger ; blue the waves she skims on,
And suddenly a flashing kris, a gasp,
A blaok fin gliding through a splash of orimson—
A foamborn gem clutohed in the cut-throat's
grasp.
A drug-besotted, yellow pirate dreaming,
Curled on the benoh beside a wretched girl;
And lustrous from the squalid litter gleaming
Upon the harlot’s throat a spotless pearl.
An Eastern shim beneath the moonlight cruel,
The grimy back shop of a Jewish fence ;
A blear-eyed jade, and her malignant jewel
That shines demure in milkwhite innocenoe.
O angel face ! O smooth, delusive fashion !
O saintly mask that covers depths unguessed !
O Love that has devoured my heart with passion !
I pin this pearl, an emblem, to your breast.
Q.
VON KOTZE.
The Last Toast.
Fill up your glasses, ye topers !
Fill them fair up to the brim ;
Fill for a toast to our Ultimate Iloab
Waiting us, patient and grim.
Here’s to the Shadow behind us !
Here’s to the Knave we shall know !
Here’s to his wine that is bitter as brine,
And here’s to his lodging below 1
Broad is his bosom and ample—
Wide hath he set us the door;
Go as ye will in his mansion so still,
Yet he hath housing for more;
Pledge him a bumper so hearty,
Give him a song ere we go,
Here’s to his wine that is bitter as brine,
And here’s to his lodging below !
Mark how he kisseth the maiden,
Kisseth her cold and pale ;
See how they come to the call of his drum,
Feeble and hearty and hale ;
Gaunt are the legions he gathers
Down where the cypresses grow ;
Mystical bane,is the wine that they drain
Down in his lodging below !
Lo, He hath harbored our fathers ;
He hath been kind to our kin,
Lapping them all in his passionless pall
Shriving them free of their sin ;
He will be good to our children,
Hushing them tenderly so,
Stilling their moan with a croon of his own,
Down in his lodging below.
Why should we falter and fear him ?
Hath he not healing and rest ?
Doth he not cull us and tenderly lull us ?
Are we not babes of his breast ?
So here’s to the hand of the Reaper,
Here’s to the men he shall mow,
Here’s to the wine that is bitter as brine,
And here’s to the lodging below !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
At the Dance.
The fun is fast, and the music loud,
And the dancers pass in a circling crowd,
And eyes are ablaze and locks are tossed,
And love in a maze of joy is lost.
Then it’s toe and heel, and around we go
While the senses reel to the music’s flow ;
For faults are rife when the spin s too slow;
And the salt of life is its sins, we know.
Then fill the cup with the red, red wine,
And drink it up to the hours that shine,
And breathe the bloom of the red, red rose,
And give joy room while the music flows.
There’s a tomb for tears, and a grave for grief
In the youthful years of a life too brief ;
Then bury them deep while the music’s high—
There’s a time to weep —and a time to die.
V.
BEN SUN.

E, RICH & CO. LTD.
PARTICULARS.
P & R Ruberoid Roofing'
Has for a foundation very strong felt, which is thoroughly
saturated with a permanently soft solution of P & B water and
acid-proof compound. Before finishing a harder coating of
similar composition is applied, which protects the interior and
makes it impossible to dry out or be affected by atmospheric
changes.
P & B Ruberoid Roofing
Is made in four thicknesses or plies—£, 1,2, and 3 (the
heaviest). 2or 3 ply are recommended for permanent roofs,
i or 1 ply being used for buildings of less staple character.
The approximate weights (crated) and prices are as follows:
i ply ( 701b5.) 17/6 per roll, 3ft. wide 72ft. long.
1 ply ( 851b5.) 21/6 „ „
2 ply (1051b5.) 29/- „ „ „
3 ply (1351b5.) 30/- „
Three ply Ruberoid (the heaviest) is about an eighth of an
inch thick.
P & B Ruberoid Roofing
Is strong, durable, light, safe, and waterproof ; water is not
injured by flowing over it and may be used for all domestic
purposes. It is about the colour of new slate. In laying
P & B Ruberoid the edges are lapped two inches, and
Ruberine (Liquid Cement) is applied between with a brush
cold. After fixing with the nails, spaced 2in. between points,
a final coat of Ruberine should be applied over the seam,
which will prevent rusting. We supply (free of charge)
sufficient Ruberine (Cement) to secure the 2in. seams, also
nails and washers for fixing each roll.
P & B Ruberoid Roofing
May be laid. First, over a foundation of close boarding;
Second, over a Galvanised Wire Netting 36in. wide, 3in. mesh,
and 16 guage ; Third, over lin. by 3in. battens spaced 3in.
apart. Laid over Netting being the least expensive method,
and much cheaper than iron. The saving varies (according to
the district supplied and the thickness used) from 6/- to 20/-
per 200 square feet.
Pour Years’ Use
Of this material in Australia has demonstrated that its endur-

SATURDAY, JANUARY 13, 1900.
The “Traitors” at the Cape.
The desperately “loyal” section of the
Australian press —the servile section whose
God is J ohn Bull, and whose idea of loyalty
is to swear that whatever Bull does is right
however glaringly wrong it may be, and to
slander and malign and lie about everyone
with whom Bull has a just or unjust quarrel
—is especially bitter just now against the
Boer sympathisers in British South Africa.
One or two of the wildly-frenzied patriots
and papers who disgrace Australia more than
half suggest even the execution of Presidents
Kruger and Steyn and their principal sup-
porters, if captured. If France or Germany
or Russia were waging war with Britain, and
their papers proposed the hanging of Queen
Victoria on a sour apple tree if the chance
came in their way, doubtless the barbarism
of the idea would fully strike these wild and
drunken fragments of the community. As for
the Boers on the British side of the border who
aid or openly sympathise with their brethren
of the two republics, the loyal press is solid
on the necessity for their prompt execution,
and hold that their offence is beyond all
consideration. They are “traitors, and
“rebels,” also “ungrateful ruffians who
have tasted the blessings of free British
institutions and now turn to bite the hand
of their generous host.” Likewise, they are
many other things, including “murderers,”
“stirrers up of strife” and “dealers in
sedition,” and their crime is so obviously
without possible excuse that defence is use-
less. In fact, to even suggest that there
may be a defence is a proof of disloyalty,
and a matter which calls for inquiry and
possibly other things to follow.
. * * *
To the unreasoning herd which only con-
siders that the Cape is British territory, and
that for the inhabitants of any country’s
territory to sympathise with any foreign
enemy under any circumstances is treason,
this reasoning is satisfactory enough. The
Cape Dutchmen are traitors—technically at
least. The Cape territory belonged formerly
to Holland, but it became permanently
and definitely British about the time of the
great cleaning-up when Napoleon I. was ex-
tinguished, and the Powers proceeded to put
straight again the world-map which he had
so badly disorganised. Many things hap-
pened about that time. It was agreed that
Norway should be violently handed over to
Sweden whether the inhabitants desired it or
not, as a reward for the miserably inefficient
aid lent by King John Bernadotte in
the campaign which ended in Napoleon’s
downfall at Leipsic. It was agreed that
Belgium, despite the bitter complaints of the
Belgian people, should be given to Holland;
that Lombardy and Venice should be put
under the iron heel of Austria ; that Poland
should go back definitely to its old servi-
tude ; that Greece should remain a Turkish
province (in fact this latter point was
hardly even called in question); and
that Naples should again be enslaved
by old, long-bearded Ferdinand. Also
the Pope again became a King in a
small way, without any special regard
for the wishes of the Roman people. And
about this time, and among the general
handing over of small communities to large
ones, Britain became definitely possessed
of the Cape of Good Hope. Having a
more uneasy conscience than the other
annexers, Britain paid cash for the new
territory, and Holland took the money grate-
fully. It had no hope of getting back the
Cape in any case, and it was better to lose it
and get cash for it than to lose it gratis.
But the Dutch population of the Cape wasn’t
consulted, nor was it affected by the cash
aspect of the case. And thereafter, if the
Cape Dutchman rose against Britain, or the
Greek against Turkey, or Garibaldi against
King Bomba, or the Poles against Russia,
or the Belgians against Holland, or the
Spaniards against their priestly king and the
restored Inquisition, or the Italians against
Austria, or the Romans against their holy
Pontiff, they were all equally rebels.
* * * *
Of the communities which were handed
over about this time, and in this off-hand
manner, all save the Norwegians, the Poles,
and the Cape Dutch have since upset the
arrangement by force of arms. And Britain
sympathised with all the rebels—except her
own crowd of rebels. Her claim on the
loyalty of the Cape Dutchmen lies, first of
all, in the fact that she bought them— like so
many cattle. It lies, secondly, in the fact
that she gave them, probably, a better form
of government than was enjoyed by any
other of the transferred races—the Norwe-
gians only excepted. Yet British rule at the
Cape has been no greater an improvement
on that of the Dutch than the Russian rule
inPolandis ontheold Polish anarchy, or than
the yoke of Austria in Venice was on that of
the decayed Venetian oligarchy. If Britain
lifted her new subjects from the ways of the
eighteenth century to those of the nine-
teenth, the other two nations dragged their
unwilling subjects frqm the darkness of the
sixteenth century to the dim light of the
seventeenth or thereabouts. They all ex-
pected gratitude and none of them got it.
Britain, while perfectly able to understand
the situation as regards the other States,
can’t even begin to understand it in her own
case. And probably Austria and Russia
were equally able to understand why the
Cape Dutchmen never became fully recon-
ciled to British rule, and yet couldn’t even
begin to comprehend what right or reason
Poland and Lombardy had to object.
* * ♦ *
Certainly the men of the old Dutch majority
of the Cape Colony never did fully acquiesce
in the policy whereby they were bought by
an alien Power without their consent, and
unless the buying of human beings is lawful
there is no special reason why they should.
They were told, no doubt, that they were
the gainers by the exchange, and that British
rule was vastly better than the old Dutch
domination. Their reply, if these silent
owners of many cattle had put it into words,
w r ould doubtless have been that every nation
says its system of ruling is the best ; but
even if it is, the people have a right to prefer
their own bad government to the stranger’s
good one if they feel so inclined, or else
the alleged rights of man are a fraud, and
the alleged principles of self-government are
a delusion and a hypocrisy. And, if
the rights of the white population only
are considered (the black population, in
considering the disposal of the country, was
left out of court by mutual consent) their
position was perfectly logical. Even the
recent granting of so-called self-governing
powers has no bearing on the real question
at issue. During the many years when the
Cape had no self-governing powers, it was
provided by its new rulers with a vast mass
of well-meant law that was to a great extent
alien to the spirit and ideas of its people.
And then it was granted a limited autonomy ;
in other words the Dutch majority may alter
that foreign law in so far as the veto of its
foreign rulers permits, but it may not call
in question the one fact that it wants
to call in question—the fact that it was
purchased by a strange nation, and handed
over without its consent, and that foreign
governors, and the veto of a foreign sovereign,
and the constant sight of a foreign flag, and
the ever-present bayonets of a foreign nation’s
army of Absent-Minded Beggars, keep it in
constant remembrance of its subjection.
• * * *
To the Australian “loyalist,” with that
melancholy inability to put himself in the
other man’s place which characterises so
many people besides loyalists, the Cape
Dutchman is merely a coarse, brutal traitor
filled with Original Sin. He is a person
who, after being kindly and hospitably
treated, wants to stab his host in the back—
no allowance being made for the fact that
the host first forcibly annexed his guest’s
house, and that, no matter how kindly the
new owner may afterwards permit the old
one to stay in the house, or how cordially he
may tell him to make himself at home there
during good behaviour, that old proprietor
is never likely to feel quite good towards
him. If Britain sold Australia to Russia
without the authority of the people, and
Russia reformed its political methods and
governed the country for many years with
the most benevolent of despotisms, and im-
posed on it good foreign laws and systems
that it didn’t understand, and only hanged
the people when they objected forcibly to
Russian rule—still the strange garrison, and
the strange language, and the strange flag,
and the feeling of subjection to the stranger,
and the degradation of knowing that they
had been sold like so many slaves or cattle,
would probably keep a festering sore open
for centuries. Considering the mutinous
spirit in which Australia objected to the
presence of the British troops, and its
openly expressed joy when the last red-coat
quitted the country, it would be many times
more “ traitorous ” than the Cape under like
conditions. The new rule might be better
than the old one (the Australian has shown
such a thriftless and slovenly incapacity for
self-government that he might easily find
some foreigner who would rule him more
wisely than he has ruled himself) but that
would probably make little difference. And
if the Australian revolted he would be
technically a “traitor”; also he would be
“an ingrate who tried to stab his generous
host in the back ” and all the other expres-
sions of loathing now current but all the
vituperation under Heaven wouldn t make
him feel as if he was any of these things.
He would still call himself a patriot and a
Garibaldi and a Kosciusko,though he can t
look at the other man in that aspect.
* * * *
The Cape Dutchman’s reasons for object-
ing to the easy yoke of Britain are just the
same noble reasons which the Australian
loyalist uses for his own glory and for the
Dutchman’s cursing and degradation. The
Cape “ traitor ” is also devoted to the flag—
the flag which is a symbol that part of his
race is still ruled by men of its own
kind and its own speech. He has also
ideas about the “ crimson thread of kinship.
He believes that “ blood is thicker than
water.” He hears the voice of his brothers
calling just as loudly as the Australian
does. He reckons he has the same right to
join in their quarrel without asking if it be
right or wrong as the Australian has. He
doesn’t hold himself a traitor to the country
that bought his ancestors, because he never
admitted that that pu:chase gave it any
right to his fealty. If he took oaths of
allegiance to Queen Victoria his theory is
that the road to eminence in every walk of
public life was barred to the man who didn t
take that formal oath, and it was therefore
in the nature of' a forced obligation. He is
as noble as Kosciusko, so belauded in Eng-
lish verse. Kosciusko fought to exchange
the bad rule of Russia for the worse rule of
the old Polish noblesse , and to bring back
the old Polish freedom under which nine-
tenths of the Polish people were slaves. If
it is a crime for a misguided patriot to pre-
fer independence and (possibly) bad govern-
ment to foreign subjection and (possibly)
better government, then Britain should
either put the Cape “ rebel ” and the Polish
patriot on the same pedestal—or it should
ban them with the same curse. They were
fighting against very different foreign rulers,
but all things are comparative, and the
Russian autocracy was quite as superior to
the old bloodstained Polish anarchy as British
rule is to the rule of Paul Kruger..
• * * *
It may be for the world’s good in the end
that Kruger should go under and that his
Cape sympathisers should be suppressed.
On the" other hand it may not ; only a
prophet can tell. But at all events the foam
and fury of the wild Australian loyalist be-
cause the British Boer doesn’t love his
purchaser is quite uncalled for. He would
do exactly the same as the Cape and hiatal
“ traitors ” if he was in the same situation ;
and he would be despised by his children,
and his children’s children, and his wild-eyed
bard, and his horse-poet, and his dog-poet,
and his long-haired “ patriot,” and his
orator, and his Dan O’Connor if he didn’t.
The Australian “loyalist,” because he is loyal
to what he regards as his own flag, is furious
at the Cape Dutchman because he isn’t loyal
to a flag that to him only represents foreign
conquest and foreign domination—and
foreign purchase. The Australian never
tried the experience of having to be loyal to
a French or German flag, under penalty of
being reviled as a traitor by papers written
in a language that isn’t his own, and hanged
by a grieved and benevolent foreign Govern-
ment that was deeply shocked at his ingrati-
tude. If he did, he might still fight against
what he calls the Cape “ traitor,” for the de-
sire to go out and kill some complete
stranger is strong in the human soul ; but if
he was any kind of a gentleman, he would
at least revile that “traitor” in a milder key.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted—State Land-Grant Railways.
The years roll on, and Tasmania still waits for
its Great Western Land-Grant Railway. The
syndicate which was to build the line, and own
it, and charge practically what fares and
freights it pleased, and get a present of about
one-sixteenth of the whole province for doing
it, gets no further forward even under these
favorable conditions. It refuses to put up the
cash and start work in a plain business-like
way ; in fact, it apparently hasn’t the cash to
put up. Therefore it wants to float the con-
cession for a gorgeous lump sum in England,
and then go away with the profit, and have no
more trouble. But it asked <£850,000 boodle for
itself, and the concession, so far, has failed to
float at that awful price ; so now the syndicate
suggests, in addition to the other things, that
the Tasmanian Government should guarantee
interest on £600,000 worth of the co.’s deben-
tures. Of course, the Tasmanian Govern-
ment might as well guarantee interest
on .£600,000 worth of its own deben-
tures, and build the line with the money,
and own the line itself, and get the profits
itself, and save the huge land-grant, but
though the Government seems to understand
this sufficiently to reject the syndicate’s latest
and most impudent proposal, still it apparently
fails to realise that the whole Great Western
railway business is about the most incredible
swindle that the island ever suffered from up
to date. Here is the situation condensed :
(1) The Government’s west-coast railways are the only
ones which pay expenses and interest on construction.
Every other line in Tasmania shows a loss. The western
district, where it is proposed to bribe private syndicates
with immense land-grants to build and own railways, is
the one paying district in the province—the district above
all others where the State should insist on owning the
lines itself to make up for the losing business in the east
and centre. . , ...
(2) The whole land-grant railway business is a fraud.
The usual mode of reasoning is that, if a railway can’t
be profitably built and worked by the State without a
subsidy in land or otherwise, then a company should be
allowed to build it with a subsidy. Which is foolishness
and unholv rot. If it is estimated that the Great Wes-
tern railway can’t he built and worked profitably unless
Helped by a grant of (say) a million acres, then the obvi-
ous course is for the State to build it and to hand over the
million acres to its own railway department to make up
the loss. It should build its own land-grant railways, in
short.
(3) Whether the railway is built with money raised in
England by a company, or with money raised in England
by the Government, the people ot Tasmania, who use the
line, must pay the interest. And the people who have to
pay the interest owe the principal to all intents and pur-
poses. So the bill figures out like this : If the Govern-
ment built the Great Western Railway at a cost of (say)
£1,000,000, the interest would be about 3i per cent.—
£32,000 a year. If the syndicate built it the cost of the
actual line would probably be about the same, and in
addition there is a trifle of £850,000 plunder demanded
by the promoters, making £1,850,000 to pay interest on.
And the syndicate will want at least 4 per cent.—£74,ooo
a year. Tasmania has to pay the interest either way.
The only difference is that if it built the line itself the
bill would be £32,500 annually ; if it lets the syndicate
build it, the bill will be about £74,000. Also the syndi-
cate is to get the land-grant in addition.
For these reasons the private land-grant rail-
way is a fraud, and the promoter thereof is a
public enemy, and the politicians who abet
him are mostly boodlers and speculators, or
else they are too hopelessly incapable to
see the points of the game. The quality
of the average syndicate politician’s honesty
or intelligence can be gauged by the
invariable argument with which the land-grant
syndicate’s case is supported : “ The State
can’t build and work this railway without a land-
grant, therefore a private co. should be allowed
to do it with one. It is admitted that the line
can be made to pay handsomely if the earnings
are assisted by the income derivable from, say,
1,000,000 acres of land ; so instead of setting
aside 1,000,000 acres of our own land for the
purpose, and making our own railway pay, and
taking the profits ourselves, let us give the
million acres to a shindykit and let it take the
profits.” That is the whole case boiled down,
and every politician who supports private land-
grant railways on such a case calls for careful
inspection to see if it is dishonesty or only plain
stupidity that is the matter with him.
The White Flag-.
Certain Australian dailies reprinted some
remarks from G. W. Steevens’ Daily Mail
war letter re Boer treatment of prisoners at
Nicholson’s Nek, but without large type display
or any moral reflections. “ The Boers took their
revenge for many things, full measure, in
kindness.” “ They tended the wounded and
succoured the prisoners. . . . They gave the
whole men the water out of their bottles ; they
gave the wounded the blankets off their own
saddles, and slept themselves on the naked
veldt. They sent the wounded back to Lady-
smith, giving mules for their conveyance,
although they needed mules badly themselves,
and they broke up rifle stocks to make splints
although Lee-Melfords were scarce amongst
them.” The British officer in command flew the
white flag in token of surrender, of which, says
Steevens, apparently unconscious of the signifi-
cance of his remarks : —
It is some sneaking consolation that for a long time the
soldiers refused to heed it. Careless now of life, they
were sitting up well behind their breastworks, altering
their sights, aiming coolly by the half-minute together.
This time, be it noted, it was not a case of the
treacherous and malignant Boers hoisting the
white flag of truce and then firing on their
enemies—but the British. And—here comes
the astounding part of the case—the British
scribe, who is so enraged at Boer treachery
in doing exactly this thing, is proud of it when
The Saving of the Empire.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
Britannia sat in trouble,
With grey, gaunt, ashen look ;
The hand that grasped her trident
With nervous terror shook ;
The seas that were her bu wark
Were trembling with her throes,
When up against her power
Two small Republics rose.
They were singing ‘ ‘ Rule Britannia ”
In the music-halls,
When they saw the fateful writing
On the painted walls,
For it holds till this day sure
The legend sad —
Whom the high gods would slay sure
They first make mad.
The Tower of London famous,
By Julius Csesar built,
It shook to its foundations—
Remembering old guilt ;
The Stock Exchange was blighted
By black and sudden frost,
When the Cassandra cable
Brought news of battles lost.
But they still sang “Rule Britannia ”
In the music-halls,
Though more lurid gleamed the writing
On the painted walls,
But they glared at each other
With eyes not glad —
Whom the high gods would smother
They first make mad.
The Constitution sacred,
In secret cellar kept,
Beneath the House of Commons,
No longer soundly slept;
It raised itself up slowly,
W ith aching limbs and numb,
And visage melancholy—
And moaned “ My hour has come ! ’
Still they howled out “ Rule Britannia ’
In the music-halls,
Though the script had started marching
On the painted walls.
But their blood was as water,
Their feelings bad —
Whom the high gods would slaughter
They first make mad.
Then came the news tremendous,
Which flushed their faces wan—
That to the Empire’s rescue
“ A ” Battery had gone !
And ev’ry Briton living
His hat in frenzy waved,
And roared a hoarse thanksgiving
For the Em pire saved.
And they whooped out “Rule Britannia '
In the music halls,
And the lurid letters faded
From the painted walls. •
And the gods played second
Fiddles that day —
For they had not reckoned
On Battery “A.”

“A Disastrous Measure.”
Sydney Evening News persistently gathers
and publishes the opinions of small, angry
shopkeepers re early-closing, and declares the
Act “a disastrous measure.” The carefully-
selected shopkeepers are mostly newsagents —
apparently the great idea was to see if the sale
of the late edition with the ’Orror in it would
be injured. The news-agent promptly tickled
the willing ear of his interviewer with the
assurance that it would. The employers who
are applied to admit a lot of things. They
allow the information to be reluctantly torn
from them that they are good, honest, liberal-
minded men who never sweated their employes
and wouldn’t dream of such a thing, so the Act
is quite unnecessary. And they are sorry—
deeply sorry—for the shop assistants who, in
some inscrutable way, are going to be ruined by
not being worked so hard as formerly. Of
course, while the News looks for its informa-
tion only among employers it will get
this kind of information—until the first
unreasoning panic is over. It will probably
get it even after that, for the average
trader likes to pose as a man struck by
unmerited disaster—it helps his application
for a reduction in the rent. And it is impos-
sible to get much information from the em-
ployes as to the other side of the case. It
would be interesting to read that Mr. Smith,
employed in the well-known emporium of
Boggs and Cos., being interviewed, remarked
that Boggs and Cos. were sweaters of the
worst description, and their employes were
practically slaves till this Act came in force ;
also, that Boggs and Cos. were really doing
better now than in the long-hour days,
but denied it in the hope of getting the rent
reduced and with a view to faking their income-
tax schedule—but the average Smith knows
better. In fact, the average Smith knows the
average Boggs so well that he tries to look as if
he were rather sorry than otherwise to leave at
6 instead of 10 or 11. Therefore the newspaper
evidence is mostly on the Boggs side of the
case. Yet a ballot would probably show that
Boggs, though lie looks so imposing in print, is
only the tenth part of an insignificant minority,
or hardly even that.

Legal Illogicality.
The beauty of inconsistency ! At Ballarat
(Vic.) early in December, Chief Justice. Mad-
den released upon his own recognizances a
youth self-admitted to be guilty of unlawful
intimacy with a girl of 15, who professed to be
older; at the same time making sundry trite
observations upon the absurd law under which
the man, and not his equally guilty partner,
was punished. Also he gave it as his opinion
that the said law did vastly more harm than
good. Yet a week or two later, at Sale, the
same bewigged dispenser of justice apparently
altered his mind with startling suddenness.
Before him were brought two men, each charged
with intimacy with the common or precocious
damsel under 16. The girls were to all
appearances much over the stipulated age, and
were consenting parties, and in one case the
jury recommended to mercy, yet Madden, being
in a vile temper, or suffering from an impedi-
ment in his intellect or something, froze on to the
two offenders with great violence, and sentenced
each to twelve months’ hard labor and Jive lashes.
Some day, when the law is in a more sane
mood than usual, it will perhaps begin to con-
sider some aspects of this unwholesome matter
which it has hitherto neglected. For the pro-
tection of men it should be made a heavily
punishable offence for a girl of, say, 14 to look
as if she was over 16. Or else her unprincipled
conduct in alleging that she is over 16 when
she isn’t should rank as perjury, and be pun-
ished accordingly. Or, again, the law should pro-
vide some means, say, by tattooing the necessary
date on the shoulder, by which the painfully
complaisant female’s statement of her age can
be put to immediate proof. (Her frock
should, by legal enactment, always button down
the back, to facilitate reference.) The immoral,
large-sized, old-faced she-child who fraternises
with strange men, and alleges that she is years
nearer maturity than she is, and calls
her old-young countenance as a witness,
is a standing danger. If there was a
law requiring every female, under the
severest penalties, to w'ear short frocks till her
16th birthday, then the average man would
know what to do. When he saw a short-
skirted damsel looming up on the horizon he
would either run for his life or give her notice
not to approach within 100 yards at her peril.
At present, everything is loose and unfixed,
and every girl is supposed to be as old as she
looks—which she often isn’t; and as bad as she
looks—which she often isn’t, for in many cases
she is a great deal worse ; aud everj man is
supposed to know things which Solomon in all
his glory didn’t know, and the law is an ass,
and the average Judge is its prophet.

Spreading Out.
“ Hermes ” on the attenuated nature of
things:—
The other day a French Minister told a mob of French
jingoes that France with her almost stationary popula-
tion would be choosing the wiser part if she abstained
from annexing more territory and settled down to
develop what she already had. Looking at the map of
the world one cannot help being struck with the
thought that when “ Bobs,” Duller, Kitchener,
and Cos., have wiped the “Boer blot” off the
map of South Africa the time will have arrived
when the great British Empire should lay down its
smoking rifle, put its lyddite shell in its pocket, and
seriously contemplate the fact that it is getting spread
out horribly thin. In fact, the white population of the
modern Rome (including some millions of more or less
disloyal Dutch, Irish and not too enthusiastic French
Canadians) is merely one solitary white daub among six
daubs of various colors. Moreover the Empire is not a
solid hunk of territory which can be gridironed with
strategic railways, it is a collection of fragments scattered
far apart, and in some cases with hundreds of miles of
frontiers contiguous to those of other great military
powers. In America its Canadian border is also
the U.S. border for about 1000 miles; in Asia
the Indian border will soon be within striking
distance of the Russian camps ; in Africa, it bumps
against French, German, Portuguese, and other terri-
tories. With a total force of between 70,000 and 100,000
men the possibly greatest living British generals
find the South African nut so hard to crack that more
army corps are to be dispatched from Britain to assist in
the ‘contract. South Africa is a country of vast dis-
tances (most parts of the British Empire are countries of
vast distances), and it is to all intents and purposes a
hostile country, with the extra disadvantage that the
troops in their present locations may not destroy pro-
perty, as it is nominally British. Wherever the Boer
pony has set his hoof the bridges have been destroyed
and the railways torn up. The Boer always chooses his
ground and awaits the British onset on ridges and behind
kopjes which can only be finally taken by rushes of men.
In these rushes of men the stormers always lose heavily,
and when they are almost taken, the Boer rises up
hastily and quite fresh, and retreats to the next ridge or
kopje, which has to be taken in the same manner at the
same cost of life. And the British are getting sick of it.
They want more men. The days of small armies have
gone by. The force that can be surrounded is doomed.
The little army corps of 15,000 men, so beloved of British
commanders, is a thing of the past. Safety is absolutely
a matter of numbers. So, when the weary Titan has
accomplished his South African contract and named his
sovereign lady Empress of Africa, it is time for him to
pack up his warlike gear, buy some cradles, and settle
down to breed hard, for Mars has been dethroned,
and the new god of war is the small damp baby.
The only way to secure South Africa for the British
Empire is to have an overwhelming British popu-
lation, agricultural as well as professional and shopkeep-
ing, side by side with that of the Dutch. The Gape
Colony volunteers who are serving with the British to-day
are mostly the descendants of 2000 British families sent
out to Algoa Bay nearly 70 years ago by the British to
guard the frontiers of the colony against the Kaffirs. It
cost the Government of the day about £50,000, but it was
one of the best investments the British Government ever
made. And if the sub-continent is to remain British the
Government will have to do something of the sort to-day.
The Boer is, in some things, a most ignorant hog, but if
the British Empire were to take a leaf out of his book it
might do wisely. When the Boers conquered the Trans-
vaal they divided it into so many farms. As far as this
writer can discover it was an understood thing that every
male Boer, by virtue of his Boerdom, was entitled to a
farm. If there was not enough land in the State to give
him one the State very properly went out and stole some
under arms, and handed the farm over with a lordly grace
that would have done credit to a Roman Emperor.
There is no need for the British Empire to go out and
steal any more to give every man in its population who
really needs it a farm. There is room within the Empire
and to spare, and the sooner the whole show is
turned into a co-operative settlement the better.
Whenever Russia has annexed new population it has
been population which she could assimilate and
Russianise. Whenever Britain has annexed new
peoples, except in Canada and Cape Colony,
they were niggers or yellow men with whom her people
cannot mix without degenerating, and to whom educa-
tion means simply the discovery that they are an inferior
race. A discovery like that is never calculated to make
the black and yellow man friendly to the Britisher, and
in all probability some day the British will have to fight
their colored fellow-subjects or lose them. When people
go on to the frontiers and settle down to work in the
open air they breed splendidly. The man from the farm
has always made the best soldier. The Roman legionary
who wiped the floor with the earth was a peasant farmer.
The English who overran France were tillers of the soil,
and when they were killed off the English empire in
France crumpled up. The Spanish soldier who was once
the terror of Europe was a hard working individual from
the Iberian vineyards. The Ironsides of Cromwell were
farmers, and the Britishers who played the deuce and
broke things during the Napoleonic wars were farm-
hands and crofters. The victorious German armies of
1870 were drawn from an agricultural population, and
France in her hour of agony built up huge armies in a
day from the peasant farmers. And so on right down the
list. Therefore, I advise the alleged weary Titan to have
a spell, and turn his quick-firers into go-carts, and fill up
his frontier districts with a hard}', healthy agricultural
population, and breed as hard as possible, and when the
great day of Armageddon comes he may triumph by
sheer weight of babies.
The War Bill.
The Australian producers begin to pay the
price of the false-loyalty boom straight away.
N.S.W. charges much of the bill indirectly to
loan account, and so passes the burden on,
though there is a call for the interest year by
year. South Australia, however, is apparently
going to struggle to pay most of its share from
current revenue ; but as that is only barely
enough for peaceful requirements, and the death
of one moderately well-to-do farmer more or
less means to Treasurer Holder a surplus or a
deficit, Ministers have already looked round
for something or somebody to squeeze. Fif-
teen months ago it was proposed to charge
Is. 6d. per ton more for carrying Broken
Hill ores over S.A. lines : but Parliament
twice jumped on the proposal—first when
it was introduced, and again a few weeks
ago. The case is quite simple. The Govern-
ment makes a fair profit on the Barrier
line—enough, anyhow, to make ud for the big
losses on some other lines. Still, some Broken
Hill ores could stand more freight; the richest
could, in fact, stand five times more. But
there are, on the other hand, tens of thousands
of tons of low grade ore which barely pays ex-
penses—said expenses amounting to about 355.
per ton. An extra shilling turns this ore into
mullock ; it is left in the mines, and, of course,
the 355. per ton is not distributed (labor usually
gets four-fifths of it). The S.A. Parliament,
therefore, threw out the proposal because its
adoption would render tens of thousands of tons
of ore unprofitable, and would thus reduce the
life of the province’s best customer ; besides it
saw that sooner or later the province gets most of
the3ss. perton, beforementioned,becauseS. Aus.
supplies Broken Hill with most of its pro-
visions, and does most of its carrying and
smelting. Therefore the proposal amounted to
S.A., in its great need for 2d., paying 6d. for it.
No sooner, however, had the Parliament, which
twice refused to sanction the increase, gone into
recess than Harassed Holder, wanting money
to shoot his brother Methodist in the Transvaal,
issued a bylaw raising freights 9d. per ton.
Give us pence in our time, O Lord —and hang
the future ! So the Broken Hill co’s will
be forced to leave untouched thousands of
ore which they have hitherto been able to
handle, to the ultimate loss of scores of thou-
sands of pounds sterling to the country. And
this is the first effect of the fever in only one
little section of the continent; all the others
will pay, sooner or later, and more or less.

The Moan of Old Groperdom.
Westralian departmental officials are appar-
ently instructed, whenever possible, to drag
disparaging remarks about Federation into
their reports, and explain that the unusual
drought, or the prevalence of dingoes, or the
great increase of dogs under the bed, is doubt-
less owing to its near approach—people realise
that their country is lost and are too dejected
to put the dog out as before. This, from the
Department of Agriculture, is very fine :
The department responded to a very larjje number of
enquiries on dairying- matters duriner the half-year, but
so far there is no indication of the establishment of
another factory. lam inclined to think that the unwel-
come prospect of Federation is keeping these and kindred
industries back, but I hope that the tour of the colony by
the dairy expert with the travelling- dairy plant will cause
a revival, and that it will not be long before we hear of a
movement in the direction of the establishment of
another factory.
Ihings are getting no worse, you see, than
before the disastrous prospect came in view,
but even the fact that theyare remaining as they
were shows that Federation is bad. Of course,
if butter factories were increasing that would
also show that Federation is bad ; it would be
explained that when the industry was growing
so fast under provincialism it was obvious that
provincialism was a good thing and not to be
lightly disturbed. It is a weird fact that
even the failure of the Sandgroper butter in-
dustry under provincialism can be claimed as
showing that provincialism is a most excellent
arrangement. Or, put it this way for short:
(1) If a thing fails now under provincialism it is wholly
owing to the prospect of Federation.
(2) If a thing succeeds now under provincialism its
success is owing to the presence of provincialism— not
to the prospect of Federation.
(3) If a, thing has always been a failure under provin-
cialism, that shows it is kept back by the prospect of
federation ; after failing for many years under pro-
vincialism it was just going to start prospering under
la 1 ? m on '-'" Federal prospect prevented it.
. W “ an industry, after long failure under provincial-
ism, starts to boom now that Federation is in prospect,
that shows that the initial difficulties have at last been
overcome, and if the nefarious Federal idea is only
abandoned and things left as they are, there are good
times ahead.
(5) If an industry is going down, it is the prospect of
Federation that, is killing it.
an industry remains exactly as it was all along
that shows that it is the near approach of a disastrous
r ederation that makes it remain as it was all along.
In fact, whether a thing happens suddenly, or
stops happening suddenly, or just keeps hap-
pening monotonously, it all shows that Federa-
tion means ruin.
A Drapery Swindle.
“ Malle ” : In many Sydney shops, employes,
whose nominal wages are only a few shillings a
week, have even that pittance reduced, some-
times by as much as one-half, through fines
inflicted under all kinds of pretences. Crimes
include failing to make sales a certain number
of times to the impossible women who never
intended to buy anything, being a minute late,
sitting down, lack of promptitude, or w T earing
an expression which the floor-nobleman thinks
isn’t the sort of expression they should wear,
even though it may be the only expression they
have ; also answering the nobleman back ; like-
wise being sulky and not answering him back.
The Bulletin has a vague idea that both fine
and imprisonment are the prerogatives of
magistrates and judges—not of floor-walkers or
any other kind of draper ; and that it is just as
illegal for the latter to fine a miserable girl-
employ4 Is. as to sentence her to seven days
imprisonment in the cellar. In theory it is a mere
matter of agreement; the assistant, w’hen en-
gaged, becomes subject to the rules of the estab-
lishment and they provide for fines for a large
number of offences—also, they make the man who
profits by the fines sole judge and jury to decide
when these offences are committed. Yet if the
rules specified, instead of fines, imprisonment
in the shop’s private black-hole, chains, or
caning, the law would fall on that drapery
fiend promptly. No matter though a girl-em-
ploye fully agreed to be bound by rules which
gave her boss power to lock her up or cane her
for specified offences, the law would regard the
one as illegal detention and the other as assault.
And when the law gets its second wind it will
decide in like manner that the fining process is
plain embezzlement. In fact, if the matter
were properly placed before it, it would pro-
bably come to that decision now without wait-
ing for any more wind than it has got.
Governor Brassey is going to make his exit
from Melbourne city at “the head (not the tail)
of the second Victorian Contingent. The
association of Brassey with the young man
who has undertaken to shoot for the integrity
of the bulging British Empire, and uphold the
value of his stake in the dear old country, will
be fittingly terminated when the procession has
passed over Queen’s Bridge. His Excellency
(salute !) will then break away in the direction
of Port Melbourne Town Pier, with certain
yachting vanities in view, whilst the more
interesting “loyalists” march to the Railway
Pier. The arrangement, needless to say, is in-
tended to give an air of popularity to Brassey’s
vice-regal person on this noisy occasion. Mon-
day’s newspaper reports will place a liberal
percentage of the cheering to the Governor’s
credit; also, it will be faintly suggested that a
few of the tears of the Contingent’s troubled
mothers, sisters and best girls were really shed
for Baron B. aforesaid. Hence The Bulletin’s
timely assertion that Nobody, in a general way
of speaking, will raise a tear or a cheer for the
departing Governor of Vic. on Saturday next.
The fact that he will depart on the 13th inst.
may be said to shatter an ancient superstition
re the unluckiness of number thirteen. As
a speechmaker, a social entertainer, a figure-
head and a shining light Governor
Brassey has proved himself a failure. Some
of the irritating tactics charged against
the rubicund old gentleman may have been due
to the mental feebleness of his official flunkeys, '
but, anyway, it should be the first care of any
Vice-regal sojourner to see that his flunkeys
are of fair average intelligence. As regards
the notorious cheapness of Brassey’s hospi-
tality, The Bulletin finds no fault with his
Lordship. In so ordering his wines and light
refreshments that a number of “representa-
tive ” guzzlers lost all enthusiasm for Govern-
ment House tickets, Brassey was doing an un-
intentional service to the Democratic cause.
But he served no good purpose by invariably
being behind time at functions where the
grovelsome authorities always awaited his
arrival.
One of the many Federation difficulties in
Westralia is the Forrest Government’s de-
mand that the mail-steamers, which already
call at the Westralian port of Albany, will go
many miles out of their way to call at Fre-
mantle instead. This will mean a delay of at
least 12 hours, and that 12 hours later arrival
and 12 hours earlier departure will mean that
merchants in N.S.W., Victoria, Queensland,
and Tasmania will be a whole week
behind in their correspondence. W.A. pays
one-tenth of the mail subsidy, and already has
an advantage over the other provinces inas-
much as it gets the first delivery of letters.
But unless the steamers come right to the back-
door of Perth so as to bring thither the trade
that now goes to Albany, the Forrest crowd
won’t be satisfied. The possible end of it all
may be that the eastern provinces will
arrange for an accelerated mail service
which will leave Westralia out altogether,
and unfederated Westralia will have to nego-
tiate a service of its own. Forrest’s Govern-
ment has still another grievance in its post
and telegraph department. In the scheme for
a cable from South Africa to Australia the
cable co. proposed to bring the new line ashore
at Albany (W. A.), but the Bigjoiin Government
demands that it also shall come direct to Perth.
Big John’s idea is that everything, barring the
plague and the bailiff’, should come ashore at
Perth, so as to bring all the trade to the place
where brother Alice: and bear-leader Hackett
have so much property.
The usual happy-go-lucky, let-it-rip style of
doing things was, the other day, responsible for
another man’s death in Melbourne. The de-
ceased had been carrying goods aboard the
Ouraka when he slipped from the gangway,
missed the protecting net, and was drowned.
It was shown in evidence that the gangway had
only one protecting rail, whereas the Act
requires two, and the timorous coroner had a
fine opportunity to show his strength and use-
fulness by summoning before him the respon-
sible person and inviting him to explain his
delinquency. But he carefully avoided the
chance, and urged that the occurrence was
purely accidental. Verdict of accidental death
accordingly. So the process of losing men’s
lives and saving a two-shilling rope will pre-
sumably go on as usual.
4.
Launceston (Tas.) Examiner utters a truth
which this paper has been hammering at for
years:—
Has the present generation a right to pay for what it
uses up or not ? Let us take an ordinary timber bridge,
the life of which is from 30 to 40 years. Suppose the
policy is followed of charging renewals or repairs to loans
for a few generations, what will then be the position?
Will it not be that those who are then living will be
debited with the cost of, it may be, half-a-dozen bridges,
when in reality they have one only ? Those who have
preceded them will have used the previous structures, and
will have thrown the cost of them all on posterity. We are
told that posterity should be satisfied, as it will have as
an asset a well-developed country. This is true, but if
that country is mortgaged up to the hilt, then those who
come after will have reasonable cause to complain. There
is but one proper financial course to pursue, namely, that
followed by every sound and well-conducted business
firm. If we turn to their balance-sheets we find that
every year there is a writing-down of the value of pro-
perties in use and an allowance made for wear and tear.
Any repairs that are found necessary are paid out of the
earnings of the year. So it should be with regard to our
national affairs. What are roads and bridges but the
means by which we carry on our national business?
And as such they should be kept in repair as we go along.
If we use up a bridge or wear out a road we have a right
to replace the one and mend the other. The fact that
these works are not revenue-earning has nothing to do
with the matter; but if it had, it does but furnish an
additional reason why they should be kept in repair out
of revenue. The earning capacity of our railways may,
and doubtless will, increase as time goes on, but the same
cannot be said of our roads and bridges. They have no
earning capacity except so far as they are of use to the
generation of the day. Let it be assumed, for the sake
of argument, that it is sound finance to charge repairs
and renewals to loans, then why raise or levy any rates.
Why not charge the whole to loans ? It would cer-
tainly be an easy way of getting over our difficulties. We
could dispense with the rate collector altogether, and
local bodies would have an easy time of it. The fact that
the interest bill would be increasing need not bother us
in the least, as it would be an easy matter to raise more
money by way of loans to meet every item.

Westralian Premier Forrest has come over
to the East to negotiate with the benighted
Premiers on this side, aud persuade them to
accept the W.A. committee’s proposed amend-
ments on the Federal Constitution. Forrest
says (in a S.M. Herald interview published
last Monday) that—
Unless some means are devised by which the amend-
merits required by the Legislature of Western Australia
can be given consideration to, it will not be possible for
that province to enter the Federation as an original
State.
It would be interesting to know what Forrest
thinks he is thinking about. The Westralian
Assembly voted that both Bills—the existing
one and the proposed Bill with the amendments
alluded to above—should be submitted to the
people, therefore it didn’t say that it “re-
quired ” the said, amendments. It left the
matter to the people. The Westralian Council,
after a series of confused votes, refused to have
anything to do with either Bill. So far from
declaring that Bigjohn’s amendments were
“required” it refused even to listen to
a proposal that they should be laid
before the other provinces for their assent
—which presumably means that even if the
eastern provinces accept them the W.A.
Council won’t. The W.A. people haven’t said
the amendments were required—they haven’t
been consulted. The amendments which For-
rest says are required to ensure Westralia’s
adherence to the Commonwealth haven’t been
definitely accepted by any authority in Wes-
tralia, and J ohn is a delusive person without
any credentials.
Sample of alleged Australian sentiment fired
off at a Tas. Patriotic Fund meeting, the other
night, by a leading official of the local branch
of the A.N.A. :
All money subscribed in Tasmania should be sent to
England, to be distributed through the Mansion House
Fund. I recognise, as every Australian roust recognise,
that in this war England is fighting Australia’s battles
(applause), and should have full control of all our men
and money. (Cheers.) Another reason, your Excellency
(Gormanston was in the chair, and had treated
the audience to the funniest history of South
Africa ever imagined by even the most frantic
lunatic of all the jingoes) another reason why
the money subscribed for the relief of the families of
our troops should go to the Mansion House Fund, is that
Tasmania is such a small place.and so poor, that in all
probability the beneficiaries will receive more from the
Mansion House Fund than if Tasmanian contributions
were kept for Tasmanian sufferers ! (Prolonged cheers).
Old Adye Douglas was the only speaker who
opposed the proposition, but it evidently re-
quired a younger and more vigorous republican
than old Adye to stem the sheep-like rush of
the Natives after the jingoistic bell-wether of
Imperialism. And the money goes to England.
What share of it will come back to the
one-legged Tasmanian will be seen by-and-bye-

“David Garrick” appeared at the Criterion
last Saturday for a six nights’ season. This play is
always a favorite —probably because the moral is so
totally and sinfully wrong. A respectable aider-
man’s daughter falls in love with actor Garrick be-
cause he impersonates Hamlet well, whereupon the
old man is unspeakably shocked. These were the
days when it was considered rather frivolous to
read Shakespeare ; nowadays the girl who studies
Hamlet is a serious and weighty personage of
enormous soul-tonnage. What old Ingot would
have done if Ada had been a modern girl
and had become enamored of a frivolous
mummer’s legs is past all saying; anyway,
he is much put out, and agrees gladly
when said mummer kindly offers to seem so drunk
and objectionable that Ada will shun him and
Shakespeare thereafter, and rush gladly to the
embrace of the other young man, who is really
drunk. Why public opinion has never risen up
against that parent will perhaps be made clear in
the Hereafter. Some odium is likewise due to
David Garrick, the pure, unselfish hero who com-
mands his best girl to take the other beery fellow,
in order that her father may shed tears of joy on
her head, and her future children may respect her
for giving them a parent who comes home drunk
every night. And when one reflects that she
would have taken the other fellow, if the old man
had not happened to listen at the door, the moral
of a play which goes askew if someone doesn’t
listen at the door calls loudly for disapproval.
This paper would have some admiration for the
virtuous mummer if it could think that he heard
the old man coming, and gave his moral lesson
accordingly ; but as it is, it badly wants to know
the 18th-century word for Dog, that it may call
David that, and be at peace.
* ste * *
The audience which had fled from the
Transvaal drama rushed back when it heard that
Walter Bentley had been specially engaged
to perform this atrocious libel on a fellow mummer
who cannot resent the indignity for reasons that
are obvious. Walter does the libel in the same
graceful manner as of yore, so that one does not
observe what a prig he is till the curtain
is down, which is Art. Miss Dunckley is not
equally convincing as the girl who wanders round
with the large red Shakespeare under her arm.
The soulful eyes of by-gone Lilian Wheeler are
badly wanted, as necessary appurtenances to the
type of girl who falls in love without knowing
about the settlements. Miss Dunckley gives an
impression of too much sense altogether, and when
Bentley is advising her how to bring up her
children—the other fellow’s children—the chil-
dren of the drunk young man who lives in an
atmosphere of stable and dog—one momen-
tarily expects her to get up and toll him
not to be a silly ox. The hat which she
wears in the last act quite explains Garrick s noble
unselfishness in giving her to the other man.
Harry Diver, in a wig like a snowed-upon hay-
stack, takes the part of the young man who is
really drunk, and except for the fact that his legs
are much drunker than his voice, would impress
the audience very favourably—if one could forget
his splendid acting in, say, “The Ilesh and the
Devil.” The fact that he acted so magnificently
in “The Flesh and the Devil” is going to be a
curse to Diver for many days to come. Sweeney,
as the respectable alderman with the curious taste
in sons-in-law, rises to the occasion in the first act.
The dinner-party somehow leaves an impression
that someone has forgotten his part, or that an
important character is detained behind the scenes
—but that is a mere trifle.

That weary Kipling song, about the mendi-
cant whose intellect was away, now rings out
nightly at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. Miss Dorothy
Vane sings it, and The Bulletin representative
listens to it occasionally, and feels that he takes
the prize for patience, with «Tob ten lengths behind
and breaking down rapidly. Why this poetic gem
should be hung so persistently at night on. the
wreck of the public intellect it is difficult
to say. Still, the pantomime is the only
show that is doing very large business jusc
now in Sydney. Possibly the melody about
the pauper who went away leaving his brain
behind him on the towel-rail really attracts
the public. Possibly the public forgives the song
for the sake of Dorothy Vane’s bright young face
and fresh young voice. Perhaps the pictorial
glories of the pantomime, which are many and
glorious, are sufficient to keep things going de-
spite the drawback of the Beggar. These things
arc mercifully concealed from us. All the people
herein commended last week have earned further
approbation in the interval, especially Miss Came
Moore, whose loveliness in the brown uniform is
beyond all question. At the same time Carrie
and her troops don’t seem to capture Kruger’s
castle after all. They are left pounding on the
door of that tower when the curtain comes down.
