“ The Only Way,” at Sydney Royal, ought
to be renamed “The Only Play,” because it is
about the oidy theatrical production at present in
Sydney which does not say anything about the
dreadful subject of the contingent. The manage-
ment probably refrained lest the audience might
think it an anachronism. The play is noticeable
for a kind of mystic glooin which envelopes the
characters from the very beginning. Everyone
feels something dreadful is going to happeu even
before there is any decided news on the subject.
There is a lost, doomed look about Carton’s hair ;
it is long and clan.iny, like hair whose natural
place is the grave. The pathos of Sydney
Carton’s sacrifice really lies to a great extent
in the fact that he has been such a bad lot
during the earlier period of his existence. If
he had been a good young man all that time,
and gone to church regularly, and given up his
cricket subscription to the poor, no one would
have thought half so much of his noble conduct in
taking the place on the guillotine of the other
man with the similar hair. The reason is simple
enough. Carton’s conduct is a compliment to
every hard-drinker in the audience. It makes
him feel that the road to heroism is lined with
beer and snakes, and that he is a noble character
just like Carton because he drinks like him, and
that if he continues drinking he may soar to in-
credible heights of glory.
• * • •
All the same “ The Only Way ” hasn’t
been a great success. It will cease happening
before thin houses this week. “Tess of the
D’Urbervilles ” is announced for Saturday. Mr.
Tyrone Power and Miss Edith Crane (the lady
whose bare feet left an imprint on this country’s
memory in “Trilby”) are the principals in the new
drama.
* m * *
The spangled-white child who appears in
the stage clouds, and looks like nothing hut an
old-fashioned Christmas card, still strikes terror
into the beer-sodden soul of the villain at Sydney
Lyceum. “The Power and the Glory” is a re-
freshingly immoral play. The virtuous heroine,
who won't be anything but legally married to the
aristocrat, is thereupon robbed of her child and
repudiated by her husband, and gaoled, and tied
up, and nearly thrown off a monument. And
when her persecutor is shot, she merely re-marries
into her own class, and probably takes in washing,
and doesn’t seem likely to have any better time
than she had before. The wicked adventuress, who
also returns to lowly life as a panto, girl, has at least
the satisfaction of remembering that she had a good
time while it lasted, but with the good woman
times are sometimes bad and mostly worse. When
a law can be passed depriving criminals of the
good times they have had, the tide of morality
may go up. The conduct of the blind child is
inexplicable even before she gets among the span-
gled clouds, because it is a scientific fact that
when a blind person regains the sight he has
never possessed, he has no knowledge of perspec-
tive whatever, and frequently knocks people
down because he thinks they are still half-a-inile
off, and likewise he may be seen cautiously
refraining from upsetting mountains that are
some dozen miles distant. Consequently when
Linda picks out her friends without once
banging her small nose against them, she perforins
a scientific miracle. These remarks are not in-
tended to detract in any way from the pictorial
magnificence of the “Power and the Glory.” As
a spectacle its glory is beyond all question and
almost beyond all record.
* * * *
At Sydney Palace there is a young man
named Daly with a fine baritone voice which lie
doesn’t know how to use. Also, he is not quite
sure what bis hands are for —still, with that voice
he should have a good future before him if he can
get under a good hard master. The most painful
circumstance is a man with a black face billed as
Wal. Cottier. There is quite an outbreak of
dancing by Miss Sinclair, Fanny Powers, the
Smith Sisters, the Newsky Family, the Marion-
ettes, Miss Taylor, and the Bell Ballet. The
Marionettes command great applause, especially
the two goblins that dance in the graveyard, a
Chinese juggler who gets fearfully and wonder-
fully mixed up, and the ancient clown who is
bombarded with old cases and kerosene - tins
and comes up with the cheerful remark, “ And
he never hit me ! ” after each avalanche. The
skeleton that is rapidly dying of that tired feeling
till a gun-shot fills him with fresh energy, and
causes him to indulge in grotesque dances all in
one piece, instead of separating as he did when he
was too tired to dance all at once and had to do
it in sections, is funny. The Newskys break out
in several new places. Their dancing is original,
while their songs seem to be mostly something
about “-ski” and are solemn and reminiscent
of the name Archangel.
* * * *
The severity of the midsummer weather
in Melbourne last week kept many people from
going to the Royal to get hotter, but “ Little
Christopher Columbus ” maintained a good
average prosperity ail the same. A rise in the
temperature makes no difference to the music,
color, and personal character of this burlesque-
opera, although it is very trying to the poor little
jokes and puns. The dialogue seemed limp and
despondent last week, especially in the second
act, where streaks of conversation serve to
fill the crevices in an oriental variety
show which is said to be happening close to
the Chicago Exhibition. When the thermometer
registers !)5 in the shade, or thereabouts, the
humor of that second act ought not to be ex-
pected to raise a laugh. The constant strain of
pretending to be cool and festive under adverse
conditions has not visibly affected Little Chris-
topher. Also, Mrs. Block and Guinevere ; like-
wise Silas, l’epita, Don Juan, Captain Slammer,
daughter Hannah, and the ballet appear much
the same as usual, but O’Hooligan’s understand-
ings threaten to melt into nothingness. They are
now so thin that he looks as though his life were
hanging on to a couple of threads.

At the Alexandra, which claims to be
Melbourne’s coolest theatre, despite the warm
odours of steak and onions that float into the
auditorium from the surrounding district when
supper-time draws near—at the appetising Alex-
andra, be it known, the Vic. Op. Soc. is continu-
ing its smooth, respectable performance of “ Les
Cloches ” for a third week. The tuneful Markee
has improved somewhat in his method of paying
addresses to Germaine, and Germaine, who was
quite satisfactory when Miss Ray Jones played
her, is just as pleasing in the form of Miss Boanas,
a handsome damsel with a round soprano warble.
The Serpolette (Miss Kate Hardiman) has gone
from good to better, as amateurs go, neither have
certain other people taken a turn for the worse.
A few wicked comments on the tenor’s stage
figure are cancelled herewith out of respect for
the chorus and orchestra. The little season at the
Haleck will terminate in a burst of floral tributes
next Saturday (13th), The Bulletin supposes.

At tho Bijou, an evenly balanced pro-
gramme fills the perennial want. In the natural
course of business the Rickards show will lash
itself into a patriotic fury next Saturday, on
which occasion Melbourne will get drunk again—
probably drunker than when the first contingent
went away. The sanguine agitator who snorted
“To tl—l with Kruger and the Boers,” two
months ago, is no longer available, but
the supply of war songsters is practically
unlimited, and the local hotel resources are inex-
haustible, therefore another great night at the
Bijou seems certain to ensue. Opinions may
differ on the Boer question, but if it comes down
to Beer the present writer feels no compunction
about swilling Long Life to as many Contingents
as anybody likes to suggest.
At Poverty Point.
Demise by Fire, Ballarat Theatre Royal. Foundation
laid by O. V. Brooke, in 1858, with Coppin as fugleman.
Magnificent orations about the great step in advance thus
taken by the noble legitimate drama. Ballarat even
then had had three theatres, the Montezuma, Napier,
and Tontine. Hoskins was the manager most identified
with the Royal, where he did five Shakesperian produc-
tions, such as the “Tempest.” Nevertheless, the house
came down to a drapery shop, and has been devoted to
vulgar trade for 27 years. " Similar-wise, the Geelong
Theatre Royal became first a brewery, then the Salva-
tion Army Barracks. And the Melb. Queen’s turned to a
coach factory. The foundations of a drapery shop, in
Smith-street, Collingwood, were laid for a theatre.
“ llaresfoot: The just burnt Ballarat Theatre Royal
had an unlucky thirteen years of Thespian existence.
Its moving spirit was Maxwell Brown, who, after dwel-
ling at Poverty Point, made a pile out of lucky Ballarat
mines. Of course there was naught for him to do but
regenerate the drama. The Royal saw everything that
was tip-top, ranging through Brooke, Sullivan, Dillon,
Jefferson, Mr. and Mrs. C. lvean, Celeste, Montgomery,
Lyster’s Grand Operas, and landing poor, kindly, plain-
tive Max in the slough where he started. Then he tried
conjuring, introduced the basket trick, and took to other
precarious devices. Max wrote one remarkably good
drama, “The Will and the Way.” It was an incessant
favorite in the goldfield days of Kate O’Reilly and Kate
Warde.
A Sydney Criterion advt. recently announced “ ‘ A Tale
of the Transvaal,’founded on that famous novel ‘Jess,’
depicting thrilling and realistic events now taking place
in the Transvaal.” “Jess ” was written many years ago,
before the present war was thought of. The man that
would disguise Haggard’s fine story by bringing 10
Australians to the rescue of the British Empire, and
advertise that pathetic work as depicting the events of
the present campaign, would describe the Deluge as a
thrilling episode during the recent wet weather.

“ D.”: Maud M‘Carthy, who is to fiddle at the concert in
aid of the Factory Girls’Club, is very much of a girl herself.
In the intervals of concert work Miss Maud rusticates at
Coogee, and romps on the beach with the youngest, and
even (in strict confidence) climbs trees. Coogee, by the
way, always a favorite spot with theatricals, still mourns
the beauteous Carne, who returned per tram after the
performance with closed eyes, thus affording infatuated
damsels an opportunity of gazing their fill on his classic
countenance.

buried in Melbourne last week, was Jupp. The stage
title had become so strongly associated with the family,
however, that the heir, Rev. Gladstone, the anti-dancing
parson of Nathalia, has retained it. Tet Jupp would
sound well enough for clerical purposes.
The late Edward Gladstone was an actor of uncertain
age apparently, for, whereas the books of the Old Actors’
Fund made hint out to be an octogenarian, an obituary
advt. in Melb. Auk set him down as a comparative youth
Of 72. The best work be did within the past many yea'.s
was undoubtedly his “ loyal tenant” sketch in “ Little
Lord Kauntleroy." liis lust performance on any stage
was that of the steward in “Under the Red Rohe.'”
Truth to tell, the veteran wasn’t nearly strong enough
for the part. Poor old Gladdy had been dying by inches
for a fortnight, and his family, including the Dancing
Parson of Nathalia, were able to rally round him at the
finish.
Extracts from a London newspaper criticism of a new
war drummer, entitled “ The Absent-Minded Beggar.”
“ Gilbert liay comes galloping into the camp at Glencoe,
having ridden with despatches from General Yule to
Ladysmith, and having returned amidst a terrific
thunderstorm. Hay detects the features of Van Buren,
now a Boer spy, in a pseudo Bril ish officer, the Africander
having donned the garb of a slain English captain, and
having tried to frighten Kathleen from exposing him by
threatening to have her child at Durban killed. ... A
rascally, braggart Boer, named Peter Hoch, intends to
despoil his friend, Van Buren, of the expected fair booty,
Kathleen. Hoch commands a party of Boers who attack
the fugitive women and chtllren under the strangely
inadequate escort of three raw recruits from London.
... At the beginning of Act 4, Van Buren, sentenced to
be shot as a spy, is allowed to have a farewell interview
with his mother at her house at Ladysmith. Disregarding
his pledge to make no attempt to escape, the villain robs
his mother, half murders her, and would have got clear
away had he not been shot dead by the watching Kaffir
whom he had formerly ill-treated. ... A change takes
us to the enemy's position on the heights which are being
stormed by the English. This scene should he worked
more slowly, and thus greater effect would be given to
the treacherous use of the white Jlag by the Boers to pro-
cure the temporary cessation of firing. The Boers use
their quick-firing gun vigorously during the brief respite
thus afforded them, but their treachery was of no avail,
for the heights are carried in most splendid fashion.”
The one thing wanting to improve the educational influ-
ence of this great Imperialistic work is a scene depicting
a Boer Cannibal Feast, with President Kruger in the
chair. There are many British playgoers who would be
quite prepared to learn that the Boers conceal the num-
ber of their losses in battle by eating their dead.
Leslie Stuart, the author of “ Soldiers of the Queen,”
is raking in the profits on a sale of 12,0u0 weekly. W hen
he first wrote the ever-present song it was a hitter satire ;
and, published in the Artist 8 Model, it fell flat, lu those
days the first verse ran :
When war's rumors suddenly begin.
Our chests expand with mighty swell;
We’ve no dread for sound of battle's din,
The gatling gun, the shot and shell.
Enemies are quaking, we’ve declared to fight,
The British lion’s mouth shows foam ;
We don’t fear them,
For we're not near them,
But we can read about it here at home.
Stuart was out for dollars and, finding that the public
would not even nibble at the satire, he prompt Jy with-
drew it, and re-wrote it in its present form, and tried it on
the dog once more. The dog fairly flew at it, and has
been absorbing it as fast as possible ever since.
He Sinclair’s burlesque Cinquevalli act in “ Little Chris-
topher Columbus.” Not many are aware that Sinclair is

that line in English music-halls.
Some years ago, when Snazelle was touring South
Africa with his “ Music, Song and Story ” show, he was at
the Standard Theatre, Johannesburg, and one night he
treated a dense audience of Boers and Uitlanders to views
of the “ Passion Play." When the great picture of Christ
was thrown on the canvas, the Burghers gazed silently at
the marred visage and the crown of thorns, and then a
murmur went round the house, and immediately the
Boers commenced to discuss the accuracy or otherwise of
the picture. The argument ended with a cry of rage
from one old Boer : “Can’t be—where’s his pipe?”
Adelaide University, which was the first in Australia to
establish a Chair of Music, has now seven graduates in
that, art—four ladies and three of the other sort. One of
the last couple to qualify is Judge Burnley’s only daugh-
ter ; the other is the daughter of the local Commissioner
of Audit, Ebenezer Cooke.

Most Australians still remember Ada Ward, who left
the stage and put on the glaring red jersey of the Sal.
Army. Scoffers maintained at the time that her conver-
sion was conveniently postponed till her best days were
over, and the takings were falling off lamentably before
she saw the evil of her ways - but that is an irrelevance.
Poor Ada, it seems, didn’t find peace leaping in the mud
behind the drum and the tambourine, bo she quitted the
poke-bonnet crowd and joined the Church Army. And
she didn't find peace there either, so the other day the
willowy figure which used to delight this country was
publicly dipped in cold water, and Ada Ward is tempor-
arily resting in the bosom of the Baptist sect.
“Compton” : Went to hear Maud M’Carthy’slast Syd-
ney recital, and was artistically disappointed. A great
audience, and plenty of real enthusiasm. Rut Maudie
left me cold. A tip for the Sydney music critic, which
has never, in a fairly-prolonged concert experience,
failed me yet. When you are coming away think of
gyges—do you rememher him or don’t you ? Sykes is so
good that if the star is good enough to eclipse him in
your general recollection of the evening, that star is a
genius. If she isn’t, she may have all the minor things
—touch, taste, and training, accuracy, tunefulness and
feeling, all of which indeed the small prodigy of last week
made ample display of—and yet the heavenly spark is
not there. Tried in this balance, Maud M. is found want-
ing, as are most of the debutantes Sydney has heard
lately. I remember too much Sykes.

Mr. J. A. Wallace, the pioneer of the gold
dredging industry in Australia, holds a long stretch of
the Sandy Creek, near Yaokandandah, and intends instal-
ling a very large electrical plant to operate several
dredges. The Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South
Melbourne, has an order to supply high-class compound
condensing engines with Corliss valve gear of 400 h.p.,
together with boilers and fittings, to operate the first sec-
tion of the plant. A duplicate plant to tji>8 will be in-
stalled later and will work three electric dredges on a
stretch of about two' miles of the creek. Capacity of each
dredge will be about 150 cubic yards per hour.*

* * • •
The United Brothers gold mine, situated at
Glen Wills, about 25 miles from Omeo, Gippsland, has an
improved appearance, and even better things are looked
for as deeper ground is reached. The mine possesses an
up-to-date mill. The stone is conveyed automatically to
hopper, thence to Gate’s Crusher and on to 20 head of
stamps of the A.T.B. pattern. A small but complete
plant of Luhrig Vanners and Classifiers treats the battery
tailings and continues to win a large percentage of valu-
able concentrates, which are a satisfactory source of
revenue to the co. This Luhrig plant was supplied and
erected under contract by Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere,
506 Little Collins-street, Melbourne.*
* * » •
Zeehan is much interested just now in the
wonderful returns obtained by the Fahey tribute party
working the South Silver King block. They are taking
out ore at the rate of £7000 a month, and have a five
years tribute. To the west of them the Argent lode is
giving splendid yields to a Bull co., known as the Mount
Zeehan Tasmmia Silver Lead Mines, Ltd. This show has
paid one or two dividends to its London shareholders.
An off-shoot of the Mount Zeehan co. is the Zeehan
Montana -Mine, Ltd., which has been paying regular
dividends since 1894. South, and adjoining the two Bull
co.’s, is the Colonel North Mines and Railway Co., Ltd.,
on which a large amount of prospecting has been done.
This co. has lately acquired from the liquidator of the
Van Diemen’s Land Bank, the old Grubb’s mine with con-
centrating plant, and a system of tramways which cost
±15,000 to construct. Sufficient new capital has recently
been raised by the Colonel North Co. to enable it to start
work in the Grubb’s shaft, where tribute parties took out
£32,000 worth of ore down to 275ft. The Colonel North
Co. means to extend its tramways to the Comstock dis-
trict, where the lodes are either pure galena or pure zinc
blende.
* * * *
“How came you to get so much dirt in this
consignment of copper ore?” said the boss of a N.S.W.
smelting-works, recently. “Well, it was this way. I
was instructed to send not less than six tons before the
prospectus came out, and as I found it impossible to get
that quantity of ore out of the mine, I had to make the
balance up with dirt.”

The revenue returns for six provinces, so far as
their respective financial years have pone, are as follows.
Maoriland, as usual, is missing. The figures for Tasmania
represent a complete financial year, as that province closes
its annual accounts on 31st December. For the other
provinces they represent six months only, from 30th June
to 31st December :

compared with

Some tolerably solemn facts can be deduced
from these fisrures, despite the cheering circumstance
that all the provinces show an increase. In the case of
New South Wales the situation is this :
Half-year to Half-year to
31st December, 31st December,
1898. 1899.
Expenditure .. £4,909,049 £5,083,616
Revenue .. 4,676,982 4,763,709
Deficit .. .. £232,667 £319,907
The shortage for the half-year is therefore £87,240 more
than it was at this time last year ; the expenditure has
increased that much faster than the income, and there is
so much the more leeway to make up. And as the extra
item of war expenses can’t quite nil be charged to loans,
and there is therefore so much the larger a hill to meet
next half-year, the prospects are that there will he a fine
large deficit on 301 h June, 1900, and Early-Closing Bill
will fall into disgrace. The loan expenditure for the half-
year is £953,847, or £6 more than for the corresponding
half of 1898, so there is no improvement in that quarter.
Westralia’s revenue is still improving hand-
somely, hut the war expenditure may yet blight Forrest’s
hopes of getting clear of his deficit this year. South Aus-
tralia’s revenue has declined during the last quarter, and
allowing for extra expenditure incurred in shooting at
complete strangers in the Transvaal, it isn’t at all likely
that Harassed Holder will make ends meet this year.
Holder reckoned on a surplus of £195 for the year, and
there isn't much margin for unexpected wars there. The
Victorian revenue is also none too promising, though
Turner based his estimates on an expected decrease of
£222,617, and as there is no decrease thus far it is just
possible that the war bill will be paid without breaking
anything. But it will probably swallow up any chance of
further reducing the deficit this year.
The Tasmanian Treasurer estimated the year’s
'revenue at 1 946,780, and he has got almost within £3OOO
of it, so things are not very bad in that direction. And
without any allowance for Boer-shooting the year’s expen-
diture was put down at £856,600, so there was to be over
£90,000 to the good and the old deficit was to be reduced
to £166,000. The expensive killing of Braddon’s fellow-
creatures (a man like Braddon shouldn’t be allowed to
have fellow-creatures) will probably have somewhat dam-
aged this estimate, but no expenditure figures are avail-
able vet.
* * * *
For the first time since God-itnows-when Syd-
ney City Council has got through the year without a
deficit. The figures for 1899 were: Revenue, £197,181;
expenditure, £194,431 ; surplus, £2750.
* * * *
Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Works,
under the management of hard Fitzgibbon, produces
gome wonderful results. Among the most wonderful are
the hills sent in to unfortunate property-owners for the
sewering of their premises. One bill recently set forth
that the board’s humble but honest and industrious
bricklayer had spent 19£ days laying 112 bricks. And the
miserable house-owner had to pay. Nothing but the
dropping of 112 bricks on top of Fitzgibbon appears
likely to do any good.
* * • •
The Equitable Life Assurance Society of the
U.S. makes a preliminary report of its position, as at
31st December, 1899: Assurance in force, £218,759,000;
increase during 1899, £13,092,264. Assets, £58,333.333;
increase during 1899, £4,506,396. Surplus, £12,916,666 ;
increase during 1898, £997,814. The new business of 1899
exceeded that of the previous year by over £6,500,000,
and the new business at the Australian branches in 1899
exceeded the 1898 business by nearly £lOO,OOO.

The Royal Bank of Queensland shows another
solid increase in profits, the takings for the half-year,
ended 31st December, 1899, being t 8322. This compares
with some previous profits as follows :
June, 1894.. ..£6360 June, 1897 ..£7463
December, 1594 .. 6477 December, 1897 .. 7440
June, 1895 ... .. 5946 June. 1898 ... 7460
December, 1895 .. 6)33 December, 1898 .. 7579
June, 1896.. .. 6445 June, 1899 .. 7830
December, 1896 .. 6515 December, 1899 .. 8322
The profit is at the rate of £3 17s. 9d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders' funds. The usual dividend at 2} per
cent, per annum (£4815) and dividend dutv thereon ( £241)
absorb £5056. An addition of 13000 is made to the reserve-
fund, raising it to £46,000, so it has now got hack to the
exact point at. which it stood when the disaster of ’93struck
the institution. After paying the dividend and setting
aside £3OOO there remains £266, which increases the pro-
fit balance to £2595. During the half-year just ended
deposits have increased ahovit £50,000 (from £761,186 to
£810.684). The cash and State securities are £311,086,
equal to the. unusually solid reserve of 35 per cent, on all
liabilities to the public. One small side-issue is that the
paid-up capital is £385,157, whereas six months ago it
was £386,254. Nothing is said about any forfeiture of
shares, nor is anything said about what the money was
applied to. Presumably it went to write down some-
thing, but the balance-sheet might have explained
the circumstance. It is a steadily-improving
balance-sheet, and the Royal Bank’s constant pegging-
away at the rehabilitation of its reserve fund can’t be too
much commended, but the document might be more
explicit in places.

The National Trustees, Executors and Agency
Co. (Melbourne) presents a balance-sheet for the year
ended 31st October, 1899. The net profit for the year,
after allowing £l6 for depreciation of furniture, is £2102,
and the usual dividend at 8 per cent, per annum absorbs
£2400, leaving £2 to carry forward. This compares with
some previous figures as follows :
Net Profit. Dividend paid. Surplus.
1892 £4506 15 per cent. = £4500 £6
Deficit.
1893 £4442 15 „ = £4500 £5B
1894 £2813 10 „ =■ £3OOO £lB7
Surplus.
1895 £3OOl 10 „ - £3OOO £1
Deficit.
1896 £2986 10 „ - £3OOO £l4
1897 £2383 8 „ ** £2400 £l2
1898 £2390 8 „ £2400 £lO
Surplus.
1899 £2402 8 „ = £2400 £2
£24,923 £25,200 Deficit £272
The case, therefore, is briefly as follows: In five out of
the last eight years the National Trustees, Executors and
Agency Co. lias paid away more than its profits in
dividends. Taking the eight years, as a whole, it has
paid away £272 more than its profits in dividends. And
In all these bad times no visible provision has been made
for depreciation of assets apart from furniture. The
National Trustees has a reserve fund of £23,400, which
isn’t nearly large enough according to this writer’s ideas.
The necessity for Caesar's wife being above suspicion
wasn’t a circumstance to the same necessity in the case
of a trustee co., for nobodv is known to have left trust
money with Cajsar. The National, according to its old
foolish custom, lumps all its receipts as “ profit,
including interest, commission, &c., £6690.” If the
interest receipts were stated separately, it might
be possible to gather from them whether the
£24,575 of mortgages which form the largest asset were
in reasonably good health or not; the evidence is uncer-
tain, but still when assets yield 4 per cent, all round they
may be sound and fully worth the price set against them,
and when they yield 1 per cent, it is dead certain that
they are not. Owing to the National’s obscure and
blotted way of putting things, it is impossible to ascer-
tain whether its assets yield 4 or 1 per cent, all round on
their nominal value. Why the accounts are put in this
wav the management knows best. Apart from all other
questions, however, a management which consistently
spends all its profits in paying large dividends and makes
so little provision for contingencies doesn’t inspire
respect.

“W. McA.” : The action of your bui’ding
Society may be in accordance with its rules, in which case
you have no remedy. Not knowing the rules, we can’t
say. Even if there is no rule bearing on the case it is dif-
ficult to say for cer'ain that the society is acting illegally.
Probably no two judges would agree on the point. Your
only visible remedy is to sue the institution, and whether
you lost or won, your costs would almost certainly swallow
up the sum at dispute.

* * * *
“ Elle ” writes : The N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioners pleaded, in a recent test-case at Goulburn, that
the Govf. was being defrauded through people buying a
ticket for a station which was not their destination and
there obtaining a fresh ticket for the rest of the journey.
It seems curious that the two tickets should be cheaper
than one, but, in the inscrutable ways of railway-manage-
ment, such things are. By taking an excursion-ticket to
Goulburn (12s. Id.), then a ticket from Goulburn to
Gunning (3s. j, the trip from Sydney to Gunning and back
can be done for 15s. Id., second cla=s, while a return-
ticket (second class) from Sydney to Gunning is 325. 9d.—
a saving of 17s. Bd. bv breaking the journey. The Com-
missioners claim that the breaking of the journey is
fraud, but the public can’t be induced to see it in that
light.
* * • *
Of all Broken Hill companies the Sulphide Cor-
poration (B.H. Central) furnishes by far the most valu-
able data as to sulphide treatment. After it come Block
10, the British, and the B.H. Prop., co’s. in which J. Bull
is most interested ; and judging by the remarks which
the English investor occasionally makes about even these
ample reports, he would simply decline to consider the
bald statements with which Australian shareholders in
Block 14, the South, and one or two others are- supplied.
Taking the average weekly or fortnightly reports of the
latter group, the shareholder would probably conclude
that the huge concentrating mills erected on the mines
sort all the ore into two, or at the most three, classes—
concentrates, slimes, and tailings. As a matter of fact,
however, the product might be divided into a dozen
classes. The Sulphide Corporation makes five divisions ;
and this table shows what became of 102,133 tons ore,
worth 12'70z. silver, 18'4 per cent, lead, and 21 "5 per
cent, zinc., which was milled last half-year :
Tonnage. Proportion. Assay Value.
Silver. Lead. Zinc.
Concentrates 21,274 20 'S3 28'70z. 63'6% 9'9%
Middlings .. 21,105 20-66 9'2oz. 7'4% 28'9%
Slimes .. 8.590 B'4l 16'8oz. 20'9%’23'2%
Tailings .. 49,846 48'81 6'Boz. 4'4% 24%.
Zincs .. 1,318 1-29 12'8oz. 12'1% 31'7%
Concentrates are sent to the smelter ; all the other pro-
ducts call for a further treatment by concentration, except
sometimes in the case of slimes. It is in the re-treatment
of these “ secondary products,” as the mine-people call
them, that the co’s metallurgists have been mainly ex-
perimenting during the past year. F’or the further con-
centration of the middlings, jigs and vanners and Wilfley
tables are now employed. This particular co. has adopted
a jig, which has lately worked up the middlings to 12'8oz.
silver, 12'1 per cent.' lead, and 31'7 per cent. zinc. For
the further concentration of the tailings, magnetic sepa-
rators are now being used experimentally.
• * * •
The thing for a sulphide mining co. to aim at is
to increase the output of concentrates without sacri-
ficing their quality ; in other words, to get out more and
more of the silver and lead in the concentrates, leaving
the “secondary products” and r.he tailings poorer and
poorer in these metals. The recovery of lead by the Sul-
phide Corporation during the past three half-years has
been (beginning from the earliest term) 68'64 per cent.,
66'61 per cent., and 70'3 per cent. ; the recovery of silver
has also improved a little, though it is still under 46 per
cent, of the total contents. That is, of course, in the
concentrates; a good deal of the balance has been con-
tained in the zincs, middlings, &e., which have been sold ;
but when contained in these products the metals fetch
only a very small proportion of their value. And this
constant improvement in the recovery of the metals has
been accompanied by a gradual reduction in cost. From
3s. 6d. per ton (of ore) two years ago, the cost
of concentration has now been brought to between
2s. lid. and 3s. As against that, however, it ought to
be stated that, the wear and tear is greater now than it
was, and allowance for this is apparently not made in
these figures. Per ton of ore, however, this would be a
trifle only. Further, this improvement in two ways—in
cost and in recovery of metals—has been accomplished in
the face of a slight change for the worse in the ore : it has
become harder and more mixed with rhodonite. Also
by the improvement of ventilation and the greater use of
air-drilling machines the quantity of ore mined per shift
per man has been increased, the figures for the past three
half-years being I*9 tons, 2'16 tons, and 2'46 tons. And
at the smelting works (Cockle Creek, near Newcastle) the
cost of roasting and smelting has been brought down.
• » * •
Precisely the same movements are taking place
at all the B.H. mines—in measure greater or less accord-
ing to the brains and experience of the managements and
the local variation of the ores.
* • * •
Broken Hill co’s seize the occasion of the Ben-
digo miners’ lock-out to beat that district again for
“ hard-ground men.” There is something wrong some-
where. The Sulphide Corporation has as good a name as
any among workmen ; yet the manager says for a recent
half-year :—“ The number of men engaged has been 1220;
leaving and discharged, 1242 ; average number employed
(in the mine and mill), 764. So continuous and extensive
a movement in the personnel is very detrimental to satis-
factory working.” Apparently the conditions are not
good enough to draw first-class men, or if men of this
class are drawn the conditions are not good enough to
keep them, in view of the awful discomforts of Broken
Hill life.
• • • *
The prospectus of the Grassy Gully Mine, ap-
pears in this issue. It has a record of 54 tons of ore
crushed for an average return of 17oz. to the ton, and
though the tonnage is small, the results are excellent as
far as they go.
» * * *
New Chum Railway is adopting the only safe
plan of building up a reserve fund ; but most Bpndigo
co.’s are slow to follow suit, chiefly, no doubt, because
Bendigo holders have been such plucky call-payers
hitherto. And, so far, the co.’s that have adopted the
plan have not specified in their reports how their reserves
are kept—a most important point, of course.

What splendid call-payers some of these Bendigo
investors are ! United Whip and Jersey has made its
110th call without a break for the payment of a single
div., South Belle Vue its 90th, Eureka its 7Sth (mostly
sixpennies). Garden Gully Consolidated its 62nd. The
worst luck lias clung to the Eureka, which has been most
enterprisingly delving away to practically no purpose in
the neighborhood of 3000 ft., while next door the N.C.
Railway has been steadily div. paying.

Greater romances than the pegging of the Kal-
goorlie mines by Brookman there are none. Six years
ago W. G. Brookman left Adelaide at the head of the
Coolgardie Prospecting Syndicate, total capital £l5O.
Passages, camels, and rations left little by the time the
syndicate’s prospector reached Hannan’s. To cut a long
story short, that £l5O syndicate secured the Boulder,
Ivanhoe, Associated, Lake View Consols, Lake View East,
South, and Extended, Oroya, and other mines, aggregat-
ing in market value to-day £21,000,000. The Kalgoorlie
mines employ 6500 men and pay £28,000 weekly in wages
—mostly on the mines acquired by the Brookman syndi-
cate. Exclusive of alluvial, Kalgoorlie has produced
1,583,3220z5. ; and 725,1590z5., or nearly half the total
yield, has been produced in 10 months of this year. Kal-
goorlie alone is now producing more gold than any one of
the eastern provinces—it has already exceeded the Vic-
torian output this year, and is running the whole of the
Queensland yield very close. The dividends declared by
Kalgoorlie mines for the ten months of 1899 total
£1,560,000, and the total divs. to date are £2,708,000.
Kalgoorlie contributes over 60 per cent, of the total gold
output, of Westralia. Of individual mines here are some
figures Lake View Consols, yield 401,7140 z. ; divs.
£875,000 ; market value, £5,000,000. Boulder, 317,2570z5. ;
divs. £688,000 ; market value, £3,500,000. Ivanhoe,
177,5930z5. ; divs. £288,000 ; market value, £4,000,000.
Associated, 173,4390z5. ; divs. £225,000 ; market value,
£6,000,000. Brown Hill, 156,1510z5. ; market value,
£1,500,000. Horseshoe, 124,5990z5. ; market value,
£3,900,000. Perseverance, 77,9850z5. ; market value,
£2,625,000. The Brown Hill, Perseverance, and Horse-
shoe were not acquired by the W. G. Brookman syndi-
cate, but the Mint, Oroya, Bank of England, Lake View
South, Lake View Extended, View and Boulder Junction,
and the Brookman Boulder were taken up by Brookman.
Most of these mines are producing, although not yet div.- 1
paying, and stand on the market at £1,650,000. Thus
from £l5O has grown a div.-payment (exclusive of 1
bonuses) of £2,076,000, and a share-market value of
£21,000,000. Kalgoorlie’s total output this year will beat
the whole Westralian production for last year. The re-
duction plant of Kalgoorlie consists of 300 stamps, 10
Huntingdon mills, 12 cyanide plants of various capa-- 1
cities, two local sulphide plants (and more in course of
erection). Enormous quantities of sulphides and telluride
ores are sent to smelters at Fremantle, Port Pirie, Port
Adelaide, and Dapto. Since the dividend-tax came into
law last July Kalgoorlie has paid £32,000 in tax. The i
total output of Westralia for this year up to the end of
October is 1,365,3840 z. Of this Kalgoorlie has contri-
buted two-thirds ; and most of Kalgoorlie’s yield was
from the mines taken up by the obscure little syndicate
of ’93.

Between Nov. 1G (when it began to be very
doubtful whether Bnller would help Kruger to some ’99
Xmas pudding in Pretoria) and the close of the market
just before Xmas, the leading dozen Kalgoorlie stocks
slumped £8,528,671. How much of this ought to he
debited to a weakening of confidence in the sulphide
plants writer isn’t going to tell—for an excellent reason.
Lake View Consols toppled £1,825,000; Associateds,
£1,781,250; Boulders, £984,370. Lower-priced stocks
don’t show it so much ; but the Golden Link’s fall from
£3 ss. on Nov. 16 to £1 12s. 6d. on Dec. 21, and Hannan s
Oroyas’ plunk from £4 6s. 3d to f 2 6s. are enough to give
any investor a lot of tired feeling. Horseshoes have
stuck on as well as anything ; their thud only amounted
to £337,500—fr0m £l5 2s. 6d. to £l4.

Tas. statistics for the year just closed set the
value of mineral raised at close on a million and a-half —an
increase over 1898 of £679,565. If Tasmania held but half
its mines as a State asset, it could do away with all taxes.
But it doesn’t own a solitary mine ; doesn’t even own a
smelting plant. The latter would be about the best invest-
ment Tasmaniacould strike just now, but the hopeless poli-
ticians of the Land of No Desire wouldn’t dream of pro-'
posing Govt, interference with private enterprise. Tas.
is the happy hunting-ground of syndicates; foreigners
swallow the mineral wealth, while the majority of Tas-
manians swallow 6d.-a-yard barracouta, and help John
Bullion to bash Boers.

SOCIETY
“ A FAMILY AFFAIR .”
MISS MURPHY : “ Please, Sergeant, will you send
a policeman up to Mrs. Murphy’s, ’cause
young Mr. Murphy is fighting old Mr.
Murphy, and old Mr. Murphy is not as good
with his dookes as he used to he, and Mrs.
Murphy says if some one doesn’t come up
young Mr. Murphy will give old Mr. Murphy
a hiding.”
S.A.’s periodical murder-case invariably
happens along from the lone, forlorn, sandy
country on the Australian Bight. People kill
each other down there because they are sorry for
each other, and it is better to be dead than ,
alive on the Bight. The latest crime comes *
from the usual spot. A petty dispute
between two Hindoo hawkers (both
soured by much sand) at Denial Bay
ended in one of them having his
skull split open with an axe. A
coroner and jury toiled out into the
wilderness and took evidence with a
pencil on tea-oase paper ; the mur-
derer was chained up in the J.P.’s
barn ; and the local clergyman humanely
buried the body. The live Hindoo is a
fatalist who apparently swears by capi-
tal punishment, for his only remark
is—“ Gentleman bring rope and me
hang.” And he seems quite willing to
meet gentleman with rope.
* * * *
A horror that will probably keep
a good front place for many years is that
story of the unknown fiend who put a
three-months-old infant in a kerosene-
tin, and left it to fry to a horrible
death from flies and the summer sun-
glare on the plain near Bourke —one
of the hottest and most parched re-
gions in the N.S.W. interior. Even
among the many horrors of that
region, which is called “bush” be-
cause it is mostly bare dust, this one
sounds new.
* * * *
Shylock was completely outdone
by that woman who at Marylebone
(London) police-court, recently, brought
forward a boy, and asked the magistrate
whether, by the law of the land, she
could impound the urchin as a security for
debt. She had lent some money to his relatives,
and wished to use it as the purchase money of the
lad. The applicant seemed quite surprised that
she could not exchange a debt for flesh and blood,
and left the court declaring that the law is still
very defective.
• • * •
There was a touch of pathos in Buller’s
complaint—“ it is evident the Boers don’t admit
more than a tenth of their losses.” The enemy
who refuses to own up to his heaps of slain is even
more irritating than the friendly boxer who gasps
“I nearly caught it that time” when you have
just landed him what you thought was a “beauty”
in the eye.
* » * *
If young Australia wants to take unto
himself a wife, a mountain-battery at 200 yards
won’t stop him. Also, being a firm be-
liever in the honeymoon section of the
marriage custom, he has many schemes
for “gettin’ the few quid t’gether ”
—one of them is cattle-lifting. At
Maitland (N.S.W.) Quarter Ses-
sions, Judge Docker, in sen-
tencing a youth named Am-
brose Isaac Caban to 12
months (suspended under
F.O. Act), commented:
“He committed the crime
two days before the date
fixed for his wedding, so
that he was evidently try-
ing to raise the wind for
the honeymoon.” It was
not the first time such a
course had been adopted.
In the Western district
a short while back,
another Y.A. was ar-

Wellington (M.L.)
police-court had its barren
monotony broken, the
other day, by a compara-
tively cheerful episode. A
solicitor came in hur-
riedly to defend his own
especial drunk, cramming
his pipe into his coat pocket
as he entered —he had been
doing a smoke on the pave-
merit outside when a bobby rushed out to tell him
that his especial drunk was on. He was an elo-
quent solicitor, and as he stated his case in power-
ful language the bench hardly noticed at first that
a thin cloud of smoke was rising from him.
Presently, however, the smoke became more
obvious, and the bench sat petrified while a
blazing solicitor addressed it. Then suddenly the
legal authority stopped short, and hurriedly
mentioning an irrelevant place of one syllable, he
left his case and fled to extinguish himself at the
nearest water-tap. Later he came back, a wetter
and a sadder man, with both coat-tails burned off,
and resumed business.
Strangers at Hobart (Tas.) used to be
struck by an effigy on a pub. opposite the wharf,
representing Lord Rodney*, or Lord-knows-who,
waving a sword and cheering his men on into the
threepenny bar. This old land-mark has gone
and the premises are to be turned into a jam-
factory. Everything in Hobart is to be a jam-
factory. Possibly when Federation is accom-
plished the remnant of local legislators will meet
in Heathorn’s Hotel (where half the business is
done even now) and let the ’Ouse of Parliament
as a jam-factory.

possibility of Brassey falling into the Yarrain the
course of his progress from Prince’s Bridge to the
Sunbeam in a rowing boat
He has chosen this mode
of departure from Mel-
bourne himself. He is to
get into a rowing boat at
the bridge and be pulled
down to his yacht, which
will be moored down
stream ■at one of the
wharves. Can he do it
without falling out of the
boat, or tumbling as he
climbs into the Sunbeam ?
The Yarraisa disagreeable
stream to fall into, and
as the old man is going,
and can bore Melbourne
no more, it is not desired
that he shall perish. Any-
way, it is unreliably alleged
that the Victorian Govern-
ment has ordered a corps of
men to follow the vice-regal craft in another
boat ; provided with life-buoys and hooked poles.
* * * *
Jones, suspected of the Broadford (Vic.)
murder, has been a good deal of a mystery. First
of all, the police, to a certain extent, constructed
Jones out of vague rumors and pieces of rather
hazy information. Then they built up the des-
cription of Jones (if he existed) from more or less
uncertain evidence, and having done these things,
they set out on Jones’s track. The track was
somewhat dim, partly through effluxion of time,
and partly through the want of any absolute cer-
tainty that Jones was at the other end of it. In
a thickly-peopled country you always find tracks.
One policeman got within a mile of a traveller be-
lieved to be Jones, who was either flying from the
police or else just going somewhere. Then he was
led off on a blind scent to the Melbourne road,
and pursued another alleged Jones till that per-
son evaporated into thin air. Later, he came
back to the original track, and it was discovered
that the suspect (still mentally described as Jones)
had drunk a long beer at Kilmore and then
vanished, either by concealment or because he
had reached home and was having tea. There-
fore both of Jones had gone, and the force was
left rushing up and down main-roads and bye-
roads, and stopping large quantities of Jones at
random. The great trouble in such cases is that
the description supplied often proves to be a com-
pound of hazy recollections of three or four differ-
ent men, and the sum-total is that the suspect is a
tall dwarf with eyes of a light black and bald
curly hair. The average man’s want of definite
observation, combined with his anxiety to help
the police by telling them all he knows and things
he doesn’t know, has a great deal to answer for.

HIS CONQUEROR.
Oo m Paul Kruger
Sitting on his stoep,
Drinicing cups of coffee,
Face aglow with Hope.
“ They have brought their cannon,
Guns both great and small,
But we have our guns, too,
Here in the Transvaal.
“ They have besought their God to
Give to them His aid—
Joshua’s God is my God !”
Oom Paul Kruger said.
Up there rode a horseman,
In his hand he bore
Something neatly rolled up
Like a music-score.
“ What is this ? ” cried Kruger ;
Horseman’s visage pales—
“ Found it on a Lancer
Come from New South Wales.”
Kruger took and read it,
Face set like a stone ;
Saw it was a ballad,
Groaned a bitter groan.
Kruger took and read it,
Face like glue became —■
“ Sons of New Britannia”
Was the ballad’s name.
From the hand of Kruger
Fell his coffee-cup.
“ All is lost,” he murmured;
“ Boys, the game is up.
“ We can face their cannon,
Never flinch nor fly—
When they sool their bards on
We can only die.”
A PICTURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
"cneath the ivattle’s gold they stood,
And the pampas' waving plume,
n the satin and gloss of their wedding clothes —■
On their brows the stamp of doom.
'h, why drawn faces and mournf ul looks *
When all should be blithe and gay ?
Why are the bride and the bridesmaids sad —■
And the groom—on the wedding day ?
Have they heard of some dear friends lost afar
On the billowing, rolling sea —
Gone through the leaves to the sea-maids’ caves
And the doom that we all must dree ?
Has the fear of Famine o'erspread the land,
Or Plague with her poison breath ?
Do the nations perish like rotten sheep
In the clutch of a nameless Death ?
But see ! —the dark suspense is o’er,
Ho longer they look forlorn ;
They break into mirth and a laughing joy
As bright as a young Spring morn.
I know not what angel of light had come,
What shadow of dread had gone ;
But across the camera’s glittering eye
The camera-cap was drawn.
M.L.

* * * *
Dunedin (M.L.) had a lively time with
the war fever, when Sandy and Duncan and
Donald, excited by the bray of the bagpipes,
planked down money to the tune of =£3ooo. Next
day there was a further collection, when the
bawbee and the mite were thrown promiscuously
into a tarpaulin spread behind the railings in the
Octagon, so that if the donor repented after throw-
ing over his halfpenny, he couldn’t get it back with-
out publicly climbing over the fence and fighting the
custodians of the money. At the patriotic meet-
ing every speaker was allowed to wave the special
flag of his country over himself to the accompani-
ment of much popular joy, and when someone
stuck over the platform a notice that England
expected every man to do his duty, the originality
of the sentiment struck the public in a fresh place.
But Dunedin showed its own particular cloven
hoof once more when a man stood up and said he
was grieved to see wine and spirits going to the
Transvaal with the contingent ? Did he expect
bottles of beef-tea and arrowroot to keep hot all
through the war ?
* * * »
The Boer war up to date has produced
nothing to equal the heroism of “several English
ladies ” who died of the landslip at Amalfi the other
week. The cable reports that the local hotel, al-
though torn up by the roots, was not actually
swept away on the instant. The ladies got safely
disentangled from the ruins, but they subsequently
perished in a gallant attempt to go back and save
their jewellery. Ye gods ! Lovely woman would
pluck a diamond bracelet even from the cannon’s
mouth.
C * * *
London Evening News’s war barometer
is a toy wall, with a ladder planted against it and
a toy Tommy Atkins climbing up. Kruger is
at the top, and his facial expression is supposed to
become more and more wild as the Absent-minded
Beggar comes nearer. Owing to certain recent
slippings-down of the Beggar, however, Kruger’s
face-barometer kept set fair for a long time and a
horrid smile appeared on his leathern countenance.

War item from London Daily Mail :
When the Boers entered the town they went to the posi-
offiee with the intention of taking possession of it. They
were met by the postmistress, an Englishwoman, who
not only declined to turn over the post-office to them,
but ordered them off the premises, and dared them to
interfere with colonial property. The Boers were non-
plussed by the lady’s determined manner. They went
outside arid hoisted the Free State flag. The postmistress
pulled it down and ran up the Union Jack again. The
Boers stuck up a proclamation annexing the district.
The postmistress tore it down, and put up the Governor’s
proclamation against treason. The Boers finally retired,
and the latest accounts from Lad.vgrey state that .the
heroic woman is still in possession of the post-office.
This reads like a very good lie or a very bad
anomaly. For if it is true, why is it that the
large-booted Dutch farmers, who are said to have
so freely bumped and bruised women who offered
no provocation, didn’t boot this heroine off the
premises and then take possession ? To act up to
their alleged character they ought at least to have
removed her by her streaming back hair, or
poured water over her till she left, or nailed her
ear to a board and then taken away the board.
At a recent Sydney
religious function a certain
sanctimonious parson was
provided with a seat be-
tween two R.C. priests
—two jovial jokists, who
immediately proceeded
to “ pull the leg ” of the solemn shepherd.
One of them remarked that Cardinal Moran
was very strict. “ I asked him if I could
go to the races the other day, and he
said ‘No; I’ll be d d if you can.’” The
sky-pilot straightened up, and said, “Surely the
Cardinal never made use of such an expression ? ”
“ Oh, no ! I don’t think he could have said that,”
chipped in the other “praste,” with a wink to his
mate. “ You must have misunderstood him.
I’ve known ‘ Pat Moran ’ for four years, and,
drunk or sober, I’ve never yet heard him swear.”
* * * *
Mrs. Fraser, the hysterical and golden-
haired Melbourne lady who shot her husband and
then got out on bail to nurse the stoical gentleman
back to life, has burst forth in a new place. She
wants to cut her golden hair short, and put her
shapely limbs into boy’s clothes, and go as a spy
among the Boers, and her proposal was gravely
submitted to the authorities. The lady says that,
among other qualifications, she has never worn
corsets, and is 37 inches round her snow-white
circumference when measured underneath the
arms; also, when she takes a deep breath she can
expand considerably. The idea seems to be that
she will go as far as the Boer lines in her present
size and then take a deep breath and disguise her-
self by expanding. The callous M'Lean, how-
ever, has rejected the proposal. Treasurer Shiels,
who is also romantic and hysterical, and given to
expanding in the head if not in the chest, ought
to have looked favorably on the lady’s proposal,
but perhaps the stodgy element in the Cabinet
voted him down.
• * * *
The call for volunteers to help the Absent-
minded Beggars in South Africa was a godsend to
some Australian public servants who had been
lost for years in dingo townships on the plains
and had pleaded vainly for a removal to civilisa-
tion. They applied for temporary leave in order
that they might offer their services in the war,
and the Department didn’t know whether any
particular candidate was spavined, er a confirmed
roarer, or gone in the feet, or otherwise ineligible,
so it assumed that the enthusiasm was all genuine
and let them come. Some of the applicants were
genuine enough, but the fat and the hopeless
streamed down also, and were rejected, and spent
the rest of the time applying for a removal. And
some exiles who had previously applied for years
without any result were brilliantly successful,
especially when they lamented because they
weren’t going to the war, and said they wanted
to be close at hand so that they could apply again
when the next Contingent was made up. Of
course sundry public servants who hadn’t sense
enough to volunteer have gone Out Back in their
places, but that doesn’t matter. The man who
doesn’t want to go on the shoot is a very small
person just now.

Thus Emily Soldene in her letter from
London to Sydney Evening News
The blood of the nation is up. We don’t get much
news, hut some of it is toothsome. What particularly
pleases me, and I’m not a bloodthirsty person, is the
bayonet charges. The Boer don’t like cold steel. He
like to skulk behind a stone or an anthill, and snipe our
boys. When the gleam of the broad, bright, sharp-
pointed bayonet falls across his eyes he tumbles on his
knees and howls for mercy.
I find this distinctly refreshing. “We went through
them again and again, We bayonetted eighty of them
all of a heap.”
That’s what pleases me. I can hear the impact—the
shriek of despair—the slide—the crash—the crackle. Then
the sword practice has not been bad.
Emily is not bloodthirsty ; she only likes to hear
the slide and the crackle.
The hen picnic is quite a usual festival
now among Melbourne girls, but it is not a
success, partly because a limp young man with a
nice tie and an expression of adoration is. essential
to the average girl’s happiness at a picnic, but
mainly because things generally go wrong. They
went wronger than usual with a girl-excursion
that landed somewhere in the bush near
Lilydale one day last week. They were
an awfully nice lot of girls, and even
the chaperone was young and lovely,
but when they were all grouped
around their first at fresco feed, a
horrid man in old clothes and having
a bad expression, chanced along, and,
after casting a gluttonous eye over the
meat and drink, simply entered into
possession. He drove the girls off in a
flock, and settled down on the comestibles
like a plague. When a timid creature ven-
tured near to expostulate, he grinned at
her with a malignant labored grin, and
shook his club, and the timid creature fled
screaming. The picnic stood round in a
circle at a respectful distance, and watched
the low, coarse brute tearing its pullets and
lapping up its elegant jellies, and resolved
with tears never to picnic in the leafy soli-
tudes again without the moral support of
at least one gigantic man. When the
unbidden guest had filled himself with
edibles, he gathered all the wine about him,
and went on a jag lasting two hours, after
which he fell amongst the ruins of the
meal, and slept. Then the girls gathered
up their belongings, harnessed their horses,
and fled from the bosky dell back to the
haunts of men.
The crop of New Year “honours” was
very poor this year. Not one degradation came
to Queensland, N.S.W., or Maoriland. Judge
Dodds, of Tasmania, and Mayor M’Eacharn, of
Melbourne, were made the very cheapest kind
of knights (the K.B. variety not even the
K.C.M.G.). Speaker James Lee-Steere, of W.A.,
who was a knight already, was made another kind
of knight, and Agent-General Cockburn, of S.A.,
got a small handle —a kind of pot-handle. Not
one politician in active service got anything,

The bushranging renaissance atßutherglen
(Vic.) has so far been a b lg success. The rangers
got away with £l5OO odd—half in negotiable cash,
and the rest in livers, easily changed at any big
race meeting. Two young men have been arrested
in Collingwood, but, although they were in the
vicinity of the robbery, the police will apparently
have their hands full to prove any more. Any-
how, the man who rises to bushranging on a
decent scale deserves credit, if not success. Few
crooked persons have an idea above second-hand
clothing or door-mats, and are paralysed at the
idea of taking £5 all at once. The robber of
thousands is nearly always an oily, underhand
person in a long black coat and a silk hat, and
when you find one wearing moleskins, and doing
his brigandage openly, he should be respected for
his originality. The Vic. police have found no
trace of any of the stolen money, yet all Riverina
has been busy prospecting the route of the alleged
robbery for the past fortnight^
Young Australia has little reverence for
old world traditions, even when he believes them.
A tale comes from Cardigan, near Ballarat, of a
batch of small boys who went missing at midnight
on Christmas eve, and after a search were dis-
covered, armed with shot-guns, lurking on the
roof of a large house occupied by the parents of
one of the marauders. It took many threats and
a yard of trace leather to get the truth out of the
ringleader, but at length he explained that for
two years Banta Claus had not brought anything
to three of their number, and their intention was
“to stick the beggar up as he was going down
the chimney of a favored house, and get away
with the whole of his stock.

Queensland Police Commissioners heave
the following joke at the public’s head :
The fact that so few of the Brisbane public came for-
ward to prefer charges against the police in their indivi-
dual characters warrants us to conclude that, generally
speaking, the police behave with circumspection in their
relations to the public.
True! The class of men abused by the police
have mighty little chance of a square deal in case
they prefer a charge against our old friend, Con-
stable X. The average policeman knows his
mark fairly well, and seldom kicks a disorderly
city magnate to any great extent, or thrashes a
drunken M.L.A. in the cells. Occasionally he
makes a mistake, as in the recent case of Scott
(the well-known Brisbane athlete and son of a
prominent civil-servant), who lost his eye,'the
other day, during a short dispute with a vigorous
constable. But, as a rule, he picks his victim
with a great deal of the above-mentioned circum-
spection.

suicide. It was just about 12 months from the
famous Murphy tragedy, and crime tends to run

Already Britain appears to be getting into
the hysterical condition of Paris at the Singe,
when the more or less imaginary German spy was
everywhere, and had a bad time when caught.
Recently two recruits who attempted to join a
British regiment were diagnosed l>y Tommy as
the hidden agents of Kruger, and almost mur-
dered on the spot. Probably they were Germans.
The desire to batter Queen Victoria’s country-
men is very strong just now.
Mrs. Dr. Fraser, the St. Ivilda (Melb.)
markswoman, is eager to exercise her soft blan-
dishments upon the Boers as a spy or scout on
behalf of the Australian contingent, or “on her
own.” Mrs. F. must remember a woman’s
success is generally in proportion to the seductive-
ness of her charms, ami as P.D. corsets bestow the
greatest charm, obviously the first duty of the
enterprising lady should be to provide herself with
a full equipment of P.Ds.*
“The light that (never) failed,” is without
doubt given by the " Albatross Lamp Wick.” Keep the
reservoir filled and all your work is over. “ It requires
no I rimming,” emits no offensive odour, gives a more
brilliant light, saves your oil, and unless your lamp has
some material defect, it is impossible for it to explode.*
An official denial of the rumor that N.S. W.
Govt, had agreed to pay Drs. Scot-Skirving and
M'Connick vast sums for volunteering to the
Transvaal was, it seems, really needed. Sawbones
innumerable were writing and wiring from all
quarters offering their services at “reasonable
rates,” and several hundred copies of diplomas
were dumped into the Col. Secretary’s Depart-
ment.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 8, 1900.
My Dear Myee,—
His Lx. is off on his travels again. The cables
never said a word about an exploring turn of
mind, and Sydney has had to grow up to the fact
that H.E. acids to his other dazzling qualities the
talent of acting the “Man who was Away.” In
his eight months of office he has inspected more
backblock architectural structures, brilliantly lit
with kerosene hanging from the cast-iron roof,
than any other Gov. up to date. At present the
vice-regal wanderer is getting impressions of Nor-
folk Island, accompanied by the genial Mr. B. R.
Wise and the only aide left him.
Society is mostly away at Bowral just now. It
seems that the Mountains are a shade less toppy
than they used to be. Everyone with a reputation
to keep up makes for Bowral, and the only fashion-
able event of the time-being happened up there
last week—the Hospital Bazaar, opened by the
Gov., and carried out by all the smartest women.
It has been quite an excitement this week,
getting sold when you went buying. On Wednes-
day the usual rush of suburban people went to
shop at the Universal Providery, and found the
doors locked, the shop deserted, and the Early
Closing Act in full swing.
To shoppers the difficulty is to know who will
be open on Wednesday afternoon or who shut.
One smart drapery haunt in King-street closes at
1 on Wednesdays and keeps open till 10 on Satur-
days. All the others round about are open till 6
every day. When we get to know their ways by
heart it will be all right, but at present you might
strike twenty shops wrongly on Wednesday after-
noon before you could get a pair of shoes.
But when you think of those hundreds of
ansemic shop-girls taking an afternoon off every
week of their lives henceforth, sleeping, lazing,
picnicking all those hours that used to be spent in
bending before fat women, and squeezing the
large fat feet of other people into little boots, you
are forgiven for feeling the same {esthetic thrill
that goes through you when a big man comes up
and kicks the man who is heaving metal at a lean
dog.
The only wedding I know of this month is Miss
Slattery’s. That comes olf in a fortnight at St.
Mary’s Cathedral, an early morning wedding,
eventuating at!) a.m.
The “Absent-minded Beggar” has arrived.
Dolly Vane introduces him into the
Panto, rather brusquely in my opinion. “Ah,
kind ladies, have you seen my boy, he has gone
away and left me. Ah, well, he always was an
Absent-minded Beggar.” That’s how she
tacks it on to Red Riding Hood. Then she
rattles her tambourine, and sings with all
the vim she possesses till her face breaks into
moisture. She wears her little pink and white
costume of the Land of Arcadia scene, ])ink
brocade polonaise over white satin petticoat, lace
falling from her elbow sleeves, and pink slioesand
stockings. A good deal of small money is thrown
after each verse, chiefly pennies ; for the first few
nights, at all events, it averaged £'A a night.
Everybody knows the dashing words, as character-
istically Kiplingesque as any Kipling ever wrote.
As for the music—Arthur Sullivan’s—it isn’t any-
thing to rave about, catchy, but more common-
place than you expect.
A second visit to the Pantomime found things
going gaily there. “ Red Riding Hood ” is having
a big success. It x was the special Saturday
Matinee—the Panto, bills two matinees a week
now—and the house was crowded with small
people whose comments often rival the text. For
instance, a little boy in the d.c. sat next to his fat
mamma. A thin lady in pink satin came out and
turned six somersaults backwards. “ Oh, can-you-
do-zat. Mummy ?” cried a shrill little voice that
carried all over the house. Everyone turned round
hastily and beheld Mummy endeavoring to con-
ceal her matter of twenty stone behind a six-inch
handkerchief.
On Saturday night, “David Garrick ” revived
at the Criterion with Walter Bentley in the name
part. Bentley airs rather an elderiy expression,
and the orthodox picturesque plum-colored
clothes, with velvet cape and big boots. Miss
Beatrice Dunckley, a Melbourne girl, dressed Ada
Ingot as if she were covering a piece of furniture.
In the first act there was a baggy pink muslin robe,
the hair curling on the shoulders, and filleted
with pink ribbon. In the dinner-party scene
she wore white satin tied up far too care-
fully with far too much wide blue ribbon. The
pretty frilly clingingness that always characterises
Ada Ingot’s dressing was found wanting, and a
slight “flnffiness” in her lines was thrown in in-
stead. On the whole, Miss Dunckley looked
rather well and acted promisingly, and, if she
would substitute even a simple white muslin for
the blue-and-white satin, the d.c. would think
more of her acting.
A great point in favor of this production is that
nobody wails. Miss Fanny Wiseman’s Araminta
Brown, in yellow cashmere, with a red frill round
the lower edge, and a red cockatoo in her hair, is
played chippily and neatly. Miss Ethel Hunt, as
Mrs. Smith, certainly leaves out all the large, fat,
comfortable aitchlessness of the mother of seven-
teen, but those weeping inflexions that are all the
wicked vogue just now among actresses clo not
blot the fair surface of “ David Garrick.’’
Why does the actress wail? Why, I mean,
when there’s nothing to wail at ? Ada Ferrar and
Maud Jeffries did this moanful style to death. It
was positive torture sometimes to get their
monotonous tearful tones ringing in your ear.
And yet half the actresses in Sj'dney are frantic-
ally chasing the mannerism, pulling out the tcar-
stop, and elocuting as if tlvere was a large boil at
the back of the soul. The most aggravated case is
Alice Deorwyn, in the “ Power and the Glory,”
followed very close by Ada Gresham. I notice
sorrowfully that Carrie Moore is dropping into
throatyism lately in her speaking voice. Henri-
etta Watson was blessedly free from it —more free,
perhaps, than any actress heard in Sydney for a
long day.
At “David Garrick” some inquisitive being
looked at the play bill and said, “ Who’s the play
by?” The play-bill named no author. The
query passed along the row. “ Who wrote ‘ David
Garrick’?” Everybody knew perfectly well,
don’t you know, but couldn’t remember for the
moment. Someone said it was by T. W. Robert-
son, and that was the time when everybody was
quite certain of one thin"—that ‘David Garrick ’
was not by Robertson. Is that one of the disad-
vantages of writing a too-successful play ? The
play lives ; the author dies.
In new music, there is Allan’s (Allan and Co.,
Melbourne) “ Musical Magazine,” a good shillings-
wortli of all sorts and conditions of song and
dance, including a piece called “Can it be Love?”
by Clarence Clare, that makes one waltz out of
Kenningham’s two songs, “My Darling” and
“ Can it be Love ? ” with four others thrown in.
By the way, there is only one tune in the Eng-
lish-speaking world just now. It is on everybody’s
lips, in everybody’s ears—“ Soldiers of the Queen,”
by Leslie Stuart. There is always a band playing
it somewhere, a boy whistling it, or a girl hum-
ming it; every patriotic meeting is flooded with
it; soldiers inarch away to it; the suburban villa
plays and sings it in the evening; and as long as
any of us remember the Boer war we will hear
that haunting, stirring march-song which even
threatens to swamp the great “God Save.”
The latest thing in Sydney sights has the quality
of novelty, anyway. A pretty wax woman sits at a
sewing-machine in a shop-window. She wears a
smart blouse and skirt, golden hair curling under
a net, and natty little patent-leather shoes. Her
wax feet inside the shoes rest on the pedal.
Her wax hands are on the machine, which works
mechanically, and all the day long she sits there
watching a piece of silk run under the needle, and
work its way back, and run under it again. I
was fascinated with the clever way in which the
moulder had caught the true worried dressmaker
look, and fixed it into the wax about the corners
of her mouth. All day long a crowd watches this
wax lady, and wonders why real flesh and blood
has to be offerei! up for the machine-god’s sacrifice
when wax seems to do so well.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“New Year’s Day, the date of Gov. Brassey’s
last appearance at Flemington, was the thirstiest
weather on record. If Skinner’s non-intoxicants
had run out, and an angel from Heaven had
chanced to drop down on the grand-stand with an
empty nectar bag, you bet she’d have drunk what
there was, and leaned a swelled head on her golden
harp next day. The Ancient Mariner, all in white,
looked a clean, wee gnome, with his cutty jacket
and pants and his big brolly, as he peeped out
from under a huge helmet. When not talking, he
dozed. Then he started from his dreams as if a
burning-glass had scorched him in the eye, when a
woman came by in scarlet blouse, poppy-laden
hat, and white skirt, and stood flaring in front of
the vice-regal pen, as if challenging the brazen
sky to out-dazzle her surface.
“ Aide Nevill and his employer had apparently
dressed with a view to contrast. The junior
looked phenomenally long, lank and sombre in
loose black alpaca and white gamp. Black
alpaca, somehow, seems associated with prunella
boots, white stockings, and bush parsons. Nothing
weird about the rest of the gubernatorial group.
The Governoress wore white muslin over blue and
black, and a crinoline hat. Attaches Pakenham
and Hindlip, dressing for coolness, had yet drawn
the line at sensationalism. The first-mentioned,
the cheeriest and ehirpiest of the Brassey staff, re-
joins his regiment in Africa almost immediately.
Lady Hopetoun’s brother, sugar - planter De
Moleyns, from Bananaland, is a tall, dark, good-
looking man, shy and rather short-nosed like his
sister.
“Nothing startling in recent race clothes.
Mdlle. Dreyfus, at New Year meeting, was chic in
corn-flower blue with biscuit-lace facings, pearl
necklet, white veil, and court patches. Another
Frenchwoman to the fore was the Consul s
daughter in white Eton suit, pink waistlet, and
red parasol. Daughters of Gaul are strong in
animation. When the lively demoiselle just
referred to was a school-girl, she was homely in
comparison with locally-produced spring chickens,
what with her dumpy figure, dark skin, lank
hair and irregular features. Now she’s a vivacious
chatter-box, able to hold her own with most.
What about these -timid French girls one
hears of, never off mamma’s apron-strings before
marriage? The local type is attractive but not
traditional.
“ Christmas—not usually a very riotous time in
Melb. —was from some unknown cause exception-
ally quiet this year. The one person who spent a
really merry Xmas was a small boy of unregener-
ate turn of mind and evil propensities. Said
youth, on Saturday evening, having sold his
Hkrai.ps, was leaning against a verandah-post in
Swanston-street, apparently meditating. The
verandah-post, in front of a fruit shop, was decor-
ated with a good-sized sapling far-out over the
street and tied merely by a piece of string. This
the boy cut, holding tight to the bush until tiie
psychological moment arrived for letting go. It
came —when a cyclist and cydiste were about
twenty yards off, close to the sidewalk. They
promptly rode into the falling shrub, and col-
lapsed. Then a passing cabby—in trying to avoid
the wheelers—drove right across the street into a
tram, upsetting a fruit-barrow on the way, and a
buggy-horse shied violently down Little Collins
Street until he tangled himself in a lamp-post.
All these people came back and spoke firmly to
the shop-keeper. Meanwhile the small boy looked
on, smoking a cigarette, and when it was all over
sauntered away with a bored air, remarking that,
after all, it wasn’t worth the bother.
“ The naiee people have not yet left town.
They slept the Xmas sleep of indigestion under
their own painted roofs, and are only now pre-
paring for the summer jaunts. Some, like Mrs.
George Chirnside, are remaining in town to feast
Uncle Tom ere that elderly fairy floats away on
his Sunbeam. It seems a most unnecessary act
of self-sacrifice on her part, but Mrs. G. C., bear
in mind, is one of the little band of matrons for
whom “ Uncle ” had always a winning smile—-that
is to say, as winning as he knew how to make it.
“Toys, sweets, &c., are so cheap and delightful
nowadays that grown-ups pity their own less-
favored childhood. Once upon a time, a small
girl saved, pinched, and scraped for a tea-set, with
‘real large cups.’ Finally, at a cost of 7s. Gd.,
she obtained her heart’s desire in the plainest
white-stone china. Large, lovely sets, in gaudy
boxes, go for Is. Gd. these times. In the long-ago,
the possession of a rocking-horse set a youngster
on a pedestal, to be courted and envied by his
fellows. Five pounds bought a wooden moke, in
remote districts, and the publican's son generally
owned the only specimen in the township.
‘Never mind, dearies!’ sympathetic mothers
would say, as their own darlings loitered and
■glared greedily into the hotel verandah, in pass-
ing. ‘Perhaps some tipsy man gave it to him !’
The result of this cold comfort appeared later on,
when Tommy got a ss. ‘ hobby-horse ’ for his
birthday, and said, wistfully, ‘I wish father got
tipsy ! ’ A noble breed of toy gee-gees is obtain-
able at present from 17s. upwards, but the simple
little fellow of to-day despises mimic horseflesh,
preferring the bike.
“Melbourne matrons are given to wearing
much conspicuous jewellery. Janet Lady Clarke
is burdened with an enormous gold heart, like the
emblem worn by religious sisters. Chief-Justicess
Madden wears as a brooch a large gold medallion,
with cameo setting, enclosing the enamelled
portrait of the beauty of the family. Mrs. Frank
Madden is loaded with an enormous cameo brooch.
Younger and friskier dames tinkle with all manner
of small trinkets, bracelet watches, belt brooches,
and other plate and harness.
“Srupert Clarke has not been keeping up the
interestin military affairs which his father affected,
by taking an active part in connection with the
war fund. One good reason is that nobody knows
exactly where he is. The genial young Part is
travelling somewhere in China —in Central China
it is supposed, but his whereabouts is indefinite.
Meanwhile, George Morrison, the brilliant (Lon-
don) Times correspondent in Pekin, is in Mel-
bourne on a visit to his brother-in-law, Henry
Higgins, M.P., and his numerous other relations.
He has been visiting England and is now again
bent China-wards.
“The death of Robert Chirnside. eldest of the
second generation of the wealthy clan, throws a
large family circle into crape and mourning. He
had always suffered from a weak chest, and his
devoted wife spent the last few years nursing him,
in the quiet of station life at Carranballac, Ship-
ton, or in various health resorts. Just before
Christmas they set out for Bendigo, and it was at
the Shamrock Hotel that the weary invalid died.
“ Wide-awake old Sydney has euchred us again.
Despite repeated warnings, local laggards in love
have let Miss Stanbridge, the Daylesford heiress,
slip through their fingers. She is to be wedded
very shortly to Mr. H. M. O. Cox, of Hawden,
(Rylstone, N.S. W.).
“Unmanly conduct on the part of a young
woman in a Melbourne suburb provokes these few
remarks. One young lady in the dressmaking
interest answered a matrimonial advertisement,
‘just for fun,’ and the fun went as far as the
fixing up of an appointment. At this stage the
little seamstress took a friend into her confidence,
and the friend took a high moral tone, and
urgently advised her not to keep the appointment.
The small sewing woman was so disturbed by the
moral tone that she was late in going to meet the
young gentleman ‘with a view to the above’ —she
got there, in fact, just in time to discover her
false friend getting into a cab with the prize.
There was a wild and woolly time when next they
met —the circumstance was even described as
‘assault,’ but that did not defer the wedding.
The friend married him, and he actually has a
good billet and some money. When you consider
that only about one in two hundred men who ad-
vertise ‘with a view to the above’ has the
slightest intention of the above, and that one
usually has no cash, you will admit that this is a
really hard case.
“’Tis rumoured that a moneyed belle, whoso
engagement is just announced, met her present
vassal aboard ship when her troth was still
plighted to a moro previous wooer. At that
juncture, there was nothing for the stricken
fellow-passenger to do but go nurse his grief by
India’s coral strand. Later, when the previous
person liad been shoo’d off the grass, he returned
from the plague-stricken land, and took possession
of the niche vacated in his idol’s fancy by the late
Crushed One aforesaid.
“Captain Ilaigh, aide to Cov. Jerry Smith, of
Westralia, went to the war per same boat as the
Australian contingents. Fellow-travellers report
him just the same big, rollicking, red-faced Irish-
man as ever. He was the life and soul of the
sports on board, and never looked sad except
when he, not seldom, lamented that the pretty
nurses volunteering for the front had not all come
by that boat.
“Two or three friends wrote me from London
in November that they were already heartily sick
of ‘The Absent-minded Beggar.’ The jingo verses
had been sung, recited, tableau’d, quoted, and
shillinged in all directions until they couldn’t gaze
on the letter K without loathing. And then, last
and worst of all, came a 4-act melodrama, ‘ The
Absent-minded Beggar; or, for Queen and
Country,’ which broke out at the Princess’s, a
‘respectable middle-class’ theatre in Oxford-st.
The heroine of this up-to-date agony was hand-
some Lillah McCarthy, who came to Australia as
next-to-leading lady in the Wilson-Barrett co. and
didn’t altogether agree with Maud. Jeffries’con-
stitution, seemingly.

“ Bendigo’s fame has been its own undoing.
There is a ‘coming ’ warbler in Melbourne who is
actually ashamed to own up to being a Ben-
digonian. She has gone round to all her friends and
asked them to keep her birthplace a dead secret.
People are weary of Bendigo singers.
