appear as extras on the bill.
“ Melb. Women’s Hospital has gained such evil
notoriety among medical practitioners that only
one application was received for the appointment
of a resident surgeon. An extension of time and
further advertisement brought the same candi-
date, but no more, and the appointment was made
in dead silence. After the treatment of l)r.
Crowley, the masterly inactivity when a patient
was allowed to die on the mat, and the painfully
moral attitude of the committee towards un-
married mothers, people fight shy of what was
once a favorite institution.

“Don’t drink np all the whisky,” was the Boer
request delivered to the Marfeking garrison in a hollow
bombshell, hut the full significance of that message didn’t
appear in the cable news. The Boers know that the
whisky stored under Col. Baden Powell’s direction pre-
vious to the war was “ D.C.L.” Recognising the stern
necessity for keeping his garrison in good spirits, the
gallant strategist decided to stand or fall by the “D.C.L.”
brand. On the 20th November or thereabouts, Powell
sent a case of the famous Scotch whisky (black bottle) to
the disheartened enemy, since when they have many
times renewed the attack with their tongues hanging out.*

Judge Hartley Williams (Vic.) who is
going on twelve months’ leave again, is not likely
to come back from England. He’s lame, and is
getting old, and has never been in good humor
since Madden was made C.J. over his head.
A cable report comes along to the effect
that that very lamentable poet, the Marquis of
Lome, is to be the first Governor-General of
Federated Australia. This country already suffers
the heat and burden of sewer-bard Fitzgibbon
and Lome is, to all intents, another Fitzgibbon.
Barrister Weigall (Melb.) is going to
Europe for six months with his wife (daughter of
late Governor Hamilton, of Tasmania). Weigall s
father-in-law was the most popular Secretary lor
Ireland ever appointed. Like Red vers Buller, his
stay in the country made him a Home Ruler.
Then he was kicked upstairs and made a Gover-
nor, for being too beastly honest.
Agent-General Cockburn’s knighthood was
received in grim silence in S.A.. When the
Radical doctor headed the progressive party in
S.A. he was a deadly opponent of titles, and once
actually objected to the prefix “Hon.” before his
name. It will always stand to Cockburn s credit,
however, that he invariably voted on the side of
the people. But that was the old Cockburn.
Rev. Fitchett, Apostle of Berlud, back
from Europe, tells an interviewer that, among
other offers of literary work, he had one from a
leading firm of publishers to write a volume on
“ The Reverses of the British Army.” But the
good man was horrified ; he couldn’t think of
writing of the nation’s reverses. And as many
other alleged historians feel the same way, history
as she is perpetrated in books is often a quaint
institution.

Rev. Sam. Wilkinson, buried the other
day in Rookwood Cemetery, was the last sur-
vivor (he died at 86) of the historic little band of
missionaries who laid the foundations of Wes-
leyanism in Australia. He officiated all over the
continent, and was one of those present when
Gov. Bourke directed the laying out of Melbourne
according to surveyor Russell’s plans.
Theodore Fink got overboard in such
haste from the leaky craft of the new Victorian
Ministry that he probably spoiled his own future
chances. The M‘Lean Government may be hope-
less, but even in the brief career of a hopeless
Government a man may achieve something which
will mark him out for future office. Fink got out
so hurriedly that he hadn’t a chance to distinguish
himself in any way. A man may make a useful
name while fighting a lost battle, but he can’t
make much by running away the day before the
battle.
Sudden Solomon, S.A.’s very temporary
Premier, is reputed to dye his brain thatch. “ The
hon. gentleman changes his opinions once a year,’
once said the Sudden One to Kingston. “ The
hon. gentleman,” replied Kingston, “changes the
color of his hair every morning.” It wasn’t a very
brilliant retort, but it might pass on the spur of
the moment. If only sneers and interjections had
to be pub on the notice-paper, like questions, so
that the defendant could have time to work up his
reply, Parliament would be a lot more brilliant
than it is.
Curtain, a former member for Carlton, in
Vic. Assembly, once struck a responsive chord in
the breasts of his hearers when he said : “ We are
all tending towards the same gaol.” Perhaps he
meant “goal,” but it was in the boom days, and
an immense proportion of Melbourne people were
tending towards gaol—or should have been.
John Nicholls, or “John the Cashman,”
dead the other day, was one of the oldest publi-
cans in N.S.W., and with the late James T.
Fallon bought, in the early Fifties, from a black-
fellow the famous basket of gold specimens from
what was afterwards known as the Black Range
Goldfield, near Albury. They gave <£soo for the
basket and spent a hundred times that in develop-
ing the Black Range. But Nicholls made more
out of hotels, and Fallon out of wine, than either
made out of the Range, after all. Nicholls was
73 at death.
The Boer army has no uniform—only its
brown suit and its slouch hat and its gun. Old
veteran General de Kock, who fell in one of the
early battles, went to the war in the black tail
coat and the tall black hat that he wore at church
on Sundays. His bulky belltopper blazed all
day in the forefront of the battle till at dusk the
Absent-Minded Beggars brought him down with
three mortal wounds. Then when the hill was
carried by storm, they found what looked like an
aged Methodist preacher dying in a mud-hole
beside a crippled gun and a big pile of dead.
Almost the first telegram of congratulation
which came to Premier Seddon after the M.L.
election, was a condensed yell in three words :
“Hooray, hooroo, hooroosh.” Two others were
about equally brief: “ Good old Dick,” said one,
and the other “Long may you wave.” But
19-stone Seddon is almost too heavy to wave
now. A Maori supporter of George Fisher was so
excited over his small tin god’s return, that he
rushed to a newspaper office and put in an ad vt. :
“To my frien Fisher—you wonder, no eomitee,
no talk, no noting, win heesee, me your flien.”

Chief-Justice “ Johnnie ” Dodds, of Tas.,
has only got a K. 8., and the thing loses half its
value by having nothing to show for it on the
neck and breast. Knight Bachelor is the lowest
order of knighthood —and that’s saying a lot.
It’s generally given to men who haven’t earned
it —which is also saying a lot, for few men can
avoid earning a thing that comes so easily. The
office is knighted, not the man.

John Foster Yesey Fitzgerald (or John
Foster, as he is known in Australian political his-
tory), whose death at 81 is announced, was the
only Colonial Secretary of the Australian group,
who, when a change of Constitution was at hand,
voluntarily resigned his office and thus forfeited
a life pension of <£,>oo a year. Foster was
Vic. Col.-Secretary at time of Ballarat riots.
There would have been no riots if his conciliatory
policy had not been reversed by Governor Hotham,
yet somehow public opinion held Foster largely
responsible for that unhappy episode. He was
also Treasurer in O’Shan,assy’s seven-weeks’
Ministry in ’57, which was practically his last ap-
pearance in Australian politics.
A political amenity from Wellington
(M.L.) Evening Post
T> G. KNIGHT
Is a Gagged Political Victim,
NOT A LIAB.
GERTRUDE S. KNIGHT.
Knight, a sailmaker, contested one of the Wel-
lington suburban seats at the recent election. He
came out at the foot of the poll, and lost his de-
posit. Also, he lost his temper and called John
Hutcheson (“Jack The Rigger ”) various animals,
wherefore there was an action for libel, and an
arbitration, resulting in Hutcheson’s favor. Now
his loving wife backs up her oppressed old man, as
a loving wife should.
Josiah Thomas, who, when Premier Lyne
brought down his second contingent proposal,
stood up almost alone to say that the war was no
less disgraceful and unjustifiable than ever it was,
is, curiously enough, about the most typical
Englishman in N.S.W. Assembly. An assayer
and mine-manager fresh from England, he took
the miners’ side in the ’92 strike at Broken Hill,
and, though the least combative of men, was
pushed to the front. Several times it has been
locally suggested that he should be dropped (he
represents the most uncompromising Barrier
Labor constituency) in favour of a louder and
hornier-handed person; but his courage and
honesty have always saved him, and he now
holds one of the safest seats in N.S.W.
A reminiscence of Windeyer : Some years
ago, when he was travelling up North,
his party found themselves having a
<<, late dinner in a dining-room with
doors that squeaked horribly.
The Judge’s bedroom opened off
the dining-room, and the
Judge stood aghast at the
thought of those dreadful
doors squeaking in that
hair-raising way half the
night and early in the
morning. With his
mouth full of cold
mutton and pickles
he shook his head
sadly and said, “I’m
sure we’ll get no
sleep to - night ! ”
Suddenly his clouded
countenance cleared,
and he asked the
girl if they had
p enty of butter in
the house. The
girl assured him
they had, where-
upon the Judge
fell upon his din-
ner and xte it
with joy. When
he had finished, and
the small girl started
to clear away, she
saw a sight that con-
vinced her the erratio
occupant of the Bench
was hopelessly mad.
Windeyer had
taken all the butter-
dishes over to the
door and was rub-
bing the cow-grease into the
hinges with all his might and
grunting vigorously as he did
so. Then he started to work
the door back and forwards
with the energy of a lost soul
striving for a drink. The
weather was hot, and the but-
ter ran down and trickled on
to the floor, and the Judge
stamped through it and spread
it all about the premises.
When the butter was worked
in nicely the squeaking stopped,
and Windeyer buttered the
other doors. When they were
all fixed up, and all the butter
in the house was swilling about
the floor and running out of the
hinges and dripping from his
Honor’s sleeves, he turned with a beam-
ing countenance to the rest of the party
and remarked, “There! thank goodness
we’ll get some sleep to-night now ! ”
Plain Clothes Constable Coakley, one of
the poetical policemen of Collingwood, was once
directed by the Chief Commissioner to ascertain
the paternity of a certain infant. Coakley found
the job a mighty one, for the mother was a girl
with a large heart, and at last the poet was forced
to admit himself beaten. “ I have the honor to
inform you that I have made every endeavor to
discover the father of the offspring of the girl
Polly Andrews,” he wrote, “but I have been
unable to do so. In my opinion it was a syndi-
cate.”
The dull, respectable Duke of Westmin-
ster parted company with his enormous proputty
a few days ago. Death dragged him away from
his real and personal estate. He was the son and
heir of the rich Marquis of Westminster, who died
30 years ago in an odor of stinginess ; and because
the old man had bequeathed him so much ready
money, and the value of his London land and
houses was increasing every year, Her Gracious
Majesty made a duke of him. Subsequent events
proved Westminster’s fitness for the new dignity.
He was a pompous follower of Gladstone until
Home Rule for Ireland came on the board, and
then the bloated millionaire decided to save the
Empire by joining the Liberal-Unionists. Also,
he “ turned dog ” on an oil-painting of Gladstone
which had been painted to his ducal crder by
Millais, or some other highly-expensive artist.
He violently sold the work of art, or raffled it, or
jumped on it, or turned its face to the wall, or
otherwise notified his disapproval of the Grand
Old Man. After which the tottering Empire felt
strong on its legs again. Apart from his being
very rich and terribly angry with Gladstone, the
Duke of Westminster was famous for nothing
save a triumphant racing career. He bought fine
racehorses and bred others from them, and was
the most Derby-winning dook on record. And
when his unrivalled gee-gee Ormonde had gone
wrong in its wind, the proud owner sold it to the
Argentine Republic for <£30,000, and the pur-
chaser made a very bad deal, inasmuch as
Ormonde’s breathing grew rapidly worse. This
shows how a duke may be more advanced than a
republic in some ways.

“Luggan”: Re biting-horses (8., 4/11/’99).
Here’s a cure. Put on three or four coats so that
the horse’s teeth won’t hurt you, and on the out-
side coat-sleeve sprinkle about an oz. of cayenne
pepper. Then let Pegasus have a bite—and he is
cured : that is, if he does not kill himself sneez-
mg. You can also oure gate-opening horses by
tying a piece of cayenne-sprinkled flannel on the
gate in such a way that the horse will have to put
his nose on it when trying to open. The pepper
will do the rest.
A recent experiment
by Prof. Grassi, of Rome,
seems to show the mos-
quito-connection of malaria
rather well. Some miles
out of Rome is a notor-
iously malarial village.
Grassi hired a cottage
there, wherein numerous
cases of the disease had
occurred, carefully ex-
cluded mosquitoes by
means of perforated zino
window and door screens,
and for eight nights, in the
height of malarial season,
lived there in company
with a family consisting of
father, mother and five
children from 1 to 9 years
of age. Nobody was al-
lowed to leave the protected
rooms between sunset and
sunrise. The windows
being left open except for
the perforated screen, all
were exposed to the night
air, and only the ordinary
water of the locality, un-
filtered, was used for
drinking. No case of
malaria occurred among
Grassi’s people, although
during their stay several
P /sons sleeping unprotect-
ed 100yds.„ away were at-
tacked. Grassi states that
the .malarial mosquito—a
species of Anopheles—only
bites between sunset and
sunrise.

A Queensland black-bird captain tells of a
curious form of funeral rite current at Santo, New
Hebrides. The body of the dead man is placed
on a platform in front of the residence of de-
ceased. Ihe wife is supposed to hang herself—
out of respect .to the manes of the departed. The
body is allowed to putrefy until all the bones are
clean. Ihe bones are then used in the prepara-
tion of weapons of war.

Re our friend and brother, the crow :
Dear Bulletin.— The statement made by “P.F.W.”
(B. 30/12/’99) that crows, when poisoned, “ take a draught
of water, then fly away to a tree, grip a small branch with
their claws, and swing themselves head-down until they
vomit the lot ” (poisoned wheat V), is not only absolutely
untrue and absurd, but puerile and silly as well. There
is no Australian bird whose habits I am more intimate
with than with those of Corvus australis. In its
native state, 0. a. is extremely interesting; in
captivity, it is grotesquely charming. It is ever
true, faithful and affectionate to anyone who is
kind to it, but violently vindictive and revengeful
to those who torment it—whether such tormentor be
man or beast. Bees the crow always regards with a hatred
as implacable as it is amusing. Surely every schoolboy
has seen a crow poisoned. When poisoned the crow be-
takes itself to a tree—preferring, always, a dead one.
When the first spasms seize the poor thing (I love crows
—also lizards) it outspreads its wing 3 and gives vent to
one loud, prolonged, and unnatural croak. It is immedi-
ately surrounded by every crow in the neighborhood ;
and their distress is increased in proportion to that of
the poisoned bird. With an agony even greater than
that of l heir dying companion they respond to its every
cry, and emphasise each of its agonised contortions. And
when their beloved friend is silent in death their cries
are like the wails of souls in distress. They fly high in
the air, and, returning again and again to the body of
their dead, make the locality dismal beyond imagination
with their wails of distress. Hundreds—sometimes
thousands—of crovvs appear in a locality which, one short
hour before, contained, only dozens. No other Ausl ralian
bird, with the exception of the sulphur-crested cockatoo,
manifests so much distress as the Australian crow does at
the death of one of its companions ; and no bird in the
world but the Australian crow carries its defunct com-
panion away from the scene of its death. Asa scavenger
the Australian crow is j-ar excellence the most valuable
bird in Australia. Asa noxious-insect destroyer it has
no equal. In the best of seasons on the Bogan, Mount
Harris, Bourke, and Brewarrina districts (N.S.YV.), were
it not for this much-despised and legally-outlawed bird,
every blade of grass would be swept away; but imme-
diately the destructive grasshopper appears in millions,
the crows appear in tens of thousands and exterminate
them. Every squatter knows this.—H. J. M'Cooey.
“ Werrimee ” : I once carried a sucking-
pig four miles in a thick three-bushel bag, and
dumped him into a cocky’s sty. There was no
hole or opening of any kind in the bag, the mouth
being tied up, so am prepared to swear the little
grunter didn’t see, yet in a couple of hours he
came trotting back. Scent or instinct?

LISTENER (unable any longer to contain his feel-
ings): “ Them’s my sentiments. Why, look
ere. I was once in the water for half an hour
off Cape Horn—roughest place in the world-
midwinter-40 miles from land—not a human
being in sight — an’ yet ’ere I am, alive an’
we ll”
THE PREACHER : That was indeed a thrilling ex-
perience. Yet you never lost hope, and your
faith was rewarded, for you were rescued. ”
LISTENER : “ Rescued be blowed I I 'eard the bell
go for the steerage dinner, so I got out of the
bath and dried myself. The reason why there
was nobody in sight was that I ’ad the bath-
room door shut to keep the wimmin passengers
from seein’ in while I ’ad my wash. They yiv ’
us warm water in the bath, or I wouldn't ’ave

HIS REASON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
Neither handsome nor heroio
Lying all the day a-bed,
With the patience of a stoic,
And a door-plate in his head,
Looked the cross-eyed man from Scrubby just inveigled
from the dead.
He was hollow-eyed and shrunken,
And in health took few delights
That were neither gross nor drunken
Judged by our superior lights,
And he often spent his Sabbath putting low disputes to
rights.
Yet admiring fellow-creatures
And the press with ardor vast
O er the most alluring features
Of his deed a glamor cast—
Pete had saved his mate, and borne himself the fury of the
blast.
Now the hero was much better;
Many people came to him
For the spirit and the letter
Of his wondrous tale and grim,
And he told the yarn to order, but with painful lack of
vim.
Yes, a cap-piece floored McCarty,
And the coolest man of five
Ran to save the injured party
Whilst the fuse burned in the drive.
Then she “bust.” For weeks the hero didn’t know he
was alive.
Asked a lady from the city
Why he acted as he did,
And the man’s contemptuous pity
Lowered a disrespectful lid.
“ What yer think ?” he asked. “ Why, bless yer, ma’am,
Mack owed me har/er quid ! ’’

In Giles’records of his ’75 S.A. to W.A.
expeditions he mentions that he often saw at
native camps what looked like a series of babies’
graves—the soil scooped out and heaped on each
side. “ These are the bachelors’ beds,” he writes
—“2O, 30, and 40 sometimes in a row. On top of
each raised portion of soil two small fires are kept
burning in lieu of blankets.” How’s that for
luxurious niggerdom ?
Northern Bishop
Stone - Wigg asseverates
that the Papuans could eat
ship - biscuits the whole
afternoon. Asserts also
that they are very fond of
ants —not the small sort,
but big flying ants, the
size of a cockchafer. They
delight in slugs, but their
favorite relish is somebody
else’s pet pig. They are
too fond (he continues) of
their own little pet pig to
eat it, but they will eat
that of anybody else. The
pigs and the old men are
the great difficulties in
the way of the mission-
aries. The pig can burrow
anywhere ; can climb
through the roof and fall
in. The old men say that
the missionaries upset all
fair fighting. “A man
could see a spear, but
could not see down a gun.”
The native phrase for
“ Who is passing ? ” runs,
“ What to eat goes there?”
There is a little island,
near the mission station,
called Dinner Island —so
called because 20 China-
men were shipwrecked
there, and were brought
in, one by one, to meet
culinary calls, as required.
* *
“ I remember,” said
the old bullocky, “ I was
once yoked to a cedar log
from eight in the morning
till four in the evening,
and could not get a budge
out of it. I put the tape
on it then and found that
I had stretched it four-
foot - six ! ” Then the
other perjurers gave it
best.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.

When Lafayette was in the U.S. two
young men were introduced. He said to one—
“ Are you married ? ” “ Yes, sir,” was the reply.
“ Happy man,” quoth the General. Then he put
the same question to the other, who replied—“ I
am a bachelor.” “ Lucky dog ! ” said the General.
The best essay on matrimony extant.

N.S.W. country town, and this notice was found

Ever-droughty Broken Hill broke even its
drought record last year—registered 5'32in., and as
the fall was d i vided among 33 days, nomore than one
shower, resultinginfin., was any real good. Broken
Hill, the silver town, can’t expect a deluge every
year. In ’9B, it had 8‘52i0. during the 12 months ;
and when nearly 2in. of that fell in one day people
waded out to church, reckoning that the end had
come. Only had fallen in the preceding
three years—on 145 days. But the district record
belongs to the “ sixties ” (that was years before
Broken Hill was built), when the stations got
along for a couple of years on 6fin. That was
before rabbits and over-stocking had killed most
of the saltbush.
O stewed plums !
In the beginning of the summer with what delight do we
behold thee.
O stewed plums, thou fraud !
O stewed plums 1
Thou tempteit me with thy richness of hue and thy
beguiling'smell and I do eat.
Thou fraud !
0 stewed plums !
Even when accompanied with plain boiled rice or cool
blane-mange, thy powers are potent and they pain
me.
O stew-ed plums, thou fraud !
O stewed plums !
At the end of the summer when the wintry blasts begin
to blow, how we shudder at the thoughts of thee !
O stewed plums, thou fraud !

SOME OF “HOP’S” IDEAS, MILITARY AND OTHERWISE.


A. BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away
pimples, blotches, roughness, redness, and all dis-
figurements. Sulpholine developes a lovely skin.
Is. bottles. Made in London.*
Strange to say, the cycle of evolution
seems to bring us back to the beginning in matters
of locomotion; and after the sea-going nations have
held the balance of power for so long a time, the
overland route threatens to take the place again of
the briny one as a means of carriage. The railway,
as far as the old world is concerned, will form the
tentacles with which the boss grabsters cling to
their colonial empires. There are at present
three great routes in prospective : the Trans-
Siberian, dominating the north of Europe and
Asia; the Cape-Cairo, practically the life artery
of the whole of Africa ; and the Berlin -Vienna-
Constantinople-Palestine-Persia railway. The
first Russian, the second British, the third possibly
German.

Yon Kotze on a delicate subject :
During the recent Buckley divorce case the Judge re-
marked that it would be a “ terrible and revolting” state
of affairs if the wife were not permitted to refuse cohabi-
tation, after having borne a certain number of children.
Well, I beg to arise on the side of the maligned but
virile Mr. Buckley and humbly ask, what in thunder the
learned Judge expects the poor man to do? He is forced
into a very unprofitable state of celibacy, and, no doubt,
if he runs after strange goddesses he will he pulled up
and sermonised, and dropped in for heavy expenses by
(he same erudite and shocked judiciary. But, apparently,
Platonism doesn’t agree with Buckley, and bigamy is for-
bidden, and adultery leads to maintenance orders and
damages and costs and similar misfortune, and—what is
Mr. Buckley to do?
In the days of the Blavatsky triumphal
progress Professors Huxley and Max Mueller
published many hard sayings about Madame and
her alleged esoteric sciences. But spite of all the
hard sayings one fact seems indisputable: that
certain races of India, whence the lady said she
derived her knowledge, have the power of thought
transference, known as telepathy, over consider-
able distances. There are, as far as the present
writer knows, no records of any force of Sepoys
being surprised during the Indian Mutiny by the
British ; the “secret mail ” always kept the black
men posted as to the white men’s comings and
goings. And in connection with the Nichol-
sen’s Nek surrender of the British it is alleged
that the very day of the disaster —almost the very
hour—rumors were rife in the Native Bazaars
throughout India that thousands of Fcringhees had
been captured by the Boers. The rumor was far
ahead of any telegraphic intelligence. This is
either a great mystery or a great lie.
London Evening News perpetrated' this
on the clay when the first bad news arrived from
South Africa, and London was unduly excited
over its beer, considering the very limited dimen-
sions of the event:
“HAWFUL SLORTER!"
Oh, there’s dreadful news from Ladysmith, where White
is hotly pressed;
The people in the Blank Hotel can’t get a moment’s rest;
Their beauty sleep is broken by the firing of the foe;
And we’ve just heard all about it from the man who runs
the show.
We have pigeongrams from White
That the damage has been slight,
And the ineffective cannonade he’s scorning,
And the man who sells the drinks
Says exactly what he thinks ;
And there’s dreadful news from Ladysmith this morning.
Yes, ere long our gallant army will be numbered with the
past,
For our guns are wretched failures and are hopelessly
outclassed,
And the corpulent head-waiter says his hopes are growing
dim ;
But he’s sure he’d have done better if they’d left the job
to him.
So, despite what White may say,
They’re expecting ev’ry day
That the Transvaal flag the town will he adorning ;
For the way the foeman shoots
Has alarmed the under-" boots
And there’s dreadful news from Ladysmith this morning.
Come, we’ll make a splashy heading for this shocking bit
of “ news ”
(Which is hardly a description of poor Boniface’s views),
For it really doesn’t matter if the tale is rather steep,
If we horrify the public and we make theirflesh to creep 1
Yes, there’s quite a dreadful scare,
For the page has lost his hair,
And the pretty chambermaid has given warning;
And you’ll hear our newsboys shout
Of the coming British rout!
“ Shockin’, hawful noos from Lydysmiff dis mornin’ 1 ”


Government Notices.
Land and Income Tax Office, Custom House,
Sydney, 19th December, 1899.
LAND AND INCOME TAX ASSESSMENT ACT OF 1895.
is hereby given, under section 30 of the above
t ha , r ' Returns of Income received during the
year 18.).) for the purposes of the assessment of Income
JLax tor the year 1900 are required to be furnished to the
Commissioners of Taxation, at their office, on or before
the ird any oj February, 1900, and nil persons who are in
receipt of income, and all persons liable under the pro-
visions of the Act in any representative capacity to
furnish returns for the purposes aforesaid, are hereby in-
formed that (he prescribed Forms of Returns are now
available, and may be obtained (except those for Com-
panies) on application at any Post Office, Police Office ,
Custom House, Railway Station, and the Office of any
Clerk of Petty Sessions in New South Wales, Stamp
p J* lh P'street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Oj/ice, Custom House , Sydney.
n ,^™i or C ° nl P anie » can be obtained at the Stamp
Dudes Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or pro-
fession on their own account are requi-e.j to furnish In-
come Returns irrespective of the amount of their income •
but no Return of Income is required from any person in
receipt of salary or wages only if the gross annual
amount of such salary or wages does not exceed £2OO.
All employers of labour, whether they themselves are
liable to make Income Tax Returns or not, are required
to furnish to the Commissioners of Taxation the name
occupation, and address of all employes whose salaries
or wages for the year 1899 aggregated £lOO and upwards.
Public attention is called to the fact that
a penalty not exceeding t-yenty pounds is
imposed on any person neglecting to furnish
the Return required of him within the time above
mentioned, and that persons failing to furnish such
Return are not relieved from liability to the penalty by
reason only that they may not have received a notice to
furnish the Return, or that the prescribed Form may not
have been delivered to them.
The attention of owners of taxable lands subject to
mortgage is invited to the necessity for furbishing the
requisite particulars, in order that they may derive Ihe
benefit of the deduction to which they are entitled in
respect of the interest on the mortgage of their lands
including improvements thereon. Without these parti-
culars the Commissioners will be unable to make the
statutory deduction from the tax on the unimproved
values. -
Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained on
application at any of the above mentioned offices and
should be furnished on or before the 3rd day of February
1900, before mentioned.

L. S. SPILLER, J

and directions with the Forms of Return.

The Glory of Man.
Tiie crown and glory of manhood is strength—
power. All human conquests have been won by
it; individual and collective. The earliest sign of
decay in man is not usually pain, but weakness of
some kind—of mind or body, or both. This may
be the inevitable sequence of advanced years, or
it may, as we all know, show itself at any period
of life. But it always means that the sources of
strength are failing, as the lessening of the water
in a river means a drying up of the springs and
streams which feed it.
I said “ sources' ’ of strength, using the plural
word. I should have said “ source ” of strength,
for in human beings there is but one. What is it ?
Perhaps the experience of Mr. David Jones will
help us to an answer.
“ In the summer of 1881,” he says, “ I began to
feel ill and out of sorts. I wa3 weak and tired
and quite worn out with little exertion. I had a
poor appetite, and after eating had pain and
weight at the chest. My secretions were scanty
and thick. My back felt stiff and gave me much
pain as I moved about. For three years I suf-
fered like this, being at times better and then
worse. I consulted a doctor, who gave me medi-
cines and recommended Turkish baths ; but I got
no better for any kind of treatment.
“ My brother then told me about Mother Sei-
gel’s Curative Syrup, but I had no faith in adver-
tised medicines. He, however, urged me to take
it, and after taking a few doses I experienced so
much relief that I continued with it, and was soon
strong and well as ever. Since that time I take a
dose of this remedy when I feel at all ailing, and
d sets me right. I have praised Mother Seigel’s
Syrup to all my friends, many of whom have
tried it and been benefited by it. You can pub-
lish this statement if you like. (Signed) David
Jones, 1 Dunlace-street, Walton, Liverpool, July
31, 1896.”
We have room for one more short letter before
we try to come at the answer to the question with
which we set out. It is exactly in the same line,
and ought to make the results of our enquiry all
the more clear and plain.
i( “I n the spring of 1888,” proceeds the writer,
‘ my health began to fail me. I felt languid and
heavy, as if something had come over me. I had
a bad taste in the mouth, my appetite was poor,
and the little food I took gave me pain across the
chest and between the shoulders. My food lay
like lead on my stomach. I was constantly
spitting up a thick phlegm, and when in bed
heavy sweats came over me. I got weaker and
weaker, and from time to time was confined to
bed. Often I was so bad I could not dress or un-
dress myself.
Now better, now worse, but never well, I
suffered for over five years. I took different kinds
of medicines, and had a doctor, but got no real
benefit from anything.
‘I In July, 1 , )3, a book was left at my house in
which I read of cases like mine having been cured
by Mother Siegel’s Syrup. I got a bottle from
the International Tea Company, Yeovil, and be-
gan taking it. In a short time I felt much benefit,
and, by continuing with it, gained strength. I
could eat better, food agreed with me, and by-and-
bye I was strong and well. But for Mother Siegel’s
Syrup I believe I should not now be alive. You
are at liberty to publish this statement. (Signed)
(Mrs.) .Jane Cronel, 82 Huish, Yeovil, November
29th, 1895,
What, then, do these cases show to be the source
of strength ? The answer is, Digested Food. That
and nothing else. All strength, of body or of
mind, comes from that, and that only. What
then, is the great enemy of strength, the fountain
of weakness? Indigestion—dyspepsia. You see
why ami how. A child could not miss the argu-
ment. What, then, restores strength? Mother
Siegel s Syrup. In what way ? By seeing the
digestive machinery in healthy operation. That
is what it did for our correspondents and does
daily for multitudes. Use it if you need it, and
pass the news to other weak ones.
An English paper describes the departure
of the Guards for the Transvaal:
The only exception to the general rule of admirable
behaviour has been Her Majesty’s Guards, and they must
perhaps be judged with a lenient eye, having regard to
their character as the pets of the London populace and
the special temptations to which they are exposed. But
their exodus from London last Saturday was by no means
oreditable to the Household Brigade. The scenes on the
way to Waterloo station recalled Hogarth’s famous pic-
ture of the March of the Guards to Finchley. The band
played “The girl I left behind me,” but the “girl,”
whose name was legion, would not he left behind.
Hardly a man in the line of march but had two or three
sweet creatures hanging on to him, and very few had not
been taking a parting glass. Free drinks had no doubt
been distributed widely, with the natural result. At the
tail end of battalions marching to entrain came cabs and
carts laden with the casualties, the weak heads who had
succumbed. I myself noticed a four-wheel cab with five
inside, all well “ on,” and on the roof a gay Guardsman
under the personal conduct of a corporal whom the drunk
was alternately trying to kiss and push off the cab, while
the crowd howled with delight, enjoying the fun. At the
station the Guards lay about in dozens more or less in-
| capable, and a number it was said had to be left behind
j in barracks until sober enough to travel. The senior
[ officers of the Guards are much chagrined at this
| loosening of the bonds of discipline.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advb. on page
27 of this issue.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
London Referee opines that “ the little
Englanders will now allude to Charteredsas guilt-
edged securities. ”
Victorian stop-gap Premier M‘Lean was
largely interested in the defunct Maffra Beet
Sugar Co. Hence the gibe, “ the Dead-Beet
Ministry.”
Thanks to J. G. Drake’s defection, the
Independent candidate is a back number now all
over Queensland. The independent candidate for
Enoggera polled 28 votes !
That cable item about how the Australian
oroops were “ exposed to a terrific fire” or “a
rain of lead ” for half an hour, and the casualties
were one soldier bruised through falling over a
root, grows wearisome.
Treasurer Ward, of Maoriland, proposes
*o try the experiment of an enormous reduction
(something like 40 per cent.) in railway fares.
Certainly, Maoriland railway fares are very high,
and there is much room for reduction.
The Q. Labor-party was “left on the
mat ” as regards the promised One-man-one-vote
Bill. Premier Philp dodged it for a few weeks,
and then, when introducing several feeble little
Bills, alleged “no time this session.” And if he
oan help it there won’t be time any session.
AFTER THE LODGE.
FIRST GRAND OX: “N', Vk ’ere, ol’ m’n, y’r
c’m’n’n' fr’ a drink ’fore yer yo ’ome?”
SECOND GRAND OX : “ Ain’t it a bit late, sonny?
Le’me see! how's the enemy? ”
FIRST GRAND OX : “O’, she’s al ri',she’s
'sleep. ”
Drunk with their success in throwing out
M‘Cay at Castlemaine, Victoria, the jingoes are
arranging an onslaught upon Hamilton at Ben-
digo, for having dared to speak against the export
of the Vic. Boer-butchers. But the Bendigo
miners of to-day and the remnant of the old
diggers are about the last people in the world to
punish honesty and grit and to offer a premium to
hypocrisy and froth, so there is a fair chance that
the scheme will fail.
S.A. Leg. Council last session contemptu-
ously rejected two different proposals for franchise
reform, besides measures for early-closing, work-
men’s compensation, and assessment of land
values. And S.A. is equipping a second Contingent
to fight for the oppressed Ooflauders in the Trans-
vaal !
Premier Lyne intimates vyillingness to
grant 28-year land leases at Norfolk Island, but
says he will insist on judicious use and continuous
residence. Considering that a man can hardly
escape continuous residence there unless he falls
into the sea the second condition seems somewhat
superfluous.
General Connaught applied to be let go to
the war with Roberts of Candahar, and met with a
loud, prompt refusal. Now, couldn’t Connaught
have found out beforehand whether he’d be
allowed to go, and thus have saved himself a pub-
lic refusal ? Or did he know before he applied
that he wouldn’t be allowed to leave his aged
parent ?
There is no satisfying some men, as wit-
ness this recent little breeze in Queensland
Assembly:—
Mr. McDonald : “If the Home Secretary will get his
hair off— ”
The Chairman of Committees : “ Order ! ”
Mr. M‘Donald : “ Well, if the Home Secretary will keep
his hair on—”
The Chairman of Committees: “ Order ! ”
Mr. M'Donald : “Well, if the Home Secretary has any
hair—”
The Chairman of Committees : “ Order ! Order ! ”
The man who supplies cable news to Aus-
tralia feels bound to earn hia salary by tickling
the long ears of the Australian ass. So he wires
that the inhabitants of Douglas (S. Africa) were
glad when the Boers were driven out of the town,
but when they found that it was the Australian
and Canadian troops who had driven them out
their joy was inexpressible. Why they were so
much gladder at being relieved by the Australian
Bill than by the British Tommy isn’t explained,
but it seems they almost fell down dead with joy.
The extinction of Gillies as
a political factor is a feature of
the Victorian situation that has
escaped remark through its very
completeness. The cherubic lit-
tle fellow was supposed to have
some talent for tactics, if he had
lost every other fitness for leader-
ship. He is no orator, he is dis-
credited in finance, and he is a
proved failure in constructive
statesmanship ; but his admirers
still said, “ Oh, he is a rare
hand at manoeuvring.” Nobody
can say that of him now. He
might have made his own terms
with M'Lean. He failed to do
so, and lost his chance. In con-
jecturing what may happen, no-
body thinks of mentioning
Gillies. He is a forgotten article
at the political dead-letter office.
Poor defaulting Drake bade
good-bye to his Enoggera (Q.)
constituents in a wretched pul-
ing address printed in the
Courier. The gist of the afore-
said apology consisted in a state-
ment that the local Worker, by
advising electors in future to
vote for straight Labor candi-
dates (advice which was given
subsequent to Drake’s defection),
had really justified J.G.D. in his
tergiversation. The perverted
legislator went on further to
assert that there was no evil like
a -weak Government, for which
reason he had allied himself to a
strong one. A few years ago the
Enoggera M.L.A. used to loudly
assert that there was no evil
like a corrupt and dishonest
Government; and that the Con-
tinuous Ministry of Queensland
was such a Government.
The only real volunteers in
the Boer war are the Boers them-
selves. They fight for home and
freedom, and do not get a doit of
pay. The British Tommy Atkins
is a mercenary at Is. 2d. a day.
The Australian contingent are
bigger mercenaries, for they get
the bigger pay —4s. 6d. a day.
And the war expenses are already
beeinning to be charged against
the good old loan, so it appears
that the loyal Australian who
goes won’t go without pay, and
the loyal Australian who stays
at home won’t pay. If the pre.
sent generation is to have the
glory, and excitement, and
change, and whoop, and pensions,
and titles, and V.C.’s, and leaves
future generations to pay the dreary
bill, the only possible inference is that
the present generation is two frauds and
a sham and a hollow swindle.
The re-arming of the Vic. military forces
with the Martini-Enfield rifle looked like a hideous
job when it was perpetrated ten months ago, and
in the light of the recent action of the British
commanders it grows still more smellful. The
Martini-Enfields cost the country £IO,OOO, and
despite the strong protest of people who pointed
out that the weapon was obsolete and abandoned
in Europe, the deal was carried out. The rifles
arrived in Melbourne, but to this day hardly any
have been served out, and in all probability they
will go direct from the stores to the pawnshops to
be retailed at 10s. each for the defence of the home
from cats. The Vic. contingent landed in South
Africa with 250 of the old arquebuses, only to
have them replaced instantly with Lee-Metfords.
Victoria is to be asked to shell out another
.£IO,OOO at least to bring her armament up to date.
Who is that calling “ Police ” ?
A usually well-informed cor-
respondent writes: —
Premier Bill Lyne, when asked just
before the recess if he would not stop
piling old people on top of each ,
other in the asylums, promised y
to consult Col. Sec. See about
it. What See thinks is suffi-
ciently shown by the fact that
he is stopping all the little
weekly allowances to the aged
paid from his office as fast as
they (being- only granted for
limited terms) fall in, giving
the recipients their choice of
“ Asylum or nothing.” One
old fellow who had been re-
ceiving 108. a week for his
wife and himself, applied for
a renewal. No enquiries, so
far as he knows, were made;
but the above alternative
was offered. Both husband
and wife promptly replied
that they would starve rather
than give up their liberty and
go into an asylum, and so the
matter stands.
Is the Lyne Government
saving money off the local
destitute in order to help
pay for its Boer-prodding
campaign ?
“ Britishah ” : Our
olergy are namby-pamby, mealy-
mouthy, and oreepy-erawly in their
pulpit prayers over this war. Let us
have some regular good red-hot gospel shots.
Treasurer Dickson, of Queensland, makes
the astounding assertion that all the Premiers
interested, save Lyne, of N.S.W., have agreed to
pay the expenses incurred by the British New
Guinea land syndicate in unsuccessfully trying to
steal a huge section of New Guinea without the
assent of these provinces, as required by law.
Queensland has already voted its share of the
expenses. This is about the most amazing case of
sheep-like humility in Australia’s history.
In various parts of Australia the violent
and drinky “ loyalist ” has broken out in bitter
animosity against Germans. In fact, he claims to
be so devoted to his purely German Queen, and his
wholly German Prince of Wales, and all the Ger-
man royal family, that his yearning to bash every
German he meets is unconquerable. How Queen
Victoria will feel if she ever hears, in her peace-
ful old age, that the alleged loyalists of Broken
Hill have been trying to wipe up the town with
her countrymen is a difficult question. Possibly
she will wish that the loyalists of Broken Hill
were dead.
Every year the question of denominational
education crops up in Queensland Parliament,
more or less disguised. During last session the
sectarian crowd made a big stride. It got a
motion through the House that State scholarships
should be open to pupils of private and sectarian
schools, and that the winner of a scholarship
might take out his one or two or three years of
State-paid higher education at a denominational
school if so inclined. When the State thus provides
indirect cash assistance for denominational schools,
the free, secular and compulsory principle has in-
directly gone broke.
A Teuton writing to Melb. ’Ebald on the
“ pro-Boer-British-anti ” subject, .remarks that
“ the onlooker always sympathises with the party
who will get beaten in the end.” Also, he re-
minds all and sundry anti-German Jingoes (many
of whom have no minds worth mentioning) that
British sympathy was with the French in the
Franco-German war. Just as well for the new
Australian generation, born since 1870, to know
that their progenitors showed a noisier anti-Ger-
man feeling (when the French were going to get
beaten in the end) than the anti-British feeling
exhibited by the Germans to-day.
A letter to an Adelaide daily :
As the Government are thinking of sending another
contingent to Africa, I would suggest that the men be
drawn from the distressed farmers. There are hundreds
of these who would jump at the chance. They are
accustomed to long hours, privations, and a rough life,
and know how to use a rifle. They would not get sore
feet with walking a mile or two. There are plenty of
these men who would rather meet the Boers than their
creditors year after year, and who, if a bullet found its
way home, would lay down and be thankful it was over.
Their horses, too, have learnt to live on sand and salt-
bush.
Heaven only knows how that ever got printed
there !
The cry of intelligent jingoism i.e.,
jingoism which hasn’t gone clean daft must be,
“Lord, save us from the Press!” One special
correspondent, quoted by Melb. Age, wrote:
“The Boers disregarded every recognised law of
civilised warfare. Dum-dum bullets were used,
and every horse on the plain was made a mark of
in the hope of hitting the rider. The British
artillery continued its excellent work, and shell
after shell fell right into the enemy’s trenches.”
It is hard to raise a splendid patriotic disgust for
the Boer, who hopes to shoot the other fellow
what time the other fellow’s cannon is trying hard
to blow the marksman into bits.
The jingo press, affirming that America
sympathises with Britain in the Boer war, finds
it hard to explain away the vote of sympathy with
the Transvaal passed by the City Councils of Bos-
ton and New York. The fact is, that the U.iS.
is only about one-fourth Anglo-Saxon by descent,
and therefore the Anglo-Saxon brotherhood can’t
come off for want of the brother. There was a
burst of pro-British enthusiasm during the late
war, when Britain was using her influence to pre-
vent Spain receiving assistance, but already it is
cooling down. Gratitude doesn’t last long. Also,
gratitude, like charity, begins at home. Like-
wise business comes 'before gratitude.
Now that particulars of the Maoriland
local option poll are to hand it really seems that
the Cold Tea Brigade has some reason for its con-
fidence. By the existing law the Accursed Drink
can’t be abolished in any district unless three-
fifths of the votes cast are for “no licence,” and

but for this proviso the party would have carried

there were 16 wherein the “ no licence ” vote out-

good many districts the “no licence ” vote was

That section of the press which describes
the present war as a fight to decide whether Dutoh
or British shall own South Africa, apparently
overlooks the fact that there is a third party to the
question. Portugal (which might be a fourth
party) is too weak to count for anything, but Ger-
many now owns almost as much South African
soil as the British Government does, and if the
great red-painting of the map is to be accom-
plished there is a very large difficulty still ahead.
Here is the extraordinary series of deci-
sions and motions and amendments by which
Westralia’s Upper House rejected Federation.
It voted : (1) That the Federal question ought to
be submitted to a popular vote. (2) That the
proposal to submit to the people both Federal
Bills (the one accepted in the eastern provinces
and the one proposed by the W.A. Parliamentary
Committee) ought not to be adopted. (3) That
the new Bill proposed by the W.A. Parliamentary
Committee should be submitted to the people by
itself. (4) That Westralia should not ask the
other provinces to accept its amended Bill. (5)
That no Federal Bill whatever should be submitted
to the people. Then, feeling it had done its duty
and saved the country this extraordinary Council
shambled back to bed.
N.S.W. P.-M.-G. Crick on war matters :
The Postmaster-General said it was immaterial where
the money came from, because the money must come. If
it became necessary for the country to give a million of
money he dared say that this country would find this
money. (Loud cheers.) At the time of the Russian war
scare Mr. Gladstone came down and asked the British
House of Commons for a war vote of 11 millions.
Without one word of debate the English Parliament,
trusting in the administration of its Government, voted,
the money.
It was Disraeli, not Gladstone, who asked for
these 11 millions in the Russian war scare days of
the late ’7o’s —not that Crick’s ignorance of history
matters much. The main point is that this marks
another departure on the road to Irresponsible
Government. It has already been laid down that
Australia shouldn’t inquire into the justice of a
British war before joining in it; this country has
no voice in making British wars, but it is its duty,
as a loyal and wholly unreasoning animal without
brains, to rush in and bite the individual that
Britain is biting. Now it is further laid down
that it should also rush in without the faintest
idea about the size of the bill or how it is to
be paid. Moreover, from a strictly financial point
of view, trusting to Disraeli was a very different
thing from trusting Crick or Lyne. Disraeli
covered the expanse by nearly doubling the
income tax. If Crick and Lyne were honest
enough and courageous enough to face the
responsibility in like manner, they might also
expect to be trusted.
OUR COUNTRY.
Land of flies and land of bullocks,
Land of tears and land of sighs,
Land of dreary wastes and hillocks,
When the spirits never rise.
Where the dreamy sad-eyed oxen
Chew their cud beneath the trees ;
While above the jackass mocks ’em,
Praying for a cooling breeze.
Where they send men from the country
In another’s cause to fight;
When their own grey arid homesteads,
Long for streams of water bright.
Land of heat and suffocation,
Sweltering under skies of brass,
You will never be a nation
While you choose to be an ass.
KELPIE.

When prisoners Williams and Littlejohn
broke out of Hobart gaol and were re-captured,
Sheriff Ross held an inquiry, which was remark-
able for the points missed, or left undeveloped
when they cropped up. But, on the strength of
it, Premier and Attorney-General N. K. Lewis
ordered a warder named Burrows to be disrated.
It was clear enough to anyone but a civil servant
or a Minister that tlie gaol and the Government
were to blame, not Burrows ; but the sacrifice of
a warder was neither here nor there. It was
shown that eight prisoners, one or two of whom
had escaped on former occasions, were put to
bathe in eight bathrooms that had no fastenings
on the doors; that the yard-door was bolted on
the inside ; and that a prisoner passing out of it,
after drawing a bolt, had only to throw his leg
across the Governor’s garden-fence and walk
through the Governor’s house into the publio
■taeefe. This w«s just what Williams and Little
john did. It was so absurdly easy that an infant
or a cripple could have escaped. It is all very
good to blame the solitary warder who had to look
after the eight prisoners and do other things as
well, but if the Government is too hard-up to buy
locks for its gaol, things will happen.
An important reason of Buller’s inaction
in Natal seems to be Britain’s lack of the sordid
but useful mule. Buller ran his troops up opiv>-
site Colenso by train, but the Boers fortified the
hills over against the river fords, and the position
was too strong to be taken. And there Buller is
stuck. He cannot move up the river or across
country by Weenan or anywhere else because the
railway doesn’t run there and he has no mules.
Great is the glory of the mule.

There are two sides to every war-story,
and between the British fake-yarn and the Boer
fake-yarn you can take your pick. British say
the Boers fired on the Geneva cross at Mafeking.
Boers retort that British spread Geneva crosses
all over the place; pretty nearly every building
had a Geneva cross, making the bombardment
futile. So they notified they would respect one
and shell all the others ; and the British dodge
didn’t work. British say Boers fire on white-flag
parties after wounded. Boers say that, as soon as
British are beaten, they go “ hunting wounded ’
with white flags in parties of 40 or 50, and so
legitimate captives get off clear. And so on.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Mile Swimming Championship of the group, at
Sydney Domain Baths, Monday night, was contested by
Hamilton (M.L.), Maynard (V.), Bond, Dick Cavill,
Kearney, and plugger Smith (Sydney). They were well
together for 300yds., but henceforth young Dick gradually
drew away, tacking yard after yard on to his lead, until
the gap swelled to somewhere about 60 at the finish.
Bond (N.S.W. crack half-miler), who followed,
spurted a couple of times and decreased the vast
perspective of water between —but only on sufferance.
Maynard dropped behind from the jump—first inches,
then feet, and, by-and-by yards, till, at 1350, he turned
with Cavill, exactly two laps (90yds.) behind. ’Twas
whispered that this man would break 27, but he
couldn’t have got within coo-ee of it had he finished,
which he didn’t. M.L. champion, Hamilton, with-
drew, hopelessly out of the chase, at 350yds.,
and Kearney cried a go, very much in the rear,
200yds. further on. Time of winner (26min. 52 2-ssec.)
equals Australasian best, to credit of his brother Percy,
and is within 6see. of world’s amateur record (26min.
4645ec.), put up by one-time invincible Tyers, now a pro.
Boy Dick swam wonderfully well, as may be gathered
from the time. He was unlucky not to have entered for
the half-mile local supremacy, secured by Bond, and un-
luckier still in being shut out of the final of the hundred
on Saturday.
* • • •
In the big swimmingsprintstruggle, being second
in the fastest heat, Cavill should have started for the final,
but officials wouldn’t countenance this ; yet they allowed
it in the Quarter-mile Championship at Bathurst on New
Year’s Day. As it turned out, Dick would certainly have
annexed the title, for the effort against Cat ill took all
the steam out of Bishop, and Craig consequently went
within a hair’s breadth of snatching the race on the post.
Had little Dick started, he could have “ saddled up"
better than any other, and Bishop and Craig would have
been second and third instead of first and second, with
probably a second, or a good fraction thereof, knocked off
the time. Such a surprise was the Farm Cove
boy’s swimming that, after beating him in the
heat, Bishop looked up at the judges and, in
between a series of heavy respirations, said: “ My
Gawd! that’s a pretty hot lot to run up against.”
Later, when asked if he objected to Dick starting in
the final, he remarked, “ Oh ! I ’o'lited ’iin wonce.an’ ain’t
goin’ to stand ‘im ’ummin' round agin—not me, it ain’t
good enough.” Cavill’s swimming was the biggest eye-
opener sprung on Sydney swimmers for a very long time.
None of his top-notch brothers could have, at their best,
and under like conditions, covered the hundred as fast as
he did. Ernie, Percy, Tunis, and Sid are all well-built
athletic-looking chaps, but Dick, with his big deep chest
and sturdy' under-pins, is moulded on ideal lines for
swimming. He’ll be a world-beal er within another four
or five years. Prior to swimming on Saturday', Dick
covered 98 measured yards iu 1"2, but it was kept very
dark.
• * * *
The 100yds. championship was reminiscent
of neck-and-neck-struggles between Freddy Lane
and “Mud" Bishop last season. First heat went to
school-boy Grant (Balmain) in 1.7 3-5. Said that
this youngster licked Bishop by three-quarters of a yard
iu a trial 50yds. struggle recently'. Second heat was won
by “ Mud ’’ Bishop, who beat 15-year-old Dick Cavill by’ a
foot in 1.4 1-5. Erratic course caused Dick to lose
a little. Craig (Balmain) easily secured third heat,
while the fourth went to Kearney (West Sydney), 1.8 4-5.
Bishop led by 4yds. at the end of 50 in the final, but
Craig,'going at a great pace, gradually wiped out the
difference. They finished the last few yards together
amid intense excitement. Judges ruled that Bishop
“ touched’’ first, but there was no touch. A fast-ebbing
tide had left the rope 2yds. above the water. Time taken
was 1.5 1-s—a second slower, than that put up by the
winner against Cavill. Bishop faded a lot in the last
half of the journey, hence Craig’s bold hid. Grant
(third man) protested that the first two failed to comply
with rule which says rope must be touched. Matter wiil
be considered this week.
* • • •
Plunging supremacy of Australasia, contested at
Sans Sctici (Sydney') on Saturday, occupied lhr. smin.
Coates (holder of mother province premiership) won with
68ft. 6iu.—ten inches better than second man, Keers
(N.S.W.) M.L. representative Hamilton, (Wellington),
was third (56ft. 6in.), while T. Andrews and J. Kemp
(N.S.W.) tied, covering 55ft. 6in. each. The rest were
comparatively' nowhere. Coates weighs about 135 r.., but
Keers completely upsets theory that bulk is essential in
plunging ; he only sca'es about 9st. and easily' accounted
for eight others weighing from 14 up to 17.7. Whilom
champion of N.S.W., Harry Mulcahy, had a go after
being away from it for several years, but lack of wind
settled him inside 30seo. in each of three tries. World’s
best, under similar conditions to those obtaining Satur-
day (Imin. limit), is 78ft. 9in., created by Taylor ab
Bootle (Eng.) in ’9B. Five (Coates, Keers, Hamilton,
Andrews and Kemp) get standard certificates, which call
for a plunge of at least 55ft.
* * • •
“ Crank” : Forty-five feet high dive by Fritz Von
Hammer at Sans Souci (Sydney), Saturday, calls to mind
th#fact that the genus bridge-jumper and high-diver has
not been in evidence of late years—not since the old
batch took final leaps one after the other. If
memory serves, poor Peart (Sydney) and Tommy’ Burns
(London), who fairly reveiled in the game, were about the
last.. Yankee Steve Brodie started the craze, and
gathered in heaps of dollars with his jumps (really drops)
from Brooklyn and Poughkeepsie Bridges. He is the
only one that survived. Brodie runs a big saloon, and
lives in affluence in New York.
• * • *
Biennial Australasian swimming championships
(now being contested in Sydney) are in one sense a farce.
M.L.’s only rep. is a 1.11 man for the hundred ; there are
dozens of schoolboys here who can do better. S.A. sent
nobody, neither did Q. Maynard (Vic.) is the one pro-
perly-endorsed visiting swimmer. M.L. Association com-
plains that it had no information of dates, venues,
or anything else until too late. A.S.A. secretary
positively asserts he forwarded all necessary particulars
in ample time. Writer thinks matter should be probed
iu the interests of all the provinces—M.L. especially, for
that province never sent less than half-a-dozen repre-
sentatives before.
• * • •
Last mail from ’Ome brought news that Hunter
River (N.S.W.) sculler Geo. Towns had challenged world's
champion Gaudaur for a “monkey’’ a-side, but. as the
cable has been silent concerning the matter, it may be
assumed that wily old Canuck Jake is not “ taking any ”
of (he Australian, and has remained dumb as an oyster on
the subject. Shonlhaven (N.S.W.) Jim Wray crossed the
“ Herring-pond ’’for the purpose of trying to force Jim
Stanbury’s vanquisher from his lair, but it was no go ;
Jake got out by vowing I hat he would pull nowhere but
at Rat-portage, and Wray replied that there was no
chance of him being enticed into a rat-trap. Old man
Gaudaur will probably never row again, though be may
make-believe a lot. Towns should close on the title-
through Jake’s default. Gaudaur, who has been
rowing beyond the memory of the oldest inhabitant,
paralysed Australia three years ago by defeating Aus-
tralian Jim Stanburv. This country has been lately
under the impression that the game wag dead as a

• * * *
Recent N.S.W. League (Cyclists’) acetylene-
gas carnival was a failure so far as racing and gate were
concerned ; this despite persistent booming by a certain
Sydney daily. As is usually the case, there’s trouble
among the management. Secretary attributes loss to
absence of prominent riders and “inclement weather”
(as if they didn’t take good care to postpone upon the
slightest indication of inclement weather !). “ February
nv eting will recoup us,” hopefully remarked the see.
But then one delegate moved, and it was carried, “That
the race committee doesn’t retain the confidence of this
Council.” Whereupon the “ race committee ” resigned.
After the previous meeting, which was a success, the
“ race committee ” came in for unmeasured praise—but
RICHMOND THEOPHILUS CAVILL, aged
16, Champion Mile Swimmer of Australasia.
such is life. Joe Megson brought down censure upon
himself (or asking to be treated like riders from other
provinces—he wanted a tenner “appearance money,"
and when it wasn’t forthcoming, Joe notified through the
columns of the daily press that he wouldn’t compete ;
this “so as to clear himself with his employer (who ob-
jected to him racing) and the public.” Inference is that
had the tenner been in evidence, Joe’s employer’s objec-
tions would have heen got rid of. Oh ! why wasn’t that
tenner paid ? It would have brought a big “gate,” and
all would now be well.
• • • •
In the Vic. v. S.A. match two full days’ play on
the perfect JI.C.C. wicket produced 493 runs. That’s not
good enough to keep interest in the game alive, and, as a
matter of fact, it didn't. Four thousand people, largely
deadheads, sat around the ground on New Year’s Day, as
against 3(1,000 who had gone there to gaze at bicycle races
a fortnight earlier. The heat was not accountable lor
all the falling off in the attendance.
• * * •
Jack Lyons states that he will probably retire
from first-class cricket at the end of this season. Lyons
was one of the finest hitters the game has seen, and his
greatest feats have been performed on English grounds,
particularly at Lord s. The writer can rememher when
Jack was, as a lad, a mighty smiter of junior bowling in
Adelaide, and his great rival then was Walter Giffen.
Lyons’ name will always be coupled with that of Alec.
Bannerman, who maintained many a mighty stonewall
at one end when “ Carbine ’’ was pounding at. the other.
• • * *
Wybert Reeve was recently showing cinemato-
graph pictures at Adelaide Royal in which the great
bowler Jones could be seen mowing down English
wickets. The show didn’t catch on, and as only 12
people rolled up one fatal night the doors were shut.
N.S.W. recently made 803 against S A., mainly off Jones
aforesaid, and NVyhert Reeve is only just beginning to
realise that that recent disaster made the public feel too
sick to come along and gaze on the now faded champion's
old-time performances. The spectacle of what Jones
was only made what Jones is all the harder to bear.
* * • *
M.L. bruiser, Otto Cribb, after a big spell (for
him) is in the lists for a go with Harry Dawson at Golden
Gate A.C. (Sydney) this Saturday. Dawson is a big strong
fellow who owns a good pair of “dukes,” so that the
result is no moral. Cribb has a contract on with Ned
Burden for the middle of next month, and is arranging a
fight or two in between down at Melbourne to keep him
from getting blue-mouldy.
• • * •
Champion heavy-weight pug black, Peter Felix,
has disavowed all connection with manager Billy
M’Clain’s great bluff about fighting two men in one ring.
Felix says one man at a time is good enough for him
(writer’s opinion too), and has posted £5O to back him-
self. Word comes from W.A. that a Kalgoorlie sport will
guarantee £250 for another meeting between Felix and
Doherty, and that the latter can find £lOO side wager;
hut the big colored chap replies that he will only fight in
Melbourne.

“ K.” : Want to know a more profitable calling
than that of a champion bruiser. Jeffries got £6085 for
beating Sailor Sharkey recently, while the loser’s whack
totted up £2028. Biograph pictures of the fight are
valued at €lOO,OOO, of which each man is to receive a
third. What for? Neither is skilful in the sense that
Jackson, Foley, and Fitzsimmons were. It was a question
as to which w-ould stand the most pounding. They
finished even, but the referee gave Jeffries the palm, and
bas had a nest of hornets buzzing about his ears ever
«ince.

“W.” writes: “The Sydney sporting public
•shouldn’t miss the first mounted parade of the Mounted
Infantry portion of second N.S. Wales war-contingent.
Upwards of 300 horses have been in the lines at camp for
•over a week, and saddles being not yet available they
mostly did nothing but fill up with hard feed and get fit
to jump out of their shoes. Also a large percentage of
the ‘ Mounted Foot ’ don’t know a horse’s hoof from its
•eyebrow. The ‘offieahs’ who tested the riding capabili-
ties of volunteers were satisfied with some remarkable
expositions of horsemanship, hut their best effort was
their rejection for-being unable to ride of rough-rider,
polo-plavcr, and all-round champion Riverina horseman,
Frank Rudd, of May. Same officers, when sorting out
the Australian Horse mounts regarded as unfit to do
■cavalry work, for exchange with the best Mounted
Infantry nags, sorted out amongst others a little black
borse, and then promptly picked him out of the Infantry
lines as a good cavalry mount, and sent him back to re-
place himself.”

A. J. C. inquiry, Thursday last, re alleged in-
consistent running of sensational Kogarah-trained Mora
(Fid* BtJLt,RTiN', par. 6 1 4900), resulted in exoneration of
owner, “Teddy” Knight, and trainer, “ Dick ” O'Connor,
as it was shown that former put £3OO on the mare for
Xmas Handicap; but rider (Frank Fielder) was “sent
up ” for three years. This action requires a lot of under-
standing. Xmas Handicap was run on Roxing Day, and
had anything cronk occurred then, it should have been
seen and dealt with straightaway, and not left till same
nag took part in another race a week later with a different
jockey in the saddle. Ample evidence is forthcoming
that Fielder ptre put a century on Mora himself, and had
a “pony” in owner’s commission for the race wherein
his son Is alleged to have earned the punishment meted
■out. Truly, the ways of the A. J. C. are at times peculiar,

What a great game “ backing ’em ” is for
punters was never better evidenced than at Moorefield
(Sydney) on Saturday. Five of twelve entrants started
in the big event. Guardsman, 6to 4, Fortuna, a point
longer, and Cavalier, 4 to 1 against, were the only nags
■deemed to have a chance ; yet old Wvalong (15 or 20 to 1
for the asking) came along at the right end and gathered
in the sweep and the added money (£100) by two lengths,
Small wonder that, Ikey waxes fat.
Tom Payten’s Loohiel filly Skein Dhu was made
an odds-on favorite for the Nursery Handicap at Moore-
field (Byd.) on Saturday last as soon as the books opened ;
but the {rood thin# did not come off. Lucifer, a powerful
Queensland-bred brown colt, by Archie, beat the favorite
fell the way. Gameboy, the winner of the Kogarah Stakes,
is a three-year-old by the Trenton horse Goodfellow, and
won like a racehorse. He is the first of Goodfellow’s
stock the Sydney public have had a win over. Up to the
fifth race the books had a bad time, but in the Moore-
fleld Handicap, the evergreen Wyalong got them some
back ; and the last race was secured by Surbiton, who
started absolutely unbacked.

luxuries to be obtained at the Randwick tea-rooms (New

Budgeree (Marvellous-F' ue), who once in the
long ago started favorite for a jlarihyrnong Plate, broke
all the watches at Flemington, and afterwards put up a
record at Hawkesbury, turned up again at Meredith
(Vic.) on Boxing Day, where he won a double, beating a
solitary opponent each time. This is his first win for six
years.

Whernside’s invincibility at Flemington was
again demonstrated on New Year's Day, when he carried
13st. 21b., and won with a stone in hand, starting at
evens. A peculiar thing is that Whernside has never
won at Caulfield.

The Miller stable must be getting pretty tired
of Carbinier, which delusive gee-gee, by the way, was
only leased from the breeder for the term of its racing
life. Carbinier was expected to atone for a number of
failures by winning the Bagot Handicap, at Flemington,
on New Year’s Day, but the son of Carbine ran like a
non-stayer. Talking of non-stayers, the mare Symmetry
bore out the character that W. R. Wilson »;ave her last
year, by tiring very perceptibly at the end of the
Standish Handicap. The Sfandish, 6 furlongs, was cut
out in something under 7&sec. —very fast time for the
Flemington course.

The Bulletin’s London correspondent writes :
—“ Kempton Park, over the straws and‘sticks, Nov. 30.
Atmosphere swamped in fog. Trains with stewards and
public arrived an hour late. Picturesque course and sur-
roundings. Fully 100 yards or so of the five races
could be seen, then the fog swallowed up the runners,
and after hiding them for some minutes, belched them
out again for the leaders to be recognised as they passed
the post. Five races were got through between 2 and 4
o’clock, the last being abandoned. * Fell in ’ over four
successive events with my friends, three London racing
editors, all three of us being loaded with dead poultry ;
but when Jimmy Brewer came out on Tornado 11. (J. G.
Clarke) for Sunbury Hurdle, 2 miles, patriotism saved
me, and much plunder came to my shrunken sack.
Jimmy won with graceful ease, and partly avenged his
mount’s recent disqualification for two races at Hawthorn
Hill, on ‘ informal entry.'”
* * * «
It is about time A.J.C. officials took a hint from
the Pony Clubs to which they have been paying so much
attention of late, and did a little towards providing a few
conveniences for their “ flat ” patrons. Every pony club
lets its “flatite” know the starters for each race and
their respective riders, and protects him as much as pos-
sible from ticket-snatchers, etc., but the superiah
A.J.C. absolutely refuses to consider the beastly
common person who parts his humble “ bob ” for
the honor of being yarded up in the centre
of Randwick course on race-days. There the
ticket-grabber, the pickpocket, and the seedy spieler
with the aged gamp, flourish undisturbed ; the scratch*
ings are hung up where no one can see them, unless he
has field-glasses; whilst all information as to riders, &c.,
is kept for the Paddoqjf and Leger reserves. In case
the A.J.C. shouldn't know', it is hereby informed that the
average “flatite” doesn’t take his field glasses to the
races—he leaves them at home, in the jewelled morocco-
case in his dressing-room. Also, that there are jockeys
riding under whom a Carbine couldn’t win a hack-race,
and it’s handy to Jearn what nags they are on, so as to
know what not to back. The “ flatite ” is allowed to pay
one shilling for a large and mostly useless race-book,
however, and toe’s a long-suffering person to stand it.
• * * *
Told of a certain turf institution in Barataria
that when, not very long ago, a crack jockey was carpeted
and ordered to “stand down,” he came out, with blank
amazement depicted on every line of his classic mug, and
said, “ ’Strewth, if that ain’t the ’ottest thing I ever
struck ; why, the last four mounts I had for one o’ them
chaps that disqualified me were as stiff as corpses.” Also
said of an Ikev’s clerk, hauled up before the same body
for laying the odds at a tabooed meeting, that he openly
threatened, if things went against him, to name half-a-
dozen or more of those sitting in judgment and suggest
that they should square-up certain sporting debts, and
look to themselves in other directions so that they might
come into court clean-handed.

Amateurism as she is in Sydney. An amateur
athletic body gives two amateur champion swimmers a
cheque each virtually for swimming a match and forming
the big draw of a big gala. By-and-by the promoters
of the event give the whole thing away. An enquiry is
held, and the only one of the two top-notchers get-at-able
toes the mark. He exhibits a receipt showing that por-
tion of the sum said to have been presented went in the
purchase of 6ome article or other, and states that the
balance was to recoup him for leaving his employment to
train. He was acquitted without the slightest smudge
on his athletic character. How would this class of
amateur get on at ’Ome? Then we hear of a professional
swimmer being a vice-president of an amateur club, and
having as much to say in its management as the
purest pot-hunter on the roll. Further, an amateur
athletic club, desiring to include a professional event
on a programme of sports, asked permission of the
governing body in the usual way ; was refused ; hut did it
just the same and one of the A.A.A. officials started the
race. Dire consequences were threatened, but nothing
done. Worse than all, betting discovered to lie rampant
Ikeys taking a hand in the game and lily-white
amateurs found working a nice little scheme, something
of the you-win-to-day-and-111-win-'to-morrow natuie.
Governing body met in solemn conclave over the matter ;
evidence taken ; one prominent delegate admitted having
had a “ biton ” himself : a prominent official and a big
enthusiast resigned disgusted, and—nothing more done.
• * • •*
The unregistered courses down South have a
turf morality entirely their own. At one of them t'other
day a notorious defaulter, who has been warned off ail
registered courses, arranged a nice little game. The lad
on the well-backed favorite was nearly 3st. underweight.
When weighing-in, after winning in a canter, the jock, a
pigskin pigmy, carried saddle and 3 alleged stone of lead
on his left-hand little finder. Then a Hebraic yell went up.
One “ book" lost £4O. It is calculated that the uncon-
scious acoessory to the rascality carried 281 b. under-
weight. At another course not long ago, in a trotting
race, a horse—say Chopsticks—was fixed up to win and
given out as a good thing. Fiddlesticks, the only other
nag with a chance, was deadened by his trainer, who put
all his friends on to Chopsticks. At the last moment the
jock’s pals backed Fiddlesticks at a fair price for all they
were worth. Fiddlesticks won by a neck. An explana-
tion was sought by the trainer behind the stand. “I
couldn’t hold him back,” said the jock. “ You’re a
liar ; you rooked me.” The jock pulled off his coat and
shifted one side of the trainer’s face. The denouement
is that the trainer has been warned off the course for
life !
• * * *
No one who hasn’t “ been there ” himself has a
full idea of the luck, cunning, and determination needed
to make a win “backin’ ’em” at Sydney’s small pony
meetings. It is not enough to pick winners. If a hacker,
after he has sorted out the stiffs and found the only triers
in the race, is not very careful, “ bookie ” will probably
put the name of one of said stiffs on his ticket arid abuse
him blasphemously when he presents it for payment. If he
gets his money on the right horse, and it wins, and no one
has “ gone through ” him meanwhile for the ticket and
his watch and loose cash, and if he manages to dodge the
“ ticket-snatchers” (often employed l>y Ikey himself), and
gets said ticket safely into the hands of the “book,” that
individual will probably' dispute it whilst the “ pick-
pockets” swarm round, and, if he is a large, determined-
looking person with a heavy stick, and eventually does
get paid, his only hope is to button up his coat and run
for a cab.
• * * •
“Moslem”: Remarkable that the boys of
N.S.W. when at play (cricket, football, anything) do not
discard coats or waistcoats, and play in their shirts and
pants, as nippers elsewhere do. Have travelled much
through Australia, but only notice the peculiarity in
N.S.W. Is the mutual distrust of their forefathers in
their blood—or what?

11 p.m. in a country pub. Gamey-Leg flashes
the back of a £5-note, rolls it on counter spiral-shape,
and bets £1 nobody can say whether the number is odd
or even. A long stranger (dubbed at once “ The Slab ”)
agrees to bet the £l, if Game.v-Leg will let him feel the
spiral, as he “can do a bit of tliort readin’.” “ Right,”
says Gainey, “you can jest lay yer ’and on it.” Slab
does so, then retires to the passage “to think,” and
gently drifts out of back-door. Gainey, after waiting
some tiu.ie, picks up the. spiral and finds the Slab has
changed the fiver into a single.

With .the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables —plate,
jewellery, papers, etc.—at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (hack of Palings), and business there is proportion-
ately' brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
ate rates.*

, Queensland, October 26, 1898.
The bereaved husband who can remember with
