An episode of farewell :
A wharf crowd nowadays, witnessing the departure of
coastal steamers, is, as a rule, a composed and stoical
gathering, with emotions well under control, but now
and then a public manifestation of grief takes place quite
in the good old emigrant-ship-departure style of fare-
well. The other day as a steamer was hauling off front
Melbourne Queen’s Wharf, West bound, a young man,
who had up to that moment concealed his grief behind a
large red handkerchief, lost all control of himself. Re-
moving the handkerchief, he burst out into howling sobs
and immediately two girls on board responded in the
same key. Then the young man took off his hat and
threw it to the women. Both made a simultaneous grab
and printed kisses on it, and threw it back to their sob-
bing relative or friend on the wharf. The latter dexter-
ously caught the hat and shied it on board again. Then
one of the girls pressed it to her heart and threw a kiss,
but kept the hat. The steamer was now well away
from the wharf. The hatless and sobbing young
man began to wind the red handkerchief round
his head, pacing the wharf disconsolately. The girl
who had clutched the hat to her heart now bent
well over the taffrail, and holding out both hands,
shrieked. Lord Ullin wise, “ Dear old Patsy—good-bye-
good-bye!” The interest of the crowd had by now cen-
tred on the doings of Patsy and his two responsive lady
friends, and if anybody else had felt any grief of his
own, it was entirely subordinated in presence of the
great and poignant manifestations on the part of Patsy.
The final and despairing adieu from the girl who had
francticaily clutched the headgear to her heart proved too
much for Patsv. Before anybody could interfere, and to
the accompaniment of varied shrieks, he rushed to the
edge of the wharf and leaped into the churned-up, awful
waters of the turbid Yarra. When he came to the surface
he was fished out with a long boat-hook, and no sooner
was he got on to the wharf again than a constable stepped
up, and collaring the dripping Patsy, marched him off to
quod for— attempted suicide ! And after all, as it subse-
quently turned out, the girls were neither sweethearts
nor sisters, but sisters-in-law. One wonders what in the
name of Werther would have happened if the girl
who shrieked “Oood-bve” over the tab-rail had really
been the pledged sweetheart of the perforvid Patsy.
“ A merrier woman
Within the limit of becoming mirth,
None ever spent an hour’s talk withal;
ller eye begat occasion for her wit,
For every object that the one did catch,
The other turned to a mirth-moving jest,
Which the fair tongue (conceit’s expositor)
Delivered in such apt and gracious words
That aged ears played truant at her tales,
And younger hearers were quite ravished,
So sweet and voluble was her discourse.”

A novel device for lighting kerosene table-
lamps by electricity has been invented by Messrs.
Brandt Bros., Lamp Manufacturers, 23C Pitt-st.,
Sydney. By simply touching a button the lamp
is lighted. This can also be used for lighting their
new acetylene gas table-lamps. The firm are now
busy altering the ’bus lamps on the South Head
line to burn acetylene gas. They also send
pamphlets on application how to light-up dwel-
lings with acetylene gas.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Not one of the Melb. papers, daily or
weekly, has had the courage to tell the truth
about the Second Contingent. Practically any-
body not whiteheaded or deformed could get into
it. One youth, with more pluck than sense,
volunteered after spending half of the last 18
months on a sick bed with congestion of the lungs
—and was accepted 1 Another man practically
dying of consumption is bound for the Trans-
vaal, and the varicose veins, unsound teeth and
“riding injuries” which were said to be such
fatal objections at the time the first contingent
was sent, have been entirely overlooked. Worst
of all, however, is the hopeless ignorance of riding
betrayed by about a third of the men. Many of
these have manifestly ridden nothing but a bicycle
before, and their inability to keep upright on the
saddle of a walking horse causes the officers in
charge to use fearful language in private. To send
70 or 80 young fellows into a hostile country hold-
ing on by both hands to the front of the saddle is
a melancholy game, but “ the honor of England ”
demands that the painful fact should be hushed
up.

“ Cockney ” protests :
I didn’t try to “ bolster-up couponism ” in the letter
which has elicited a chorus of small yelps from small
shopkeepers. I merely said that any sort of open com-
petition in shop business has as much right to toleration
as any sort of open competition in all other businesses, and
that Parliament belittles itself by stooping to kill a system
which would die a natural death if left alone.
Advantage was taken of the New-Year Cale-
donian gathering at Sydney to boom once more
the “ glory ” of the Transvaal war. Premier
Lyne affirmed that it made his blood tingle
when he heard of the courage of the British
Empire in fighting two little nations whose
joint population is about one-third of Mel-
bourne’s. Dr. MacLaurin also tingled (it is
noticeable that the people who fought
hardest against Australian Federation arc
the loudest Jingoes of them all). “ The war,”
said Dr. MacLaurin, “has been shamefully
forced upon the British by the conspiracy at
Bloemfontein and Pretoria.” If the Tory
Councillor doesn’t know better than that, be
might consult English thinkers like Herbert
Spencer and James Bryce, who, with a much
better knowledge of the matter, affirm that the
boot is on the other leg. The conspiracy which
begot Jameson’s Raid and Chamberlain’s
Raid is an English conspiracy, and the only
one of which there is evidence.
“ Bill ” . On Christmas Eve, the ash
gentleman, who regularly once a week in the dead
of night snatches me from the arms of Morpheus,
left a printed memo, asking me not to forget him.
On the other side were the words: “ Use
Blankam’s Pills.” Present writer is of opinion
that if the quarries of Sinai were thoroughly
searched a mislaid commandment of the above
description would be found.
Tasmania, as befitteth the most Tory and
most abjectly grovelsome province of the
group, is the most frantically patriotic in the
matter of the Boer hunt. The newspapers are
issuing incorrect reprints of Kipling’s absent-
minded jinerle at fid. a copy, proceeds to go to the
Patriotic Fund. Any Boer sympathiser, or any-
body who looks like a Boer sympathiser, is in
danger of stoush. The other day a party of blue-
jackets from the warships in the Derwent wrecked
a poor old man’s garden because the loyal
and partially blind old citizen had, in
sheer ignorance, hoisted his Xmas flag up-
side down. The old chap wore a long beard in
addition to his other offence, and the frantic blue-
jackets, having heard that Boers always wore long
beards, concluded that every long beard must de-
note Boer proclivities, and opened fire accordingly.
The ancient hadn’t the faintest notion what it
was all about until most of his raspberry bushes
and fruit trees were scattered over the roadway.
Rumored that the Patriotic Fund Committee is to
be asked to pay the damage on the ground that it
arose out of patriotic fervor.
Queensland has had its usual Xrnas and
New Year butchery. One Snow, who lately re-
sided near St. George, had been “ keeping com-
pany ” for some time with Miss Goward, and last
week he appears to have strolled round during the
small hours, and without warning or parley shot
the young lady’s brother whom he found sleeping
on the verandah. Mrs. Goward, hearing the re-
port, rushed out and was shot with the other
barrel, anil then finished off with an axe. Mean-
while Miss Goward seems to have fled but was
pursued and overtaken by her lover. At day-
break they were found about a mile-
and-a-half away by a boy. He saw Snow
give the girl a drink and take one him-
self, from a bottle, which was afterwards found to
contain strychnine. Shortly after, the girl said,
“ I’m in terrible pain, Ted. Cut my throat to
put me out of pain.” And with the wondering
boy looking on, Snow cut the girl’s throat and
then attempted to cut his own. Finding he had
not sufficient nerve, he threw the knife and his
coat to the boy, and told him to take them to the
township. When the police and a doctor
arrived the murderer was dead also, apparently
from the effects of the poison. The emblems of
true love in Australia are a meat-axe, double-
barrelled gun, and poison bottle ; and true love
itself is apt to end either in butchery, because the
girl won't say yes, or marriage and then butchery
afterwards because she did say it. Also, in
murders committed through jealousy, the de-
mented one seems to be consumed with a burning
desire to wipe out all the family, down to the dog
and the tribal goat. Why the rejected Australian
can’t be content with one murder is hard to ex-
plain, and seems to suggest that we are developing
the East Indian proclivity to run amuck.

Mr. THOMAS MOOT MAN,

shattered nervous system was completely
cured and restored.
Statutory OSIS* Declaration.
I, THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows: —
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that,
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rass-
Mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which 1 hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALF ALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

PROSPECTUS OF THEH
GRASSY GULLY GOLD MINING CO. (No Liability).
CAPITAL - - £40,000.
In 40,000 Shares of £1 each, of which 10,000 are now offered for subscription, payable 2s. on application, 6d. on allotment, and the
balance as required.in calls of not more than Gd. per month.
All the money subscribed will be placed to the credit of the Company for working capital, the vendors taking only paid-up shares in full payment for the property.
DraEOTOKS : Hon. GK°. DAY, M.L.C., “ Arleston,” Petersham ;W. H. C. LOVELY, Esq., “ Cranbrook,” Rose Bav; CHARLES WENTWORTH DTLKE, Esq., Bligh Chambers, Sydney ; HUGH

AREA AND SITUATION.—This Co. is being formed to acquire, develop, and work the gold-leases constituting
the Grassy Gullv Gold Mine, comprising gold-leases 938 and 943 (Southern), marked No. 18 and 17 respectively on
plans, and situated' near -Currier close to the Shoal haven River about 18 nnles from Nowra, containing seven (7)
acres, also a residence area and all mining plant and requisites now in use, under an agreement dated the 13th De-
cember, 1899, between W. 11. C. Lovely, Hugh McKenzie, and James Barron of the one part, and Charles Wentworth
Duke for and on behalf of the Company as Trustee of the other pare, whereby the vendors will receives 30,000 fully-
paid shares.
OBJ LOTS. —The object of the flotation is to provide working capital to properly develop the mine and enable a
large output to be obtained. The amount offered is deemed ample for the purpose.
. VALUE OF ORE BODIES.—The mine is the first which was discovered at Grassy Gully. Some of the younger
mines are being worked with apparently good results, thus proving the continuity of the ore bodies now being
opened up in the parent mine. The value and prospects of the mine are clearly demonstrated by the smelting
returns and report appended hereto, and from them and the ordinnry reports is taken the following information :
SMELTING RETURNS.
Nearly all the ore treated to date has been taken out of the development works on the mine, only one short
stope having been made and no reef having been exposed but that at the two shafts, and almost all of that taken out
proved very rich.
Three reefs have been found on the mine, but only two have been opened up.
REEF I.—On the main reef which underlies slightly to the west a shaft has been sunk to a depth of 128 ft. The
lode averages 4ft. in width, but the rich vein, which yields the best ore, is loin, in width arid increased in value from
Boz. of fine gold per ton near the surface to over 21oz. at 120 ft., excluding silver.
At 117 ft. a fault occurred, but a few feet further another chute of ore was met, yielding 3oz. per ton, which in-
creased to within a few grains of lOoz. per ton at 128 ft., the old chute apparently being left to one side of the present
bottom of the shaft.
. In sinking this shaft the last 40ft. about 80 tons of ore have been raised, which realised about £4OOO at the smelt-
ing works, after making deductions for loss in treatment.
REEF 2.—On No. 2 reef a shaft has been sunk 40ft., in which this reef averages 12in. to 15in. iniwidlh and 2oz. to
4oz. 16d wt. of fine gold per ton.
Driving, crosscutting, and tunnelling hag been begun, but no ore body of importance has yet been proved by
these works.
DUMP.—From the mullock dump, where there are many hundred tons of stuff, have been screened 60 tons of
fines, which yielded about £5OO net at the smelting works.
BACKS.—As the shaft is situated approximately 60ft. up the side of a steep hill the height of backs available for
stoping will quickly increase as the south drive advances.
TIMBER AND WATER are abundant in the immediate vicinity. The water obtained from the mine is not pure
enough for drinking, and for this purpose it is obtained from the creek and stored for the summer. A good supply is
also obtainable from the Shoalhaven River, about a mile distant.
RIVER CARRIAGE is available for a distance of about fifteen (15) miles above Nowra, and the ore is conveyed
by steamer to Nowra and thence to Dapto by train.
SECOND CLASS ORE.—Average value per ton (less deduction): 6oz. 19dwt. 15-69gr.—£28 7s. 2d.
TOTALS.—49tons 4cwt. Iqr. Slbs. 3440 z. 14dwt. 6Jgr.—£l39s 17s. 2d.
THIRD GRADE ORE.—Average Value (less deduction): 2ozs. 7dwts. Ogrs.—£9 7s. 6d.
TOTALS.—77tons 6dwt. Iqr. lllbs. lSlozs. 2dwts. 17grs.—£724 14s. Id.
Report of the Grassy Gully Gold Mine,
ny JAMES EEILSOy, M.E.
MESSRS. LOVELY & McKENZTE.
Dear Sirs, — In compliance with your request that I should give an unbiassed report of the value and capabili-
ties of your property, known as the GRASSY GULLY OOLD MINE, I have the honour of supplying you with the
following particulars :■ —
THE MlNE.—This is situated at Grassy Gully, 17 miles distant, in a westerly direction from the township of
Nowra. on the South Coast. The area held is seven acres, being two leases of four and three acres combined. There
are TWO DISTINCT LODES PROVED TO BE PAYABLE, AND OF VERY HIGH GRADE ORE, AND ONE
LARGE OUTCROP PROVED gold-bearing in the three-acre lease, and it is mv opinion that this will turn out to be
a payable lode when it is further developed.
No. 1 SHAFT is where the most development, has heen done, and is termed the No. 1 Lode. The Shaft is now
down 128 ft. on the underlay. The formation of the lode between the walls is 4ft. in width, but the portion that
carries the richest of the gold averages 10 inches in width, which is on the foot-wall side. At 117 ft. in depth a slide
came in front the south, dipping north at an angle of 25°, and this has been the means of shifting the rich chute of
gold 40ft. further to the north. Immediately under the slide there was a change of ore, which was of poorer grade,
the lode, however, still continued in its usual course to the westward. The poor ore continued for Bft. in depth, but,
during the last 3ft. of sinking, it has improved considerably, and has every appearance of opening out into another
rich chute of ore. I may state that this chute is quite a different thing to what we were working above the slide,
and should it turn out as I anticipate it will be the means of enhancing the value of your property considerably.
STOPING AND DRIVING.—The extent of stoping and driving is somewhat limited, owing to the short period
the property has been worked. At the 78ft,. level there is a drive 36ft. south and one 30ft. north on the course of
the lode, the lode showing about the same size in both ends. What stoping has been done is to the north of the
shaft above the 78ft. level, and is 15ft. in length by 30ft,. in height, and all the ore that came from the stopes and
sinking of the shaft was of a very high grade, averaging from 3 to 220 z. per ton.
No. 2 LODE.—What is termed No 2 lode is situated chains to the east of No. 1 lode. There is a shaft sunk
to a depth of 38ft. on the underlay. The ore averages from Ift. to 15in. in width. The ore is of fair grade, averaging
front 2oz. to 4oz. 16dwt. per ton. The lode is still going down strong in the bottom and the chute of gold is dipping
south. We are now engaged repairing shaft and erecting whip, so as to further develope this lode.
NO. 3 LODE.—What is termed No. 3 Lode is situated on the three-acre lease, and is one chain west of the No. 1
Lode, and as 1 have already stated, by surface indications showing, it appears to me to be the main Assure lode, but
no work having heen done here it is impossible for me to give any details.
MACHINERY.—The hauling appliance you have at present is of a primitive nature, and what is wanted badly is
a STEAM WINDING WINCH AND A SMALL FORCE PUMP, so as to be able to cope with the bottom water that
we must, naturally expect to meet with as depth is attained. With this appliance erected on the ground, I HAVE
NO HESITATION WHATEVER IN SAVING THAT YOU HAVE A MOST VALUABLE PROPERTY, and that it
would increase the dividends most considerably.
1 have the honor to be, gentlemen, your obedient servant,
4th November, 1899. (Signed) JAMES NEILSON, Manager.
Remittances to be made payable to the Secretary, and ONE of the Arst two mentioned Sydney Directors.

Hope and Succour for the Afflicted.
The Case of
MRS. MARIA HAPGOOD.
Reported Jor the “ Marrickville Express”)
The reporter had no difficulty in finding
the residence of Mrs. Maria Hapgood at
I*o. 60 Shepherd-street, Marrickville, as it
is not far from the Emnore tram terminus.
This lady, a well-known resident, was
busily . attending her well-kept flower
garden, in which she takes a great pride,
in answer to an enquiry as to whether she
had not greatly benefited by the use of
Clemeiits Tonic, and would she object to
giving her experience, Mrs. Hapgood
replied :
“ Certainly not; I owe so much to that
remedy for all the good it has done to me
and mine that the least I can do is to give
my testimony, and I recommend it in every
case that I think suitable.”
“ How did your own sickness arise ?”
“ I do noc know the cause, but three
years ago I began to experience a number
of distressing symptoms. My digestion Was
seriousiy impaired, so that my food caused
me pain and distress At night I could not
rest, my sleep was broken and irregular.
Then X had a terrible pain in the back
across the shoulders, and I was nearly
crippled by neuralgic pains in my hands I
and wrists, so that it was with the greatest J
difficulty I could struggle through my
housework. But there was worse to come.
There was a painful enlargement in my
throat, and the back of my tongue became
so swollen as neatly to fill my mouth, and
joined to these troubles I had great depres-
sion of spirits. I did not seem to care how
things went. I went to a friend, a chemist,
and he recommended me a much puffed aud
advertised remedy, also a mixture of his
j own compounding ; but though I took them
l both as directed neither did me any good.
My husband insisted on my going to our
family doctor, of whom he had a very high
opinion.”
“ What did the doctor say ?”
“ He gave me no hope or encouragement,
and though I told him all my symptoms, he
did not tell me his opinion of my case or
what was the matter w r ith me. Of course
he gave me a prescription, which I had
made up and took without any benefit
whatever. Then it chanced that I came
across one of the pamphlets of Clements
Tonic, and although I could see no case
there just like my own, it appeared to me
Clements Tonic would be likely to do me
good. When I called on a ohemist to
purchase a bottle of Clements Tonic he
strongly advised me not to do so, and urged
me to take what he called ‘ my own tonic.’
I knew better, he only wanted the extra
profit; I had been had that way before, so
I waa resolute in my determination and
insisted on having tho genuine article—
Clements Tonic. ”
“ And what happened ?” J
“ To my great surprise, I derived great
benefit at once; and I may say that, practi-

The Stranding' of the “ Cheerful
Mary.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God dug the sea, and man made it a slavery,
And set himself to walk it—’stead of staying in
his cave ;
And the best of all the landsmen leave the land
and all the knavery
Of the man who lives in offices—to plough the
briny wave.
Man thought the ship, and his thought ’came
fact upon the ways—
Upon the skids that sicken with the launch-
grease fed too free—
And the Marine Surveyor when her stiffening’s in
a dozen ways
Makes an overdeck inspection and bequeaths her
to the sea.
God made the “ old man,” the compass, and the
telegraph
That rings its terror from the bridge, “ Stand by!
Astern ! Ahead ! ”
But Nicky made the envious mates who criticise
from keel to gaff,
And who think a skipper’s useful —if he happens
to be dead.
And Envy is a fearful thing—it gets you down
and worries you,
And it makes your eye-white spleen-flecked, and
you don’t enjoy your drink,
And it slacks your nervous system—and a bit of
danger flurries you ;
As see the fate of Shoreland of the steamship
“Cheerful Stink.”
She was launched, the “Cheerful Mary”; but
through carrying guanner
Through cross and funnel currents round the
Leeuwin’s gusty blink ;
Through odoring the salt seas from Beagle Bank
to Banner,
And from Pelsart Group to Nobby’s, she was
called the “ Cheerful Stink.”
At Wreck Point, Pelsart Island, where the Dutch-
men’s ships were wrecked,
The Zeewyck and Batavia—and their crews made
death’s elect—
Where Mr. Billy Broadbent owns the phosphates
of the past,
And the guano of the future (if the dear, kind
birds can last).
The square-tailed petrel comes ashore to make
more trade for Bill,
And noddy-terns and wide-awakes—they help the
deep ships fill;
To conserve that farm of phosphates they land —
they breed—they nest,
And Billy prays for strength for them to do their
very best.
The green-turtle and the beche-de-mcr, the quail
and parrot-fish.
Bill approves of —but the petrels have his heart’s
desire and wish ;
And in South Latitude ’tween twenty-eight and
twenty-nine,
Where the sea-breeze dripping fruit-scents comes
singing from the Line,
’Tween 28° and 29°—supposing you are there —
You’ll hear the noddy noise a-land, and Broad-
bent’s phosphate prayer.
For years the “ Cheerful Mary ” leached the salt
from out the wind,
And her awful smell was visible, and streamed
like smoke behind,
When the Orient and P. & O. ran down the
Leeuwin light,
And suddenly upon the sea an odour black as
night
Broke up the deck flirtation of the awful British
swell,
And a sickening fog crept o’er them like the stolid
smoke of Hell—
And some men quickly lit their pipes, and some
men took to drink—
They knew that in the darkness near there
steamed the “ Cheerful Stink.’’
But she left the trade in phosphates, and they
tried her in the North,
Though her cabin was thesteerage and her second-
class was fourth—
As “a fast and favorite passenger ’’ they tried her
in the Noith,
And the gales retired from business when the
“ Cheerful Stink ” put forth.
She would never lose on land or sea, or in the
docks of Hell,
By painting or by burning her, her own distinc-
tive smell;
And they made her carry bone-dust for every
other week
To Lytton Wharf, at Brisbane, from the meat-
works at Lake’s Creek.
Now Shoreland was the mate of her —a man with
envy green—
And he sneered at all the skipper did to work the
old machine.
The old man drank much squarefaoe—it was his
only fault—
(And it’s vice that’s mostly virtue—is that lime-
kiln thirst for malt.
Shakespeare—Raleigh Danton Herrick—Billy
Kidd and Mirabeau,
They washed in water, and they sent the wine
where it should go ;
And the big man, right through history—he who
does, or plans, or thinks,
Is a term for contradictions if he fails to love
strong drinks—
As witness yours sincerely—the author of this
strain
Who ha 3 been drunk a hundred times, and hopes
to be again.)
And Shoreland was teetotal—in everything but
sneers,
But at that as great a drunkard as ever schemed
for beers.
And when Shoreland’s chance had come for him
to take the smells one trip,
He found that being teetotal won’t suffice to work
a ship.
By the Meat Works Wharf the loading came
down to Shoreland’s craft—
For the old man was offended, and he cursed her
fore and aft—
The owners wrote that boozing wasn’t the work
for which he signed,
And the old inan drank more squareface and in
squareface he resigned.
So Shoreland took her South, and he’d a most
turgeseent head,
Though he saw the river narrower than from the
fok’sle head ;
He had seen the old man swing her round Egg
Island and the Ridge,
And the river then looked wider than it seemed
to from the bridge ;
But he took her out all safely, and passed
Timandra Bank,
And her breath lay over Keppel Rocks in fog and
vapour rank.
He brought her south by almanac, by chart, by
sun, by Pugh,
But he hadn’t more than a few brains—and didn’t
use his few.
And when he reached Cape Moreton he knew how
thin his blood,
For he’d struck The Wide Contingency—a first-
class, old-man flood 1
“ At minute intervals revolves a first catoptric
light ”
This was his only certainty of Pugh that raging
night—
The old man (had he been there) had put her out
to sea ;
But Shoreland’s devil hustled him, and Shoreland
wasn’t free ;
And_ the buoys had changed, and Shoreland aohed
in his turgeseent head —
On the starboard hand the buoys were black—on
the port hand they were red
The beacons not triangular and blaok, but black
and square,
And he saw the shining emerald of wreck-buoys
everywhere.
But he kept along as best he might, in soorn of
luck or lead,
And read his Pugh until it seemed all nonsense in
his head ;
And so read on as priests may read, tolling eternal
beads,
And the mind within, as the world without,
hearkens never nor heeds.
And Shoreland read—“AroundSmith Rock there
shall I find foul ground,
—Smith’s Rock is safe when Tempest bears Sou’
Sou’ West of the Sound.
Hug the Cape foot on Flinders Rocks, borrow on
the North hand
(When strangers try the Middle Run they’ll likely
meet the sand),
Keep the South Glass-House North by East of
Hutchins and the Shoal—
(Mt. Tempest over Yellow Patoh locates a
Sohnapper hole) —
For Raper Shoal bring Cartwright Point in line
with Coolmn Hill ”
At this poor Shoreland’s tottering brain refused
itself to fill
With pointers—good for any sort of weather
made for use.
But not for brawls of floods, and gales, and Hell,
and damp abuse.
He read of Tungaluma and of triune holophotes,
Of North Point and Comboyuro, and the Channel
of the Boats ;
And he seemed to get much further from the
longed-for Lytton shore,
And he swore he’d take a billet with Contingencies
no more,
And in his dire extremity the habits of the years
Slipped from him like pyjamas, and he saw the
use of beers.
The ship was steaming half a-head—which kept
her on —no more,
For the muddy river sped to sea like martens on
the shore.
So Shoreland drew the squarefaoe, and he poured
a second mate’s tot
(An inch above the “pretty,”) n he drank the
blessed lot—
Then in a flash he saw the way in scorn of light or
lead,
He took the wheel and telegraphed the engines
“ Full Ahead ! ”
And drove so through the blinding night across
the liquid plain
Through half a fog and half a drift emulsion of
the rain.
Making half-speed with “ full-ahead ” he breasted
bight and point,
Made Double Isle and nearly gave Wolf Rock his
bone and joint;
He scraped in Pumice Channel and he tore the
mangrove slime,
And finally 1 she settled in the dead backwater
rime.
I he flood ran off the longlands as th e Barron Falls
go down
To Trinity and Redlynch and the steaming tropic
town ;
And the stranded ship’s loud odour it ascended
Breakfast Creek,
And it killed some sick in Brisbane, and the
strong were water-weak.
In power each hour as the sun waxed full the
well-known smell arose,
And the “Cheerful Mary’s” agent traced the cast-
away by nose;
And they left her where she took the mud to warn
ships off her deck—
And that smell’s the finest coastguard that ever
stopped a wreck.
So Shoreland lost his ticket for many a hard-up
day.
And he’s captain of a ferry from the Quay to
Neutral Bay—
He runs ten-minute voyages, and his faith in
Pugh has fled,
And if his mate has notions that mate had best be
dead—
For Shoreland’s down on Envy since he rounded
last the Ridge,
And he knows that every channel’s but a foot
wide—from the bridge !
Cairns (N.Q.).
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

Why shouldn’t a man be hale and healthy at
seventy ? In the evening of life, after the battle
has been fought, why shouldn’t he enjoy the resb
he has earned ?
Ask the man of seventy why he stoops, and what
is the weak spot in his constitution. Nine times out
of ten he will reply, “ Oh, it’s my back. It isn’t
strong, and it pains me to move or stoop, or
straighten up.”
Ah, but is it really the back ? The back does
ache, but the real ache is deeper than that. It’*
the kidneys, for they lie just under the small of
the back, and the hurry and bustle and worry of
a long life in the fight for wealth and position
have finally told on the kidneys, and they hav#
become weakened.
Disturbances of the water have have appeared—
there is a sediment in the kidney secretions.
Disease has attacked him in the weakest part, and
will never stop of its own accord. Yet it can be
checked and cured even at seventy by Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills, for they heal the kidneys, they act
on the kidneys only, and will clear the secretions,
strengthen the back, and check the most danger*
ous of all diseases, kidney disease, even in a man
of seventy.
W. D. Harvey, of Stawell, whose picture is given
above, is seventy years old. He has lived in Stawell
for forty-eight years. He says“ My back gave
out and I had plenty of proof that my kidneys had
become diseased. In the first place, the pain in
my back was confined to the small of the back,
just over the kidneys. I could stoop over only
with difficulty, and any exertion brought an
increase of pain. Of course lam an old man, and
this pain was hard to bear. At length the kidneys
began to act wrong. I was restless at night, and
I noticed that the secretions from the kidneys
left a sediment, and were of a high color. There
could be no mistaking these symptoms, and I got
some of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills at once. I
took them with splendid results. I derived great
benefit from them, and, at my age, this is gratify-
ing. My back is better, and the kidney secretions
again natural, and I can highly recommend them
to anyone suffering from backache or any otln r
signs of kidney disease. The above is true, and
you may publish the facts.”

A Rouseabout of Rouseabouts.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A HOT, breathless, blinding sunrise—the sun hav-
ing appeared suddenly above the ragged edge of
the barren scrub like a great disc of molten steel.
No hint of a morning breeze before it, no sign on
sky or earth to show that it is morning—save the
position of the sun.
A clearing in the scrub—bare as though the
surface of the earth were ploughed and harrowed,
and dusty as the road. Two oblong huts—one for
the shearers and one for the rouseabouts m
about the centre of the clearing (as if even the
mongrel scrub had shrunk away from them) built,
end-to-end, of weatherboards, and roofed with
galvanised iron. Little ventilation; no verandah ;
no attempt to create, artificially, a breath of air
through the buildings. Unpainted, sordid-
hideous. Outside, heaps of ashes still hot and
smoking. Close at hand, “butcher’s shop —a
bush and bag break wind in the dust, under a couple
of sheets of iron, with offal, grease and clotted
blood blackening the surface of the ground about
it Greasy, stinking sheepskins hanging every-
where with blood-blotched sides out. Crease
inches deep in great black patches about the fire-
place ends of the huts, where wash-up and ‘ boil-
ing ’’ water is thrown.
Inside, a rough table on supports driven into
the black, greasy ground floor, and formed ot
flooring boards, running on uneven lines the
length of the hut from within about Oft. ot the
fire-place. Lengths of single six-inch boards or
slabs on each side, supported by the projecting
ends of short pieces of timber nailed aoross the
legs of the table to serve as seats.
On each side the hut runs a rough framework,
like the partitions in a stable ; each compartment
battened off to about the size of a manger, and
containing four bunks, one above the other, on
each side—their ends, of course, to the table.
Scarcely breathing space anywhere between.
Fireplace, the full width of the hut in one
end, where all the cooking and baking for 40
or 50 men is done, and where flour, sugar, &c., are
kept in open bags. Fire, like a very furnace.
Buckets of tea and coffee on roasting beds of coals
and ashes on the hearth. Pile of “ brownie ”on
the bare black boards at the end of the table.
Unspeakable aroma of 40 or 50 men who have
little inclination and less opportunity to wash
their skins, and have to soak some of the grease out
of their clothes on Saturday afternoons or Sundays.
And clinging to all, and over all, the smell
of the dried, stale yolk of wool—the stink of rams!
• • * *
“I am a rouseabout of the rouseabouts. I
have fallen so far that it is beneath me to try to
climb to the proud position of “ringer” of the
shed. I had that ambition once, when I was the
softest of green hands ; but then I thought I
could work out my salvation and go home. I’ve
got used to hell since then. I only get twenty-five
shillings a week (less station store charges) and
tuckerthere. I have been seven years west of the
Darling and never shore a sheep. Why don’t I
learn to shear, and so make money ? What should
I do with more money ? Get out of this and go
home? I would never go home unless I had
enough money to keep me for the rest of my life,
and I’ll never make that Out Back. Otherwise
what should I do at home ? And how should I
account for the seven years, if I were to go home .
Could I describe shed life to them and explain
how I lived. They think shearing only takes a
few days of the year—at the beginning of summer.
They’d want to know how I lived the rest of the
year. Could I explain that I “ jabbed trotters ’
and was a “tea-and-sugar burglar" between
sheds. They’d think I’d been a tramp and a
beggar all the time. Could I explain anything so
that they’d understand ? I’d have to be lying
the time and would soon be tripped up and found
out. For, whatever else I have been I was never
much of a liar. No, I’ll never go home.
“ I become momentarily conscious about day-
light. The flies on the track got me into that
habit, I think—they start at day-break—when the
mosquitoes give over.
“ The cook rings a bullock bell.
“The cook is fire-proof. The cook is as a fiend
from the nethermost sheol and needs to be. No
man sees him sleep, for he makes bread, or, worse,
brownie at night, and he rings a bullock bell
loudly at half-past five in the morning to rouse us
from our animal torpors. Others, the sheep-ho s,
the engine-drivers at the shed or wool-wash, call
him, if he does sleep. They manage it in shifts,
somehow, and sleep somewhere, sometime. We
haven’t time to know. The cook rings the
bullock bell and yells the time. It was the same
time five minutes ago—or a year ago. No time to
decide which. I dash water over my head and
face and slap handfuls on my eyelids—gummed
over aching eyes —still blighted by the yoke o
woo l —grey, greasy-feeling water from a cut-down
kerosene tin which I sneaked from the cook and
hid under my bunk and had the foresight to refill
from the cask last night, under cover of warm,
still, suffocating darkness. Or was it the night
before last ? Anyhow, it will be sneaked from me
to-day, and from the crawler who will collar it to-
morrow, and * touched ’ and 4 lifted ’ and collared
and recovered by the cook, and sneaked back
again, and cause foul language, and fights, may-
be, till we ‘ cut-out.’
“No; we didn’t have sweet dreams of home
and mother, gentle poet —nor yet of babbling
brooks and sweethearts, and love’s young dream.
We are too dirty and dog-tired when we tumble
down, and have too little time to sleep it off. We
don’t want to dream those dreams out here—they’d
only be nightmares for us, and we’d wake to re-
member. We mustn't remember here.
“ At the edge of the timber a great galvanised-
iron shed, nearly all roof, coming down to within
6ft. 6in. of the “board” over the “shoots.”
Cloud of red dust in the dead timber behind,
going up —noon-day dust. Fence covered with
skins ; carcases being burned ; blue smoke going
straight up as in noonday. Great glossy (greasy-
glossy) black crows “ flopping ” around.
“ The first syren has gone. We hurry in single
files from opposite ends of rouseabouts’ and
shearers’ huts (as the paths happen to run to the
shed) gulping hot tea or coffee from a pint-pot in
one hand and biting at a junk of brownie in the
other.
“ Shed of forty hands. Shearers rush the pens
and yank out sheep and throw them like demons ;
grip them with their knees, take tip machines,
jerk the strings ; and with a rattling whirring
roar the great machine-shed starts for the day.
“ ‘Go it, you tigers ! ” yells a tar-boy.
‘ Wool away i ’ ‘ Tar ! ’ ‘ Sheep Ho ! ’ We rush
through with a whirring roar till breakfast-time.
“We seize our tin plate from the pile, knife
and fork from the candle-box, and crowd round
the camp-oven to jab out lean chops, dry as chips,
boiled in fat. Chops or curry-and-rice. There
is some growling and cursing. We slip into our
places without removing our hats. There’s no
time to hunt for mislaid hats when the whistle
noes Row of hat brims, level, drawn over eyes,
or thrust black-according to characters or
temperaments. Thrust back denotes a lucky
absence of brains, I fancy. Row of folks g° h
up, or jabbing, or poised, loaded, waiting for last
mouthful to be bolted.
“ We nick up, sweep, tar, sew wounds, catch
sheep that break from the pensjump down and
pick up those that can’t rise at the bottom of the
shoots, ‘ bring- my-combs - from - the -
yer,’ laugh at dirty jokes and sweat an ,
in short, are the ‘ will-yer slaves, body and sou ,
of seven, six, five or four shearers, according to
the distance from the rolling tables.
“The shearer on the board at the shed is a
demon. He gets so much a , hl ‘" dre^ J Jjes
255. a week. He is not supposed, by the' rules
of the shed, the Union, and humanity to take a
sheen out of the pen after the bell goes (smoke-ho,
meals, or knock-off), but his watch is hanging on
the post, and he times himself to get so many
sheep out of the pen before the bell S oes > 3 d ,
more —the ‘ bell-sheep ’-as it is ringing. We have
to take the last fleece to the table and leave our
board clean. We go through the day of eight
hours in runs of about an hour and 20 minutes
between smoke-ho’s—from 6to 6. If th ®
shore 200 instead of 100, they d get £2 a day
instead of £l, and we’d have twice as muoh work
to do for our 255. per week. But the shearers are
racing each other for tallies. And it s no use
kicking. There is no God here and no Unionism
(though we all have tickets). Bub what am I
growling about ? I’ve worked from 6to 6 with no
smoke-ho’s for half the wages, and food we
wouldn’t give the sheep-ho dog. It s the bush
growl, born of heat, flies, and dust. I d gro
now if I had a thousand a year. We must growl,
swear, and some of us drink to d.t. s, or go mad
sober.
“Pants and shirts stiff with grease aa though a
couple of pounds of soft black putty were spread
on with a painter’s knife. ... ,
“ No, gentle bard !—we don’t sing at our work.
Over the whirr and roar and hum all day long,
and with iteration that is childish and irritating
to the intelligent greenhand, float unthinkable
adjectives and adverbs, addressed to jumbucks,
jackaroos, and mates indiscriminately. And
worse words for the boss over board—behind his
back !
“ I came of a Good Christian Family perhaps
that’s why I went to the Devil. When I came
out here I’d shrink from the man who used foul
language. In a short time I used it with the
worst. I couldn’t help it.
“That’s the way of it. If I went baok to a
woman’s country again I wouldn’t swear. Id
forget this as I would a nightmare. That s the
way of it. There’s something of the larrikin
about us. We don’t exist individually. Off the
board, away from the shed (and eaoh other) we
are quiet—even gentle.
“ A great-horned ram, in poor condition, but
shorn of a heavy fleece, picks himself up at the
foot of the “ shoot,” and hesitates, as if ashamed
to go down to the other end where the ewes are.
The most ridiculous object under Heaven.
“A tar-boy of fifteen, of the bush, with a
mouth so vile that a street-boy, same age, (up
with a shearing uncle) kioks him behind —having
proved his superiority with his fists before the
shed started. Of which unspeakable little fiend
the roughest shearer of a rough shed was heard to
say, in effect, that if he thought there was the
slightest possibility of his becoming the father of
such a boy he’d take drastic measures to pre-
vent the possibility of his becoming a proud
parent at all. .
“ Twioe a day the cooks and their familiars
carry buckets of oatmeal-water and tea to the
shed, two each on a yoke. We cry, ‘ Where are
you coining to, my pretty maids’’
“ In ten minutes the surfaces of the buckets are
black with flies. We have given over trying to
keep them clear, We stir the living cream aside
with the bottoms of the pints, and guzzle gallons,
and sweat it out again. Occasionally a shearer
pauses and throws the perspiration from his fore-
head in a rain.
“Shearers live in such a greedy rush of excite-
ment that often a strong man will, at a prick of
the shears, fall in a death-like faint on the board.
“ We hate the Boss-of-the-Board as the shearers’
* slushy ’ hates the shearers’ cook. I don’t know
why. He’s a very fair boss.
“ He refused to put on a traveller yesterday and
the traveller knocked him down. He walked
into the shed this morning with his hat back and
thumbs in waistcoat—a tribute toman’s weakness.
He threatened to dismiss the traveller’s mate, a
bigger man, for rough shearing—a tribute to
man’s strength. The shearer said nothing. We
hate the boss because he is boss, but we respect
him because he is a strong man. He is as hard
up as any of us, I hear, and has a sick wife and a
large, small family in Melbourne. God judge us
all!
“ There is a gambling-school here, headed by
the shearers’ cook. After tea they head-’em, and
advance checks are passed from hand to hand,
and thrown in the dust until they are black.
When it’s too dark to see with nose to the ground
they go inside and gamble with cards. Sometimes
they start Saturday afternoon, heading ’em till
dark, play cards all night, start again heading ’em
Sunday afternoon, play cards all Sunday night,
and sleep themselves sane on Monday, or go to
work ghastly—like dead men.
“ Cry of ‘ Fight!’we all rush out. But there
isn’t much fighting. Afraid of murdering each
other. I’m beginning to think that most bush
crime is due to irritation born of dust, heat, and
flies.
“ The smothering atmosphere shudders when the
sun goes down. We call it the sunset breeze.
“ Saturday night or Sunday we’re invited into
the shearers’ hut. There are songs that are not
hymns and reoitations and speeches that are not
prayers.
“ Last Sunday night: Slush lamps at long
intervals on table. Men playing cards, sewing on
patches—(nearly all smoking)—some writing and
the rest reading Deadwood Dick. At one end of
the table a Christian Endeavourer endeavouring ;
at the other a cockney Jew, from the hawker’s
boat, trying to sell rotten clothes. In response to
oomplaints, direot and not chosen generally for
Sunday, the shearers’ rep. requests both apostles to
shut up or leave.
“He couldn’t be expected to take the Christian
and leave the Jew, any more than he could take
the Jew and leave the Christian. We are just
amongst ourselves in our hell.
• • • •
“ Fiddle at the end of Rouseabouts’ hut. Voice
of Jackaroo, from upper bunk with apologetic
oaths: ‘For God’s sake chuck that up ; it makes
a man think of blanky old things ! ’
“ A lost soul laughs (mine) and dreadful night
smothers us.”
HENBY LAWSON.
